Question 1

Your API is receiving more traffic than expected, causing performance issues. How would you address this problem?

Situation: traffic has grown past what the current deployment handles; p99 is climbing, error rates rising, and the team needs to decide whether to optimize, scale, or both — under pressure.

Approach: diagnose before acting. RED metrics (rate, errors, duration) per endpoint reveal the hotspots; a distributed trace of one slow request shows whether time is in DB, external calls, or compute. Fix in order of ROI:

  1. Add a CDN or edge cache for GETs that can tolerate seconds of staleness (product catalog, static config). Takes 50-90% of read traffic off origin in hours, not weeks.
  2. Horizontal scale via autoscaler (HPA on CPU/RPS) if stateless. Add instances behind the load balancer; verify session affinity isn't required.
  3. Kill N+1 queries in the hot endpoint — usually the largest single win. Eager-load via JOIN or DataLoader.
  4. Add read replicas and route reads to them; writes stay on primary.
  5. Rate-limit per-user to prevent a runaway client from consuming shared capacity; add quotas for tier differentiation.
  6. Queue async work (emails, webhooks, report generation) off the request path.
// quick wins: in-memory LRU for hot reads while CDN is being configured
import { LRUCache } from "lru-cache";
const cache = new LRUCache({ max: 10_000, ttl: 60_000 });

app.get("/products/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const cached = cache.get(req.params.id);
  if (cached) return res.set("X-Cache", "HIT").json(cached);
  const p = await db.product.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (p) cache.set(req.params.id, p);
  res.json(p);
});

Trade-offs: caching adds staleness and requires invalidation discipline; horizontal scaling multiplies cost linearly and doesn't help if DB is the bottleneck; rate limits frustrate legitimate heavy users. In the first hour, CDN + cache the hottest reads. In the first day, scale out and fix N+1s. In the first week, add replicas and shard writes if needed. Long-term, invest in observability — without it, the next traffic spike is diagnosed blind.

Question 2

You need to design an API that allows users to upload and manage their profile pictures. How would you handle file uploads, storage, and security?

Situation: users upload profile pictures (typically 1-10 MB JPEG/PNG); the system must serve them fast globally, keep storage costs reasonable, and prevent abuse (oversized files, malware, offensive content).

Approach: pre-signed S3 uploads bypass the API server for bytes; the API only orchestrates. Resize asynchronously to serve thumbnails; serve via CDN.

// 1. issue upload URL — server never sees the file bytes
app.post("/profile/avatar/init", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { contentType, size } = req.body;
  if (!["image/jpeg", "image/png", "image/webp"].includes(contentType)) {
    return res.status(400).json({ error: "Invalid type" });
  }
  if (size > 10 * 1024 * 1024) return res.status(413).json({ error: "Too large" });

  const key = `avatars/original/${req.user.sub}/${crypto.randomUUID()}.jpg`;
  const url = await getSignedUrl(s3,
    new PutObjectCommand({
      Bucket: "uploads", Key: key, ContentType: contentType,
      ContentLength: size,
    }),
    { expiresIn: 300 }
  );
  res.json({ uploadUrl: url, key });
});

// 2. after successful upload, client calls back — we validate & kick off resize
app.post("/profile/avatar/commit", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { key } = req.body;
  // verify object exists and matches user namespace
  if (!key.startsWith(`avatars/original/${req.user.sub}/`)) return res.sendStatus(403);
  const head = await s3.send(new HeadObjectCommand({ Bucket: "uploads", Key: key }));
  if (head.ContentLength > 10 * 1024 * 1024) return res.status(413).end();

  // enqueue resize + moderation job
  await queue.add("process-avatar", { userId: req.user.sub, key });
  res.status(202).json({ status: "processing" });
});

The worker verifies magic bytes with file-type, runs NSFW moderation (AWS Rekognition or Hive), resizes to 64/128/256/512 px with sharp, writes to the avatars/processed/ prefix, and updates the user record with the CDN URLs. Serve via CloudFront/Cloudflare fronting the bucket; cache max-age=31536000, immutable because each URL is unique per upload.

Trade-offs: pre-signed uploads shift the "malicious file" problem to post-upload processing, but save bandwidth and memory on the API tier; buffered upload through Node is simpler but limits file size and scales poorly. Always: private bucket + signed URLs for originals, public bucket + CDN for processed thumbnails, virus scan before serving, enforce per-user storage quota and retention (delete old avatars on replace).

Question 3

A client reports that your API occasionally returns stale data. How would you implement a caching strategy to mitigate this issue?

Situation: client sees outdated data — stale user profile, old inventory count, wrong price. Caching is usually the cause. Diagnosis: which cache layer is serving stale data, and why isn't it invalidating?

Approach: layered caching with explicit invalidation on writes and short TTLs as a safety net. Four layers to check: CDN, API gateway, in-process, DB query cache.

// write path — invalidate at every layer you own
app.patch("/users/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const user = await db.user.update({
    where: { id: req.params.id }, data: req.body,
  });

  // invalidate caches — fan-out via Redis pub/sub so all instances clear their LRU
  await Promise.all([
    redis.del(`user:${user.id}`),
    redis.publish("cache:invalidate", JSON.stringify({ type: "user", id: user.id })),
  ]);

  // purge CDN surrogate key (Fastly/Cloudflare pattern)
  fetch(`https://api.cloudflare.com/zones/${ZONE}/purge_cache`, {
    method: "POST",
    headers: { "Authorization": `Bearer ${CF_TOKEN}`, "Content-Type": "application/json" },
    body: JSON.stringify({ tags: [`user-${user.id}`] }),
  }).catch(() => {});

  res.json(user);
});

// read path — short TTL is the safety net for missed invalidations
app.get("/users/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const cached = await redis.get(`user:${req.params.id}`);
  if (cached) return res
    .set("Cache-Control", "public, max-age=60, stale-while-revalidate=300")
    .set("Cache-Tag", `user-${req.params.id}`)    // CDN key
    .json(JSON.parse(cached));

  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!user) return res.sendStatus(404);
  await redis.setex(`user:${req.params.id}`, 300, JSON.stringify(user));
  res.set("Cache-Tag", `user-${req.params.id}`).json(user);
});

// subscriber — in-process LRU clears when any instance invalidates
const sub = redis.duplicate();
sub.subscribe("cache:invalidate");
sub.on("message", (_, msg) => {
  const { type, id } = JSON.parse(msg);
  memCache.delete(`${type}:${id}`);
});

Trade-offs: strong freshness (short TTLs, aggressive invalidation) costs hit rate; eventual consistency (long TTLs, lazy invalidation) risks user-visible stale data. Middle ground: stale-while-revalidate serves slightly-stale content instantly while revalidating in background — near-zero latency with good freshness. For truly critical data (account balance, inventory at checkout), skip the cache entirely or read from the primary DB. Add a If-Modified-Since conditional fallback so clients can validate when they must.

Question 4

Your API needs to support third-party integrations for user authentication. How would you implement OAuth 2.0?

Situation: users want to sign in with Google/GitHub/Microsoft so they don't need another password; the integration must be OAuth 2.1 compliant (PKCE mandatory), store minimal data, and handle edge cases (existing account with same email, revoked tokens, deleted accounts).

Approach: use a battle-tested library (openid-client for Node) rather than rolling your own flow. Three endpoints: /auth/login starts the flow; /auth/callback handles the redirect; /auth/refresh rotates tokens.

import { Issuer, generators } from "openid-client";

const issuer = await Issuer.discover("https://accounts.google.com");
const client = new issuer.Client({
  client_id:     process.env.GOOGLE_CLIENT_ID,
  client_secret: process.env.GOOGLE_CLIENT_SECRET,
  redirect_uris: [`${APP_URL}/auth/callback`],
  response_types: ["code"],
});

app.get("/auth/login", (req, res) => {
  const codeVerifier  = generators.codeVerifier();
  const state = generators.state();
  const nonce = generators.nonce();
  req.session.oauth = { codeVerifier, state, nonce };

  res.redirect(client.authorizationUrl({
    scope: "openid email profile",
    code_challenge: generators.codeChallenge(codeVerifier),
    code_challenge_method: "S256",
    state, nonce,
  }));
});

app.get("/auth/callback", async (req, res) => {
  const { codeVerifier, state, nonce } = req.session.oauth ?? {};
  const params = client.callbackParams(req);
  const tokenSet = await client.callback(
    `${APP_URL}/auth/callback`, params,
    { code_verifier: codeVerifier, state, nonce }
  );
  const claims = tokenSet.claims();
  if (!claims.email_verified) return res.sendStatus(403);

  // upsert by (provider, sub) — never by email alone
  const user = await db.user.upsert({
    where:  { provider_providerId: { provider: "google", providerId: claims.sub } },
    create: { provider: "google", providerId: claims.sub, email: claims.email, name: claims.name },
    update: { email: claims.email, name: claims.name },
  });

  // issue OUR session token — don't expose Google's to clients
  res
    .cookie("session", issueSessionJwt(user), { httpOnly: true, secure: true, sameSite: "lax" })
    .redirect("/app");
});

Trade-offs: OAuth is more complex than email/password and adds a dependency on the IdP (Google outage = login outage for affected users). Mitigate by supporting multiple providers or maintaining a password fallback. Always identify users by (provider, sub) because emails change; sub is stable. Issue your own session/JWT after OAuth completes so you control expiry and revocation. For native mobile apps, use PKCE with the OS's system browser (ASWebAuthenticationSession on iOS, Custom Tabs on Android) — never embed WebViews (phishing risk, rejected by most IdPs).

Question 5

You need to design a RESTful API for a blogging platform. Describe the endpoints you would create and the HTTP methods you would use.

Situation: greenfield blogging API: posts, comments, authors, tags, likes. Need a consistent, discoverable REST design that handles listing, filtering, and nested relationships cleanly.

Approach: resource-oriented URLs, plural nouns, HTTP methods for semantics. Nest ownership (post → comments) but keep commonly-accessed resources flat.

// Authentication
POST   /auth/register                 → create account
POST   /auth/login                    → returns accessToken + sets refresh cookie

// Posts
GET    /posts?tag=rust&author=alice&limit=20&cursor=...    → list with filters + pagination
POST   /posts                         → create (auth required)
GET    /posts/:id                     → read one
PATCH  /posts/:id                     → partial update (owner or admin)
DELETE /posts/:id                     → remove (owner or admin)
POST   /posts/:id/publish             → action endpoint — state transition
POST   /posts/:id/likes               → idempotent; returns current like count
DELETE /posts/:id/likes               → unlike

// Comments nested under post for listing, flat for individual access
GET    /posts/:id/comments            → list comments on a post
POST   /posts/:id/comments            → add a comment
PATCH  /comments/:id                  → edit own comment
DELETE /comments/:id                  → delete own comment

// Authors
GET    /authors/:handle               → public profile
GET    /authors/:handle/posts         → author's posts
GET    /me                            → current authenticated user

// Tags
GET    /tags                          → list (auto-complete)
GET    /tags/:slug/posts              → posts for a tag

Design decisions worth explaining:

  • Filters as query params (?tag=rust), not URLs (/posts/tag/rust) — filters compose.
  • Cursor pagination (?cursor=) scales to millions of posts; offset breaks past page 500.
  • Like as a sub-resource (POST/DELETE /likes) is cleaner than a toggle verb; idempotent.
  • Action endpoint (/posts/:id/publish) when a state transition doesn't fit PATCH semantics — publishing triggers side effects (notifications, SEO ping).
  • Nesting max 2 levels to keep URLs readable.

Trade-offs: pure resource-oriented REST can be awkward for complex queries ("posts trending this week, by authors I follow, excluding hidden") — accept a dedicated /feed endpoint or GraphQL for that page. Responses should include hypermedia-lite links ({ id, title, _links: { author, comments } }) for navigation. Use RFC 7807 Problem Details for errors and OpenAPI as the spec source of truth so docs, clients, and tests stay in sync.

Question 6

A customer needs to retrieve large datasets from your API. How would you implement pagination to handle this?

Situation: client wants to iterate through 10 million orders; a naive "return everything" response would OOM both server and client. Need pagination that handles deep iteration safely.

Approach: cursor-based pagination for scale; offset only when "jump to page N" is a product requirement. Cap page size. Include a stable tie-breaker.

// cursor pagination — O(limit) cost regardless of page depth
app.get("/orders", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const limit = Math.min(100, Math.max(1, Number(req.query.limit) || 50));
  const cursor = req.query.cursor
    ? JSON.parse(Buffer.from(String(req.query.cursor), "base64url").toString())
    : null;

  const orders = await db.order.findMany({
    where: {
      customerId: req.user.sub,
      ...(cursor && {
        // (createdAt DESC, id DESC) — id breaks timestamp ties
        OR: [
          { createdAt: { lt: new Date(cursor.createdAt) } },
          { createdAt: new Date(cursor.createdAt), id: { lt: cursor.id } },
        ],
      }),
    },
    orderBy: [{ createdAt: "desc" }, { id: "desc" }],
    take: limit + 1,          // +1 to detect "has more" without COUNT
  });

  const hasMore = orders.length > limit;
  const data = orders.slice(0, limit);
  const last = data[data.length - 1];

  res.json({
    data,
    pagination: {
      nextCursor: hasMore && last
        ? Buffer.from(JSON.stringify({ createdAt: last.createdAt, id: last.id })).toString("base64url")
        : null,
      hasMore,
    },
  });
});

Index must match the sort: CREATE INDEX orders_customer_created ON orders (customer_id, created_at DESC, id DESC). Without it, page 1000 takes seconds; with it, every page is <5ms.

Trade-offs: cursor pagination doesn't allow jumping to page 500 or showing "page 3 of 200" — the client only navigates forward/back. For reporting dashboards where jumping to page N is required, offset pagination works up to a few thousand pages; beyond that, pre-computed page keys or keyset scanning. Opaque cursors (base64url-encoded JSON) hide implementation so you can change the sort key later without breaking clients. Always cap limit; otherwise a malicious client requests 1M rows. For bulk export (>10k rows), point clients to an async job endpoint that writes a signed S3 URL — pagination wasn't designed for full-table scans.

Question 7

Your API must handle sensitive user data. How would you ensure data security and compliance with regulations like GDPR?

Situation: the API handles PII (names, emails, addresses, health records); GDPR and similar regulations require encryption, access controls, audit logging, right-to-erasure, and data residency. A breach carries fines up to 4% of global revenue.

Approach: defense in depth — multiple independent controls so one failure doesn't cause a breach. Data lifecycle is: classify → minimize → encrypt → access-control → audit → delete.

  • Data classification: label every field as public / internal / confidential / sensitive PII. Drives policy.
  • Encryption: TLS 1.3 everywhere in transit; envelope encryption at rest (KMS + per-record DEK for sensitive fields); hashed/encrypted fields separate from indexed ones.
  • Access control: least-privilege IAM per service; row-level security in Postgres so a bug in code can't leak across tenants; scoped API tokens.
  • Audit logging: every access to PII logs who, when, which record, why. Logs tamper-evident (append-only, signed).
  • Data minimization: don't collect what you don't need; retention policy auto-deletes.
  • Erasure: DELETE endpoint that wipes the record in the primary + all projections (search index, cache, analytics pipeline, backups within retention window).
  • Consent & portability: explicit opt-ins per purpose; export endpoint returns all user data in a standard format.
// per-row encryption of sensitive fields
import { createCipheriv, createDecipheriv, randomBytes } from "crypto";

function encryptField(plaintext, dek) {
  const iv = randomBytes(12);
  const c = createCipheriv("aes-256-gcm", dek, iv);
  const ct = Buffer.concat([c.update(plaintext, "utf8"), c.final()]);
  return Buffer.concat([iv, c.getAuthTag(), ct]).toString("base64");
}

// DEK wrapped by KMS KEK — envelope encryption
const dek = await kms.decrypt(user.encryptedDek);

// audit on every read of sensitive data
app.get("/users/:id/ssn", requireAuth, requireRole("admin", "compliance"),
  async (req, res) => {
    await db.auditLog.create({
      data: { userId: req.user.sub, action: "read:ssn", targetId: req.params.id,
              reason: req.headers["x-reason"], ip: req.ip },
    });
    // decrypt and return
  }
);

Trade-offs: encryption adds CPU overhead and complicates search/indexing on those fields (use deterministic encryption, tokenization, or secure multi-party computation if you need to search). Audit logs generate significant storage; ship to SIEM (Splunk, Elastic, Datadog) rather than storing in the OLTP DB. Compliance is a process, not a feature — DPIAs (Data Protection Impact Assessments) for new features, annual pen-tests, incident response drills, DPO appointment if required. Data residency (EU data stays in EU) drives multi-region architecture and contract review with sub-processors.

Question 8

Your team wants to migrate from REST to GraphQL. What steps would you take to facilitate this transition?

Situation: team wants GraphQL's flexibility (clients pick fields, single request for many resources) but has 50 existing REST endpoints and live clients. Hard cutover breaks everything; need a phased migration.

Approach: run GraphQL alongside REST. New features ship as GraphQL; REST endpoints stay functional but deprecate incrementally. GraphQL layer wraps existing services initially, then services own their schema fragments.

// Phase 1 — GraphQL over existing REST via Apollo Server
import { ApolloServer } from "@apollo/server";
import { RESTDataSource } from "@apollo/datasource-rest";

class OrderAPI extends RESTDataSource {
  override baseURL = "http://orders-service/";
  getOrder(id) { return this.get(`orders/${id}`); }
  listOrders(userId) { return this.get(`orders?user=${userId}`); }
}

const typeDefs = `#graphql
  type Order { id: ID!, total: Float!, items: [Item!]! }
  type User  { id: ID!, name: String!, orders: [Order!]! }
  type Query {
    user(id: ID!): User
    order(id: ID!): Order
  }
`;

const resolvers = {
  Query: {
    user:  (_, { id }, ctx) => ctx.dataSources.user.getUser(id),
    order: (_, { id }, ctx) => ctx.dataSources.order.getOrder(id),
  },
  User: {
    orders: (user, _, ctx) => ctx.dataSources.order.listOrders(user.id),
  },
};
// GraphQL now serves the same data as REST, with field selection

Migration phases:

  1. Parallel run: GraphQL gateway stands up wrapping REST. Non-breaking.
  2. Dogfood: internal tools and new features use GraphQL. Client teams try it.
  3. New clients via GraphQL only: mobile v3, new web pages.
  4. Federation: each service owns its schema (Apollo Federation, GraphQL Mesh); resolvers live with the data.
  5. REST deprecation: announce sunset per-endpoint; monitor usage; remove after grace period.

Trade-offs: GraphQL is not strictly better than REST — it wins for aggregation-heavy clients (mobile, dashboards) and loses on caching (HTTP caching assumes unique URLs), public APIs (harder to document, rate-limit, evolve), and file uploads (there's a spec but it's clunky). Guard against query complexity attacks with depth/complexity analysis and persisted queries. N+1 resolvers are the #1 pitfall — always use DataLoader. Keep REST for webhooks, OAuth endpoints, and file uploads. Migration often ends as a hybrid, not a full replacement — and that's fine.

Question 9

How would you design an API rate limiting mechanism to prevent abuse while ensuring legitimate users can access the API?

Situation: the API is public-facing; some clients hammer it (scrapers, buggy integrations, credential-stuffing attempts) while legitimate users need headroom for bursts. Need layered limits with tier-based generosity.

Approach: token-bucket for per-user smoothing with burst tolerance; distributed via Redis for multi-instance consistency; layered checks (global, per-IP, per-user, per-endpoint); tier-based limits from the user record.

// atomic multi-layer check via Redis Lua — prevents race across instances
const LUA = `
  for i=1,#KEYS do
    local cur = redis.call('INCR', KEYS[i])
    if cur == 1 then redis.call('EXPIRE', KEYS[i], ARGV[i]) end
    if tonumber(cur) > tonumber(ARGV[i + #KEYS]) then
      return {0, i, ARGV[i + #KEYS]}    -- return which layer failed
    end
  end
  return {1, 0, 0}
`;

async function checkLimits(req) {
  const user = req.user;
  const plan = user?.plan || "free";
  const limits = { free: 60, pro: 600, enterprise: 6000 };
  const perMinUser = limits[plan];

  const keys = [
    `rl:global:${Math.floor(Date.now()/60000)}`,      // 100k/min global
    `rl:ip:${req.ip}:${Math.floor(Date.now()/60000)}`, // 200/min per IP
    `rl:user:${user?.id || req.ip}:${Math.floor(Date.now()/60000)}`,
  ];
  const ttls = [60, 60, 60];
  const caps = [100_000, 200, perMinUser];

  const [allowed, layer, limit] = await redis.eval(LUA, 3, ...keys, ...ttls, ...caps);
  return { allowed: allowed === 1, layer, limit };
}

app.use(async (req, res, next) => {
  const { allowed, layer, limit } = await checkLimits(req);
  if (!allowed) {
    res.set("RateLimit-Policy", `${limit};w=60`);
    res.set("Retry-After", "60");
    return res.status(429).json({
      error: "Rate limit exceeded",
      layer,       // help legitimate users understand which quota they hit
    });
  }
  next();
});

Authenticated login uses a stricter limiter (skipSuccessfulRequests, 5 attempts / 15 min) to slow brute force without affecting normal traffic. Expensive endpoints (search, report generation) cost more tokens than cheap ones (GET by id).

Trade-offs: per-IP limits hurt users behind corporate NAT (thousands of legitimate users share one IP) — whitelist known IPs or use per-user limits when authenticated. Distributed token-bucket in Redis is correct but adds a round-trip to every request (~1ms); for ultra-high-throughput APIs, use local buckets per instance with periodic reconciliation. Always return RateLimit-Remaining so well-behaved clients pace themselves; combine with Cloudflare or AWS WAF at the edge to block obvious abuse before it reaches origin.

Question 10

You need to implement real-time notifications in your API. How would you use WebSockets or Server-Sent Events (SSE) to achieve this?

Situation: browser UI shows order status updates as they happen (shipped, delivered, refunded) without page refresh. Need a push channel that works through proxies, survives mobile network changes, and scales to many concurrent users.

Approach: Server-Sent Events (SSE) for one-way server-to-client push is simpler than WebSockets and recovers automatically via Last-Event-Id. Redis pub/sub fans events from producers to all API instances, each holding its own SSE subscribers.

import Redis from "ioredis";
const pub = new Redis(process.env.REDIS_URL);
const sub = pub.duplicate();

// publish an event from anywhere — order service on status change
export async function publishOrderEvent(userId, event) {
  await pub.publish(`user:${userId}:events`, JSON.stringify(event));
  // also persist for replay on reconnect
  await pub.xadd(`user:${userId}:events:log`, "*",
    "data", JSON.stringify(event));
  await pub.xtrim(`user:${userId}:events:log`, "MAXLEN", "~", 1000);
}

// SSE endpoint — each connection gets a Redis subscription
app.get("/events", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  res.set({
    "Content-Type":      "text/event-stream",
    "Cache-Control":     "no-store",
    "Connection":        "keep-alive",
    "X-Accel-Buffering": "no",        // disable Nginx buffering
  });
  res.flushHeaders();

  // replay missed events since Last-Event-Id
  const lastId = req.headers["last-event-id"];
  if (lastId) {
    const missed = await sub.xrange(`user:${req.user.sub}:events:log`, lastId, "+");
    for (const [id, [_, payload]] of missed) {
      res.write(`id: ${id}\ndata: ${payload}\n\n`);
    }
  }

  // subscribe and forward
  const channel = `user:${req.user.sub}:events`;
  const listener = (ch, msg) => {
    if (ch === channel) res.write(`event: order\ndata: ${msg}\n\n`);
  };
  sub.subscribe(channel);
  sub.on("message", listener);

  // heartbeat every 20s keeps proxies from timing out
  const heart = setInterval(() => res.write(":heartbeat\n\n"), 20_000);

  req.on("close", () => {
    clearInterval(heart);
    sub.unsubscribe(channel);
    sub.off("message", listener);
    res.end();
  });
});

Trade-offs: SSE is one-way; if the client needs to send data back, use regular POSTs or switch to WebSocket. SSE maxes at 6 concurrent connections per browser origin (HTTP/1.1) — fine with HTTP/2 or one multiplexed channel. For cross-origin, configure CORS carefully. Sticky routing isn't required because Redis pub/sub fans out across instances. For mobile apps, background delivery needs APNs/FCM — SSE only works in foreground. Scale: each instance holds thousands of SSE connections in RAM; scale out horizontally as connection count grows; use a connection-limit middleware to prevent one user opening 100 tabs from monopolizing resources.

Question 11

A client application requires push notifications for certain events. How would you implement this in your API?

Situation: mobile app should alert users on events (order shipped, new message, promotional offer) even when app is backgrounded. Must work on iOS and Android, handle token rotation, respect user preferences, and scale to millions of devices.

Approach: use platform push services (APNs for iOS, FCM for Android, WebPush for web). Our API stores device tokens, dispatches via platform APIs, and manages preferences. Use a reliable vendor (Firebase Cloud Messaging, OneSignal, AWS SNS Mobile) for consolidated handling.

// 1. Register device token on login / app launch
app.post("/devices", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { token, platform, locale } = req.body;   // platform: ios|android|web
  // upsert — same device re-registering is normal
  await db.device.upsert({
    where:  { token },
    create: { token, userId: req.user.sub, platform, locale, active: true },
    update: { userId: req.user.sub, locale, active: true, lastSeen: new Date() },
  });
  res.sendStatus(204);
});

// 2. Send notification via FCM (single SDK for iOS + Android)
import admin from "firebase-admin";
admin.initializeApp({ credential: admin.credential.cert(SA_KEY) });

async function sendPush(userId, { title, body, data, category }) {
  // respect user preferences
  const prefs = await db.notificationPref.findUnique({ where: { userId } });
  if (!prefs?.categories.includes(category)) return;

  const devices = await db.device.findMany({ where: { userId, active: true } });
  if (!devices.length) return;

  const result = await admin.messaging().sendEachForMulticast({
    tokens: devices.map((d) => d.token),
    notification: { title, body },
    data,                                          // silent payload for app
    apns:    { payload: { aps: { sound: "default", badge: 1 } } },
    android: { priority: "high", notification: { channelId: category } },
  });

  // clean up invalid tokens
  const stale = result.responses
    .map((r, i) => !r.success && r.error?.code === "messaging/registration-token-not-registered" ? devices[i].token : null)
    .filter(Boolean);
  if (stale.length) {
    await db.device.updateMany({ where: { token: { in: stale } }, data: { active: false } });
  }
}

// 3. Fire from event handler (queue for reliability)
orderEvents.on("shipped", (order) => {
  pushQueue.add("send", {
    userId: order.customerId,
    title: "Your order shipped!",
    body:  `#${order.number} — tracking: ${order.tracking}`,
    data:  { orderId: order.id, type: "shipment" },
    category: "order_updates",
  });
});

Trade-offs: push delivery is best-effort — APNs/FCM don't guarantee delivery (user's phone off, throttling, network). For critical notifications, also send email/in-app. Token rotation is constant (app reinstall, backup restore) — handle InvalidRegistration by deactivating the token. Silent/data-only payloads let the app update state without a user-visible banner. Respect iOS's notification categories so users can disable specific types. Rate-limit per user — spammy push kills engagement faster than it drives it. For scale beyond ~1M/hour, use topic messaging (one FCM topic subscription per user segment) instead of individual sends.

Question 12

Your API needs to be highly available and fault-tolerant. What architectural decisions would you make to achieve this?

Situation: business-critical API; target is 99.95% availability (4 hours downtime/year max). Failures must be invisible to users. Single points of failure (DB, cache, region, deploy) each need mitigation.

Approach: redundancy at every layer, automated failover, and graceful degradation when components fail.

  • Multi-AZ deployment: API instances in at least 3 availability zones behind a regional load balancer; one AZ down = 33% capacity loss, not outage.
  • Multi-region option: active-active for read-heavy; active-passive for simpler DR.
  • Stateless API: no in-memory session; any instance handles any request. Enables rolling deployments + autoscaling.
  • DB replication: primary with synchronous standby in another AZ; async read replicas; automatic failover via RDS Multi-AZ or PostgreSQL + Patroni.
  • Circuit breakers around every external call: opossum pattern opens after N failures, returns cached/fallback response, retries periodically.
  • Bulkheads: separate thread pools/queues per dependency so one slow service doesn't exhaust all connections.
  • Caching as a fallback: if the DB is down, serve stale cached data rather than error.
  • Retries with exponential backoff + jitter for transient failures; never retry 4xx.
  • Health checks: shallow (is the process alive?) and deep (can I query the DB? hit Redis?). Load balancer uses shallow; deep drives alerts.
  • Zero-downtime deployments: rolling with readiness probes, blue/green, or canary. Never all-at-once restart.
// Circuit breaker around a flaky downstream service
import CircuitBreaker from "opossum";

const breaker = new CircuitBreaker(fetchPricing, {
  timeout: 3000,                 // fail fast
  errorThresholdPercentage: 50,
  resetTimeout: 10_000,          // try again after 10s open
  volumeThreshold: 10,
});

breaker.fallback(() => ({ price: null, stale: true }));
breaker.on("open",     () => log.warn("pricing circuit OPEN"));
breaker.on("halfOpen", () => log.info("pricing circuit testing recovery"));

const pricing = await breaker.fire(productId);

Trade-offs: each nine of availability costs ~10x more than the previous. 99.9% is cheap (single region, replicas, autoscaling); 99.99% requires multi-region and serious operational maturity; 99.999% is for telcos and financial exchanges. Chaos engineering (Gremlin, Chaos Monkey) regularly kills components in production-like environments to verify failover actually works — untested DR plans usually don't. Observability is non-negotiable: you can't recover fast from what you can't see. Define SLOs (e.g., "99% of checkouts succeed in <500ms"), track error budgets, and invest in reliability when budget burns — not in features.

Question 13

How would you handle versioning in your API to ensure backward compatibility with existing clients?

Situation: the API has external clients (mobile apps, third-party integrations). Shipping changes without breaking them requires a versioning strategy chosen before the first breaking change, not after.

Approach: additive changes ship freely; breaking changes go in a new version. Strategy choice (URL vs header) affects routing, caching, and client ergonomics.

StrategyExampleProsCons
URL path/v2/usersVisible, easy routingURLs are "resource identifiers" — purists dislike
Accept headerAccept: application/vnd.api.v2+jsonSame URL for same resourceHarder to debug, cache
Custom headerAPI-Version: 2025-03-15Date-based works well for SaaSNon-standard
Query param?v=2Trivial to switchEasy to forget; cache-busts

For most APIs, URL path versioning is the pragmatic choice — visible in logs, easy to route, simple for clients. Date-based headers (Stripe's model) give fine-grained control: API-Version: 2025-03-15 means "behave as of this date." Clients pin a date and get stability until they opt in.

// URL versioning with a shared core
import v1Router from "./routes/v1.js";
import v2Router from "./routes/v2.js";

app.use("/v1", v1Router);
app.use("/v2", v2Router);

// v2 mostly re-exports v1 handlers and overrides what changed
// src/routes/v2.js
import { Router } from "express";
import v1 from "./v1.js";
import { listUsersV2 } from "../handlers/users.js";

const router = Router();
router.use(v1);                              // inherit everything from v1
router.get("/users", listUsersV2);            // override the one change
export default router;

Non-breaking changes (always safe): adding optional fields, adding endpoints, adding enum values (if clients are tolerant), loosening validation. Breaking changes (require version bump): removing fields, renaming, changing types, adding required fields, changing auth, tightening validation.

Trade-offs: too many concurrent versions multiply maintenance and confuse docs; one active version + one deprecated-but-supported version is typical. Announce deprecations early (ideally 6-12 months); send Sunset: <date> and Deprecation: true headers on deprecated endpoints; track per-client usage to know when it's safe to remove. For internal/private APIs, ship breaking changes freely and coordinate with consumer teams — versioning machinery adds overhead that's wasted when all clients are in one repo. oasdiff in CI catches accidental breaking changes before they ship.

Question 14

Describe how you would implement an API gateway to manage traffic and enhance security for your microservices architecture.

Situation: microservices architecture with 20+ services; clients shouldn't know about them individually. The gateway centralizes cross-cutting concerns (auth, rate limiting, routing, observability) and presents one API surface.

Approach: Kong, Apigee, AWS API Gateway, or a self-hosted gateway (Traefik, Envoy) fronts all services. Each incoming request passes through the gateway's middleware chain before reaching a service.

// Kong declarative config — YAML describes routes + plugins
services:
  - name: orders-service
    url:  http://orders:8080
    routes:
      - paths: ["/v1/orders"]
        strip_path: false
    plugins:
      - name: jwt            # verify JWT signature + expiry
      - name: rate-limiting
        config:
          minute: 600
          policy: redis
          redis_host: redis
      - name: request-size-limiting
        config: { allowed_payload_size: 1 }
      - name: cors
      - name: prometheus     # metrics scraping

  - name: inventory-service
    url:  http://inventory:8080
    routes:
      - paths: ["/v1/inventory"]
    plugins:
      - name: jwt
      - name: rate-limiting
        config: { minute: 1200 }

Functions the gateway centralizes:

  • Authentication: verify JWT or API key once; pass caller identity via internal header (X-User-Id).
  • Rate limiting & quotas: per-API-key, per-plan, global.
  • Request/response transformation: strip sensitive headers, add internal ones, rewrite paths.
  • Routing: path-based, header-based, or weighted (for canary deploys).
  • TLS termination: one place to manage certificates.
  • CORS: one policy for the whole API surface.
  • Observability: uniform access logs, metrics (RED), and traces (OpenTelemetry).
  • Request validation: reject malformed requests at the edge before they hit services.
  • Caching: HTTP cache for GETs with proper Vary handling.
  • WAF: OWASP rule set, bot protection, IP allow/deny.

Trade-offs: the gateway becomes a single point of failure and a performance bottleneck — run it HA (at least 2 instances across AZs) and benchmark throughput before relying on it. Adding a gateway adds ~1-5ms per request; for latency-sensitive internal traffic, skip it and have services talk directly with mTLS. Don't put business logic in the gateway — the temptation is strong but it becomes a deployment bottleneck; keep it to cross-cutting infrastructure concerns. BFF pattern complements: one gateway per client type (web, mobile, public) if needs diverge significantly. Service mesh (Istio, Linkerd) handles east-west (service-to-service) while the gateway handles north-south (client-to-service) — they're complementary, not alternatives.

Question 15

You need to provide detailed error messages to API consumers. How would you standardize error responses across your API?

Situation: API has grown; error responses are inconsistent — some return {"error": "..."}, others plain strings, others HTML. Clients can't handle errors reliably. Need one shape, applied everywhere.

Approach: RFC 7807 Problem Details. One format, generated by central error middleware so no handler decides error shape independently.

// central error classes
class AppError extends Error {
  constructor({ status, type, title, detail, code, errors }) {
    super(title); Object.assign(this, { status, type, title, detail, code, errors });
  }
}
class ValidationError extends AppError {
  constructor(issues) {
    super({
      status: 422,
      type:   "https://api.example.com/problems/validation-error",
      title:  "Validation failed",
      code:   "validation_failed",
      errors: issues,
    });
  }
}
class NotFoundError extends AppError {
  constructor(resource, id) {
    super({
      status: 404,
      type:   "https://api.example.com/problems/not-found",
      title:  `${resource} not found`,
      code:   "not_found",
      detail: `No ${resource} with id ${id}`,
    });
  }
}

// central middleware — wraps anything that bubbles up
app.use((err, req, res, next) => {
  const status = err.status || 500;
  const body = {
    type:     err.type   || "about:blank",
    title:    err.title  || (status >= 500 ? "Internal error" : err.message),
    status,
    detail:   err.detail,
    code:     err.code,
    errors:   err.errors,
    instance: req.originalUrl,
    traceId:  req.id,          // correlate to logs
    timestamp: new Date().toISOString(),
  };
  // strip undefineds
  for (const k of Object.keys(body)) if (body[k] === undefined) delete body[k];

  // log server errors; don't leak internals to client
  if (status >= 500) {
    log.error({ err, reqId: req.id, path: req.path }, "server error");
  }

  res.status(status)
     .type("application/problem+json")
     .json(body);
});

// usage — throw typed errors; handlers don't format responses
app.get("/users/:id", async (req, res, next) => {
  const u = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!u) throw new NotFoundError("User", req.params.id);
  res.json(u);
});

Trade-offs: RFC 7807 is verbose but unambiguous and tool-friendly; smaller APIs sometimes use a slimmer shape ({ code, message, errors? }). Whatever shape you pick, enforce it with lint rules on OpenAPI specs and catch inconsistencies before they merge. Include a traceId so users who report "request X failed" give you a key to find the exact log + trace. Never leak stack traces, SQL errors, or file paths in production — sanitize in the middleware. For security-sensitive errors (401, 403, 404), keep messages generic; detailed errors help attackers enumerate. Document error codes in the OpenAPI spec so clients can switch on stable machine codes rather than parsing messages.

Question 16

A client application needs to perform multiple operations in a single API call. How would you implement this?

Situation: mobile client on slow network needs to "create order + add shipping address + apply promo + charge card" in one shot; sending 4 sequential requests costs 4x latency and leaves partial state if one fails midway.

Approach: three common patterns — batch endpoint, transactional composite, GraphQL. Pick based on atomicity needs and client diversity.

// Pattern 1 — domain-specific composite endpoint (most common, cleanest)
app.post("/checkout", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { cart, shipping, payment, promoCode } = req.body;

  // single DB transaction — all-or-nothing
  const result = await db.$transaction(async (tx) => {
    const address = await tx.address.create({ data: { ...shipping, userId: req.user.sub } });
    const promo   = promoCode ? await tx.promo.findUnique({ where: { code: promoCode } }) : null;
    const order   = await tx.order.create({
      data: {
        userId: req.user.sub, addressId: address.id,
        promoId: promo?.id,
        items: { create: cart.items },
      },
      include: { items: true },
    });
    const charge = await paymentService.charge(payment, order.total);
    await tx.payment.create({ data: { orderId: order.id, chargeId: charge.id } });
    return order;
  });

  res.status(201).json(result);
});

// Pattern 2 — generic batch (OData $batch / JSON:API / Google batch)
app.post("/batch", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { operations } = req.body;   // [{ id, method, path, body }, ...]
  const results = [];
  for (const op of operations) {
    try {
      // dispatch through the router internally — not over HTTP
      const r = await dispatch(op, req.user);
      results.push({ id: op.id, status: r.status, body: r.body });
    } catch (err) {
      results.push({ id: op.id, status: err.status || 500, error: err.message });
      if (op.atomic) break;    // stop on first failure if atomic mode
    }
  }
  res.json({ results });
});

Trade-offs: Domain composites (/checkout) are the most common answer: handler knows the business rules, runs a DB transaction, returns a coherent response. Generic batch endpoints are reusable but hard to do well — each op has its own auth/validation, atomic-vs-independent mode needs to be clear, and error reporting gets complicated. GraphQL mutations can compose multiple side effects in one request, though atomicity depends on the resolver implementation. For long-running multi-step workflows (order → payment → fulfillment → shipping), consider a workflow engine (Temporal, Trigger.dev, Inngest) which survives partial failures, retries specific steps, and exposes progress. Always make composites idempotent via an Idempotency-Key header so a retry after network flake doesn't charge twice.

Question 17

How would you design your API to handle long-running tasks without causing timeout issues for clients?

Situation: report generation takes 2-10 minutes; video transcoding 30+ minutes; data import hours. Blocking the HTTP request is impossible (proxies timeout at 60-300s) and bad UX. Need an async job pattern.

Approach: return 202 Accepted immediately with a job URL the client polls (or subscribes to via SSE/webhook). Queue the work; process in background; expose status.

// Queue (BullMQ on Redis) separates accept-work from do-work
import { Queue, Worker } from "bullmq";
const reportsQueue = new Queue("reports", { connection: redis });

// 1. accept work — fast
app.post("/reports", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const job = await db.job.create({
    data: {
      type:   "report",
      userId: req.user.sub,
      params: req.body,
      status: "queued",
    },
  });

  await reportsQueue.add("generate", { jobId: job.id },
    { attempts: 3, backoff: { type: "exponential", delay: 30_000 } });

  res.status(202)
     .location(`/jobs/${job.id}`)
     .json({ jobId: job.id, status: "queued" });
});

// 2. poll status
app.get("/jobs/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const job = await db.job.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!job || job.userId !== req.user.sub) return res.sendStatus(404);
  res.json({
    id: job.id,
    status: job.status,            // queued | running | succeeded | failed
    progress: job.progress,        // 0..1
    result: job.status === "succeeded" ? { url: job.resultUrl } : null,
    error:  job.status === "failed" ? job.error : null,
  });
});

// 3. worker — separate process, same DB
new Worker("reports", async (job) => {
  await db.job.update({ where: { id: job.data.jobId }, data: { status: "running" } });
  try {
    const url = await generateReport(job.data.jobId, (pct) =>
      db.job.update({ where: { id: job.data.jobId }, data: { progress: pct } })
    );
    await db.job.update({
      where: { id: job.data.jobId },
      data:  { status: "succeeded", progress: 1, resultUrl: url },
    });
  } catch (err) {
    await db.job.update({
      where: { id: job.data.jobId },
      data:  { status: "failed", error: err.message },
    });
    throw err;     // let BullMQ retry per the attempts config
  }
}, { connection: redis });

Trade-offs: polling is simple and firewall-friendly but wastes requests when jobs are slow — supplement with webhooks (client provides callback URL) or SSE (GET /jobs/:id/stream) for long jobs. Use idempotency keys so re-submitting the same job after a timeout doesn't queue duplicate work. Dead-letter queue for jobs that fail all retries so they can be inspected; alert on DLQ growth. For jobs that cross service boundaries (generate report → email to user → notify in app), orchestrate with a workflow engine (Temporal) rather than chaining queues — failures in step 3 shouldn't require rerunning step 1. Always persist progress so clients show accurate progress bars, and trace the whole job through OpenTelemetry so slow jobs can be diagnosed.

Question 18

Your API needs to support multiple languages and locales. How would you implement internationalization (i18n)?

Situation: API must serve content in multiple languages (English, Spanish, Japanese, Arabic) with proper pluralization, currency formatting, date formatting, and right-to-left support. Translations come from a TMS (Phrase, Lokalise, Crowdin).

Approach: client sends preferred locale via Accept-Language or an explicit query param; server resolves strings, formats numbers/dates per locale, and returns Content-Language to confirm what was served.

import i18next from "i18next";
import Backend from "i18next-fs-backend";

await i18next.use(Backend).init({
  fallbackLng: "en",
  supportedLngs: ["en", "es", "ja", "ar", "fr", "de"],
  ns: ["common", "errors", "products"],
  backend: { loadPath: "./locales/{{lng}}/{{ns}}.json" },
});

// middleware — resolve locale per request
export function i18nMiddleware(req, res, next) {
  // explicit param > authenticated user pref > Accept-Language > default
  const explicit = String(req.query.lang || "").trim();
  const userPref = req.user?.locale;
  const accept = req.headers["accept-language"]
    ?.split(",")
    .map((s) => s.split(";")[0].trim())
    .find((l) => i18next.options.supportedLngs.includes(l));

  const locale = explicit || userPref || accept || "en";
  req.t = i18next.getFixedT(locale);
  req.locale = locale;
  res.set("Content-Language", locale);
  res.set("Vary", "Accept-Language");         // cache key awareness
  next();
}

app.use(i18nMiddleware);

// usage
app.get("/products/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const product = await db.product.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!product) return res.status(404).json({ error: req.t("errors:product_not_found") });

  res.json({
    id:    product.id,
    name:  product.translations[req.locale]?.name || product.translations.en.name,
    price: new Intl.NumberFormat(req.locale, {
      style: "currency", currency: product.currency,
    }).format(product.price),
    releaseDate: new Intl.DateTimeFormat(req.locale, { dateStyle: "long" })
      .format(product.releaseDate),
  });
});

Data model for translated content: store translations as a JSON column ({ en: {...}, es: {...} }) for simple cases, or a separate translations table keyed by (entity_id, locale) for many-to-many. Always have a fallback locale (usually en) so missing translations don't 500.

Trade-offs: Accept-Language is standards-compliant but a pain on CDNs (cache keys explode); an explicit ?lang= param simplifies caching. Machine translation (DeepL, Google Translate API) fills gaps for less-critical content but mishandles industry jargon and context; translate critical UX strings with humans. Pluralization and interpolation need ICU MessageFormat for languages with complex plural rules (Russian, Arabic, Japanese). RTL (Arabic, Hebrew) affects UI layout, not API shape — ship locale so clients render correctly. Timezones are orthogonal to i18n: prefer returning UTC ISO dates and letting clients format in their local zone.

Question 19

Describe how you would use API analytics to monitor usage patterns and identify potential issues.

Situation: product team asks "which endpoints are most used? which customers use which features? what breaks integrations?" Engineering needs to catch performance regressions and error spikes. One analytics pipeline serves both.

Approach: log every request with high-cardinality context (user, endpoint, status, duration, client SDK version); ship to an analytics store; build dashboards per audience.

// per-request analytics event — emit to a queue; worker batches to warehouse
import { AsyncLocalStorage } from "node:async_hooks";
const als = new AsyncLocalStorage();

app.use((req, res, next) => {
  const start = process.hrtime.bigint();
  const ctx = {
    reqId:     req.id,
    userId:    null,
    apiKeyId:  null,
    clientSdk: req.headers["user-agent"]?.slice(0, 100),
  };

  res.on("finish", () => {
    const durMs = Number(process.hrtime.bigint() - start) / 1e6;
    const event = {
      ts:          new Date().toISOString(),
      reqId:       req.id,
      userId:      req.user?.sub,
      apiKeyId:    req.apiKey?.id,
      planTier:    req.user?.plan,
      method:      req.method,
      path:        req.route?.path ?? req.path,   // pattern, not raw path
      status:      res.statusCode,
      durMs:       Math.round(durMs),
      bytesOut:    Number(res.getHeader("content-length")) || 0,
      clientSdk:   ctx.clientSdk,
      country:     req.headers["cf-ipcountry"],
      errorCode:   res.locals.errorCode,
    };
    analyticsQueue.publish(event);
  });

  als.run(ctx, next);
});

Data destinations by audience:

AudienceQuestionsStore
Engineeringp99 latency by endpoint; error rate spikesPrometheus, Datadog, Grafana
ProductFeature adoption; retention; churn signalsAmplitude, Mixpanel, PostHog
Customer SuccessPer-account usage trends; SDK versionsData warehouse (Snowflake, BigQuery)
Finance / BillingMeter usage for billing; tier utilizationOLTP or dedicated metering store

Use cases unlocked: identify deprecated endpoints with zero usage (safe to delete); find customers on old SDK versions (target for migration help); detect integration breakage (one customer's error rate spikes); correlate slow endpoints with specific query params; track quota exhaustion patterns.

Trade-offs: high-cardinality logs get expensive fast — sample (10% of successful requests, 100% of errors) or aggregate at write time. PII in analytics is a compliance risk; hash user IDs, redact sensitive headers, separate analytics data from production DB. Use URL patterns (/users/:id), not raw paths, so every /users/123 and /users/456 aggregates correctly — Express exposes this via req.route.path. Tie analytics events to traces via reqId so a slow endpoint can be debugged from metrics → trace → log in seconds.

Question 20

A new feature requires changes to your API's data model. How would you manage this change to minimize disruption for existing clients?

Situation: need to split Address field on User into separate street, city, country fields. Live clients depend on the current shape. Big-bang rename will break everything.

Approach: expand-and-contract pattern. Add new fields alongside old; populate both; migrate clients; retire old.

// Phase 1 — EXPAND: add new columns; write both; read both in API
ALTER TABLE users
  ADD COLUMN street  TEXT,
  ADD COLUMN city    TEXT,
  ADD COLUMN country TEXT;

// one-time backfill
UPDATE users SET
  street  = split_part(address, ',', 1),
  city    = split_part(address, ',', 2),
  country = split_part(address, ',', 3)
WHERE street IS NULL;

// API — accept both shapes in writes; return both in reads
app.patch("/users/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const body = req.body;

  // if client sends old shape, parse into new
  if (body.address && !body.street) {
    const [street, city, country] = body.address.split(",").map((s) => s.trim());
    Object.assign(body, { street, city, country });
  }
  // if client sends new shape, keep old in sync for old readers
  if (body.street) {
    body.address = [body.street, body.city, body.country].filter(Boolean).join(", ");
  }

  const user = await db.user.update({ where: { id: req.params.id }, data: body });
  res.json({
    ...user,
    address: `${user.street}, ${user.city}, ${user.country}`,    // old shape for old clients
  });
});

Phase 2 — Migrate clients: release new docs showing the new fields; deprecate address with a Deprecation: true header and a Sunset: 2026-10-01 header; announce in release notes; open support tickets proactively with heavy users. Track address usage per client: how many requests still send/read it?

Phase 3 — Contract: once address usage is near zero (log analysis over several weeks), stop returning it in responses; ultimately remove the column. New API version (/v2) can drop it entirely while /v1 keeps the bridge.

Trade-offs: expand-and-contract doubles writes during the transition and adds response bloat, but is the only way to migrate shapes without breakage. Shadow reads help validate correctness: when a request comes for address, also compute from new fields and log discrepancies. For large-scale backfills, batch with a job queue to avoid locking the table. The hidden work is often in downstream consumers: search indexes, ETL jobs, webhook payloads, export APIs — they all need updating in parallel. Feature flag the new shape so you can gate rollout and roll back quickly if analytics reveal unexpected client breakage.

Question 21

How would you implement a webhook system to allow third-party services to receive event notifications from your API?

Situation: third-party apps want to react to events in our system (new user signs up, order ships, subscription expires) without polling. Webhooks push events to registered URLs; must be reliable, verifiable, and idempotent.

Approach: subscribers register a URL + secret; we sign payloads; deliver via a queue with retries; log every attempt for debugging.

// 1. Subscriber registers a webhook
app.post("/webhooks", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { url, events } = req.body;     // events: ["user.created", "order.shipped"]
  const secret = crypto.randomBytes(32).toString("base64url");
  const hook = await db.webhook.create({
    data: { url, events, secret, userId: req.user.sub },
  });
  res.status(201).json({ id: hook.id, secret });    // show secret ONCE
});

// 2. Event dispatcher — call whenever a domain event fires
export async function dispatchEvent(event) {
  const hooks = await db.webhook.findMany({
    where: { userId: event.userId, active: true, events: { has: event.type } },
  });
  for (const hook of hooks) {
    await webhookQueue.add("deliver", { hookId: hook.id, event },
      { attempts: 8, backoff: { type: "exponential", delay: 30_000 } });
  }
}

// 3. Worker signs + sends with retry
import crypto from "node:crypto";
new Worker("webhooks", async (job) => {
  const { hookId, event } = job.data;
  const hook = await db.webhook.findUnique({ where: { id: hookId } });
  if (!hook || !hook.active) return;

  const timestamp = Math.floor(Date.now() / 1000);
  const body = JSON.stringify(event);
  const sig = crypto.createHmac("sha256", hook.secret)
    .update(`${timestamp}.${body}`).digest("hex");

  const res = await fetch(hook.url, {
    method: "POST",
    headers: {
      "Content-Type":        "application/json",
      "X-Webhook-Id":        event.id,       // idempotency key for receiver
      "X-Webhook-Timestamp": String(timestamp),
      "X-Webhook-Signature": `sha256=${sig}`,
      "User-Agent":          "example-webhooks/1.0",
    },
    body,
    signal: AbortSignal.timeout(10_000),
  });

  await db.webhookDelivery.create({
    data: {
      hookId, eventId: event.id, attempt: job.attemptsMade + 1,
      status: res.status, durationMs: Date.now() - job.timestamp,
    },
  });

  if (res.status >= 500 || res.status === 429) throw new Error(`retry ${res.status}`);
  if (res.status >= 400) {
    // 4xx is receiver's fault — don't retry indefinitely; auto-disable after repeated failures
    await autoDisableAfterFailures(hookId);
  }
});

Receiver's perspective (documented for integrators): verify the signature with the shared secret; compare the timestamp (reject if >5 min old) to prevent replay; use X-Webhook-Id as an idempotency key because retries happen.

Trade-offs: webhooks are at-least-once, not exactly-once — receivers must be idempotent. Retries with exponential backoff (30s → 1m → 4m → 16m → ...) eventually give up after ~12 hours; offer a replay endpoint so receivers can fetch missed events. Signed timestamps prevent replay; HMAC-SHA256 over (timestamp.body) is the standard (Stripe's model). Commercial services (Hookdeck, Svix, Inngest) handle delivery, retries, observability, and a developer dashboard — worth adopting over building from scratch. Provide a "send test event" feature in your dashboard so integrators can verify signatures work end-to-end.

Question 22

Your API must integrate with a legacy system that uses XML. How would you handle data format conversions?

Situation: integrating with a legacy ERP that accepts only XML/SOAP; our internal API is JSON. Need a bridge that shields our code from the legacy format while letting us drive it.

Approach: adapter/facade layer owns the XML parsing/serialization. Internal code sees only clean JSON; only the adapter touches XML. Caches idempotent reads; batches writes where possible.

import { XMLParser, XMLBuilder } from "fast-xml-parser";
import soap from "soap";

const parser  = new XMLParser({ ignoreAttributes: false, attributeNamePrefix: "@" });
const builder = new XMLBuilder({ ignoreAttributes: false, attributeNamePrefix: "@" });

// 1. SOAP client (WSDL defines operations & types)
const wsdlUrl = "https://legacy-erp/services/?wsdl";
const soapClient = await soap.createClientAsync(wsdlUrl, {
  wsdl_headers: { Authorization: `Basic ${process.env.ERP_BASIC}` },
});

// 2. Adapter — translates our domain to SOAP args and back
export class ErpAdapter {
  async getCustomer(id) {
    const [result] = await soapClient.GetCustomerAsync({ customerId: id });
    // translate legacy shape → our domain
    return {
      id:     result.Customer["@id"],
      name:   result.Customer.FullName,
      email:  result.Customer.EmailAddress,
      status: result.Customer.Status === "A" ? "active" : "inactive",
    };
  }

  async createOrder(order) {
    // translate our domain → legacy shape
    const [result] = await soapClient.CreateOrderAsync({
      Order: {
        "@customerId": order.customerId,
        Items: { Item: order.items.map((i) => ({
          "@sku": i.sku, Qty: i.qty, Price: i.price,
        })) },
        Total: order.total,
      },
    });
    return { erpOrderId: result.orderId };
  }
}

// 3. REST endpoint thin — just calls the adapter
app.post("/orders", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const order = await db.order.create({ data: req.body });
  try {
    const { erpOrderId } = await erp.createOrder(order);
    await db.order.update({ where: { id: order.id }, data: { erpOrderId } });
    res.status(201).json(order);
  } catch (err) {
    // legacy systems fail mysteriously — don't expose SOAP faults to clients
    log.error({ err, orderId: order.id }, "ERP sync failed");
    await errorQueue.add("retry-erp-sync", { orderId: order.id });
    res.status(202).json({ ...order, erpSync: "pending" });
  }
});

Trade-offs: the adapter adds a layer but quarantines legacy concerns. Never let XML leak into your domain — if you find SOAP namespaces or @attributes in your business logic, the adapter isn't doing its job. Legacy systems are often slow and fragile: use circuit breakers, cache reads aggressively (customer data rarely changes), and queue writes with retries. Test contract with the legacy system via recorded fixtures (nock, VCR) since real ERP uptime in dev is unreliable. For two-way sync (ERP → us), poll or subscribe to change feeds; transform via the same adapter on the way in. If the SOAP WSDL is large, use code generation (wsdl-tsclient) to get typed client stubs. For REST-over-XML (non-SOAP), you may need xmlbuilder2 or xml2js; the pattern is the same — one module that translates.

Question 23

Describe how you would implement a secure token-based authentication system for your API.

Situation: need stateless auth for a public API used by browsers and mobile apps. Must support logout (revocation), short sessions for security, and long sessions for UX. Tokens shouldn't survive a breach.

Approach: access token + refresh token pattern. Short-lived access JWT for API calls; long-lived refresh token in an HttpOnly cookie; rotation on every refresh.

import jwt from "jsonwebtoken";
import crypto from "node:crypto";

const ACCESS_TTL  = 15 * 60;             // 15 min
const REFRESH_TTL = 30 * 24 * 60 * 60;   // 30 days

function issueAccessToken(user) {
  return jwt.sign(
    { sub: user.id, role: user.role, tenantId: user.tenantId },
    process.env.JWT_SECRET,
    { algorithm: "HS256", expiresIn: ACCESS_TTL,
      issuer: "api.example.com", audience: "api",
      jwtid: crypto.randomUUID() }
  );
}

async function issueRefreshToken(userId, familyId = crypto.randomUUID()) {
  const raw  = crypto.randomBytes(32).toString("base64url");
  const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(raw).digest("hex");
  await db.refreshToken.create({
    data: {
      tokenHash: hash, userId, familyId,
      expiresAt: new Date(Date.now() + REFRESH_TTL * 1000),
    },
  });
  return { raw, familyId };
}

// LOGIN — issue both, set refresh cookie
app.post("/auth/login", async (req, res) => {
  const user = await verifyCredentials(req.body);
  if (!user) return res.sendStatus(401);
  const access = issueAccessToken(user);
  const { raw: refresh } = await issueRefreshToken(user.id);
  res.cookie("rt", refresh, {
    httpOnly: true, secure: true, sameSite: "lax",
    path: "/auth", maxAge: REFRESH_TTL * 1000,
  }).json({ accessToken: access, expiresIn: ACCESS_TTL });
});

// REFRESH — rotate; detect reuse = breach
app.post("/auth/refresh", async (req, res) => {
  const raw = req.cookies.rt;
  if (!raw) return res.sendStatus(401);
  const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(raw).digest("hex");
  const record = await db.refreshToken.findUnique({ where: { tokenHash: hash } });

  if (!record || record.expiresAt < new Date()) return res.sendStatus(401);
  if (record.usedAt) {
    // REUSE — the old token was already exchanged. Someone's replaying it.
    await db.refreshToken.updateMany({
      where: { familyId: record.familyId }, data: { revokedAt: new Date() },
    });
    return res.status(401).json({ error: "Token reuse detected; please log in again" });
  }

  await db.refreshToken.update({ where: { id: record.id }, data: { usedAt: new Date() } });

  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: record.userId } });
  const access = issueAccessToken(user);
  const { raw: newRefresh } = await issueRefreshToken(user.id, record.familyId);
  res.cookie("rt", newRefresh, { httpOnly: true, secure: true, sameSite: "lax", path: "/auth" })
     .json({ accessToken: access, expiresIn: ACCESS_TTL });
});

// LOGOUT — revoke whole family
app.post("/auth/logout", async (req, res) => {
  const raw = req.cookies.rt;
  if (raw) {
    const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(raw).digest("hex");
    const record = await db.refreshToken.findUnique({ where: { tokenHash: hash } });
    if (record) {
      await db.refreshToken.updateMany({
        where: { familyId: record.familyId }, data: { revokedAt: new Date() },
      });
    }
  }
  res.clearCookie("rt", { path: "/auth" }).sendStatus(204);
});

Trade-offs: JWTs are stateless (fast) but hard to revoke mid-flight — 15-min TTL keeps the window of exposure small. Refresh rotation with reuse detection detects token theft: if the attacker uses the old token, family revoke forces re-login. Store refresh tokens as hashes, not plaintext — DB dump doesn't expose working tokens. Refresh cookies must be HttpOnly (XSS protection) and SameSite (CSRF protection). For B2B APIs, prefer OAuth 2.1 with short-lived access tokens + PAT-style long-lived keys for server-to-server. Consider passkeys (WebAuthn) as the login method — phishing-resistant and the 2026 gold standard.

Question 24

How would you handle cross-origin requests (CORS) to ensure secure access to your API from web applications?

Situation: SPA at app.example.com calls API at api.example.com; different origins trigger CORS. Browser sends preflights; cookies require credentials. Must allow known origins and block unknown ones.

Approach: explicit allowlist per environment; register CORS before auth so preflights don't 401; set credentials: true only if cookies/Authorization are needed; include all custom headers in allowedHeaders.

import cors from "cors";

const ALLOWED = new Set([
  "https://app.example.com",
  "https://admin.example.com",
  ...(process.env.NODE_ENV !== "production" ? ["http://localhost:5173"] : []),
]);

const corsOptions = {
  origin: (origin, cb) => {
    if (!origin) return cb(null, true);   // server-to-server / curl
    if (ALLOWED.has(origin)) return cb(null, true);
    // staging subdomains
    if (/^https:\/\/[a-z0-9-]+\.staging\.example\.com$/.test(origin)) return cb(null, true);
    cb(new Error(`CORS: origin ${origin} not allowed`));
  },
  credentials: true,                                // cookies
  methods: ["GET", "POST", "PUT", "PATCH", "DELETE"],
  allowedHeaders: [
    "Content-Type", "Authorization", "X-Request-Id",
    "Idempotency-Key", "X-CSRF-Token",
  ],
  exposedHeaders: ["X-Request-Id", "RateLimit-Remaining", "RateLimit-Reset"],
  maxAge: 86400,                                    // cache preflight 24h
};

// install BEFORE auth — preflights don't carry tokens
app.use(cors(corsOptions));
app.options("*", cors(corsOptions));

// public endpoints can override with permissive CORS
app.get("/status", cors({ origin: "*" }), (req, res) => res.json({ ok: true }));

// private endpoints
app.use("/api", authenticate, apiRouter);

Common bugs & fixes:

SymptomCauseFix
Preflight 401auth middleware runs before CORSInstall CORS first
Browser rejects * + credentialsinvalid combinationUse allowlist function
Custom header "disappears" in fetchnot in exposedHeadersAdd to exposedHeaders
Preflight on every requestno maxAgeSet maxAge: 86400
Error body missing from fetchCORS failed, response hiddenCheck response status in DevTools Network

Trade-offs: CORS is browser-enforced only; native apps, curl, and server-to-server calls ignore it. Real security comes from authentication + authorization, not CORS — CORS only prevents cross-site JavaScript from making credentialed calls. Don't over-restrict: reject unknown origins, but include your staging environments and local dev explicitly. Never use origin: "*" with credentials: true — browsers refuse this combination; use a function that echoes the origin if allowed. For public APIs with many unknown consumers (SDKs, embeds), origin: "*" is fine if requests don't carry credentials; auth via bearer token in Authorization still works cross-origin. SameSite cookies + CSRF tokens are complementary defenses for session-cookie flows.

Question 25

A client needs to upload large files to your API. How would you handle this, ensuring reliability and performance?

Situation: users upload 5-500 MB files (videos, backups, datasets). Buffering through the API tier OOMs at scale; slow networks mean uploads take minutes and fail mid-transfer. Must handle retries, resumability, progress.

Approach: pre-signed S3 multipart upload. Client cuts the file into chunks (5 MB each), uploads each directly to S3 with its own signed URL, reports progress per chunk, and finalizes with a commit call. Node never touches the bytes.

import { S3Client, CreateMultipartUploadCommand, UploadPartCommand,
         CompleteMultipartUploadCommand, AbortMultipartUploadCommand,
         HeadObjectCommand } from "@aws-sdk/client-s3";
import { getSignedUrl } from "@aws-sdk/s3-request-presigner";

const s3 = new S3Client({ region: "us-east-1" });
const CHUNK = 5 * 1024 * 1024;   // 5 MB — S3 minimum
const MAX_SIZE = 5 * 1024 * 1024 * 1024;   // 5 GB

// 1. INIT — return signed URLs for all parts
app.post("/uploads/init", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { filename, contentType, size } = req.body;
  if (size > MAX_SIZE || size <= 0) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Invalid size" });

  const key = `u/${req.user.sub}/${crypto.randomUUID()}/${sanitize(filename)}`;
  const { UploadId } = await s3.send(new CreateMultipartUploadCommand({
    Bucket: "uploads", Key: key, ContentType: contentType,
    Metadata: { "uploaded-by": req.user.sub },
  }));

  const partCount = Math.ceil(size / CHUNK);
  const partUrls = await Promise.all(
    Array.from({ length: partCount }, (_, i) =>
      getSignedUrl(s3, new UploadPartCommand({
        Bucket: "uploads", Key: key, UploadId, PartNumber: i + 1,
      }), { expiresIn: 3600 })
    )
  );

  await db.pendingUpload.create({
    data: { key, uploadId: UploadId, userId: req.user.sub, size,
            expiresAt: new Date(Date.now() + 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000) },
  });
  res.json({ uploadId: UploadId, key, partUrls, chunkSize: CHUNK });
});

// 2. COMPLETE — client reports {PartNumber, ETag} for each uploaded part
app.post("/uploads/complete", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { key, uploadId, parts } = req.body;
  const pending = await db.pendingUpload.findFirst({
    where: { key, uploadId, userId: req.user.sub },
  });
  if (!pending) return res.sendStatus(404);

  await s3.send(new CompleteMultipartUploadCommand({
    Bucket: "uploads", Key: key, UploadId: uploadId,
    MultipartUpload: { Parts: parts.sort((a, b) => a.PartNumber - b.PartNumber) },
  }));

  // verify final size matches to defend against tampering
  const head = await s3.send(new HeadObjectCommand({ Bucket: "uploads", Key: key }));
  if (Math.abs(head.ContentLength - pending.size) > 1024) {
    await s3.send(new AbortMultipartUploadCommand({ Bucket: "uploads", Key: key, UploadId: uploadId }));
    return res.status(400).json({ error: "Size mismatch" });
  }

  await db.$transaction([
    db.file.create({ data: { key, ownerId: req.user.sub, size: head.ContentLength } }),
    db.pendingUpload.delete({ where: { id: pending.id } }),
  ]);
  res.status(201).json({ key });
});

// 3. ABORT — clean up on cancel
app.post("/uploads/abort", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { key, uploadId } = req.body;
  await s3.send(new AbortMultipartUploadCommand({ Bucket: "uploads", Key: key, UploadId: uploadId }));
  await db.pendingUpload.deleteMany({ where: { key, uploadId, userId: req.user.sub } });
  res.sendStatus(204);
});

Client strategy: upload parts in parallel (3-5 concurrent); retry failed parts individually; persist upload state locally so a browser refresh can resume; use fetch with ReadableStream.pipeTo or xhr.upload.onprogress for progress.

Trade-offs: pre-signed multipart shifts complexity to the client but is the only approach that scales to large files on lossy networks. Resumable.js and tus.io are libraries that implement this pattern (tus has its own protocol supported by Vimeo, Uppy, Cloudflare Stream). Always validate post-upload (magic bytes, size, virus scan) before making the file accessible — the client claimed it was a PNG, verify it. Set lifecycle rules on the bucket to abort incomplete multipart uploads after 7 days (they cost storage even when incomplete). For video specifically, use specialized services (Mux, Cloudflare Stream, Bunny.net) that handle upload + transcode + delivery together.

Question 26

You need to implement a search functionality in your API that supports filtering and sorting. How would you design the endpoints?

Situation: users search a product catalog (millions of rows) with text query, category filter, price range, and sort order. Must be fast (<200ms), relevant, and handle misspellings.

Approach: a dedicated search index (Elasticsearch, OpenSearch, Meilisearch, or Typesense) for text relevance; the OLTP DB stays authoritative. Expose one endpoint with typed query params.

import { z } from "zod";
import { Client } from "@elastic/elasticsearch";
const es = new Client({ node: process.env.ES_URL });

const SearchSchema = z.object({
  q:         z.string().trim().min(1).max(200).optional(),
  category:  z.enum(["electronics", "books", "clothing"]).optional(),
  priceMin:  z.coerce.number().min(0).optional(),
  priceMax:  z.coerce.number().max(1_000_000).optional(),
  inStock:   z.coerce.boolean().optional(),
  sort:      z.enum(["relevance", "price_asc", "price_desc", "newest"]).default("relevance"),
  page:      z.coerce.number().int().min(1).max(100).default(1),
  limit:     z.coerce.number().int().min(1).max(50).default(24),
}).strict();

app.get("/products/search", async (req, res) => {
  const p = SearchSchema.parse(req.query);

  const must = [];
  if (p.q) must.push({
    multi_match: {
      query: p.q,
      fields: ["name^3", "description", "tags^2", "brand^2"],
      fuzziness: "AUTO",             // handle typos
      operator: "and",
    },
  });

  const filter = [];
  if (p.category) filter.push({ term: { category: p.category } });
  if (p.inStock !== undefined) filter.push({ term: { inStock: p.inStock } });
  if (p.priceMin !== undefined || p.priceMax !== undefined) {
    const range = {};
    if (p.priceMin !== undefined) range.gte = p.priceMin;
    if (p.priceMax !== undefined) range.lte = p.priceMax;
    filter.push({ range: { price: range } });
  }

  const sort = {
    relevance:  ["_score"],
    price_asc:  [{ price: "asc" }, "_score"],
    price_desc: [{ price: "desc" }, "_score"],
    newest:     [{ createdAt: "desc" }],
  }[p.sort];

  const { hits } = await es.search({
    index: "products",
    query: { bool: { must, filter } },
    sort,
    from: (p.page - 1) * p.limit,
    size: p.limit,
    highlight: { fields: { name: {}, description: {} } },
    aggs: {    // facets for filter sidebar
      by_category: { terms: { field: "category" } },
      price_stats: { stats: { field: "price" } },
    },
  });

  res.set("Cache-Control", "public, max-age=30").json({
    total: hits.total.value,
    data:  hits.hits.map((h) => ({ ...h._source, _highlight: h.highlight })),
    facets: {
      categories: hits.aggregations.by_category.buckets,
      priceRange: hits.aggregations.price_stats,
    },
    page: p.page, limit: p.limit,
  });
});

Indexing: write to ES from the outbox pattern — on every product update, enqueue an index job; worker upserts into ES. On deletes, remove from ES. Reindex from scratch quarterly or on schema changes via a cursor-paginated scan of the source DB.

Trade-offs: a dedicated search engine is overkill for <100k rows — Postgres full-text search (tsvector/tsquery) handles up to a few million rows well with a GIN index. For hundreds of millions of rows or complex relevance (synonyms, stemming, multi-language, learning-to-rank), Elasticsearch/OpenSearch are standard; Meilisearch and Typesense are simpler modern alternatives with excellent defaults and typo tolerance out of the box. Cache the hot queries (homepage carousels, popular searches). Always validate/bound params (limit, page, query length) — search is a common DoS target. For personalized relevance, pass userId and rerank at the edge; for semantic search, add vector similarity alongside keyword matching (hybrid search).

Question 27

Your API is experiencing a high rate of failed requests. How would you troubleshoot and resolve the issue?

Situation: error rate jumped from 0.1% to 5% in the last hour. Dashboard is red; phone is ringing. Must diagnose quickly, stabilize, then root-cause.

Approach: follow an incident checklist — observe, mitigate, investigate. Don't guess; look at data.

// Step 1 — WHAT broke? Filter errors by endpoint, status, user, region.
// Typical dashboards: Datadog/Grafana query on error logs
// rate({service="api", level="error"}[5m]) by (endpoint, status, region)

// Step 2 — WHEN did it start? Correlate with deploys, feature flags, upstream outages.
git log --since="2 hours ago" --format="%h %s %ar"
kubectl rollout history deployment/api
aws rds describe-events --duration 60
// Check status pages for dependencies: https://status.stripe.com, status.aws.amazon.com

// Step 3 — WHY? Grep logs with the request pattern; open a trace for a recent failure.
// Look for: new exception class, spike in DB connection errors,
// timeout to specific upstream, rate-limit responses from 3rd party,
// memory/CPU saturation.

Common causes and mitigations:

SymptomLikely causeQuick mitigation
Rising 5xx correlated with deployBad releaseRoll back deployment immediately
5xx from downstream timeouts3rd-party slow/downOpen circuit breaker; return cached/fallback
DB connection errorsPool exhaustion or DB downScale up pool; check DB metrics; PgBouncer
4xx spike with 429Traffic surge / bot attackLower rate limits; enable Cloudflare Under Attack mode
Memory OOM restartsLeak or traffic spikeScale horizontally; heap dump for analysis
Specific endpoint failingRecent code change, schema driftRevert handler or feature flag

Stabilization first, then RCA. If a deploy is suspect, roll back — don't debate. Feature-flagged changes can be toggled without a deploy. Traffic shifts to a healthy region via DNS failover. After mitigation stops the bleed, the incident channel continues RCA calmly.

Post-incident: blameless retrospective documents what broke, why it took N minutes to detect, what helped and hindered response. Action items become issues with owners: add missing alert, fix fragile code, improve runbook.
