Error budget tracks cumulative downtime; when the budget's exhausted, feature work pauses in favor of reliability investments (Google SRE pattern).

Trade-offs: the fastest path to low MTTR is investing in observability before you need it — dashboards for every major dependency, alerts on SLO burn rate, distributed tracing linked to logs, runbooks in Confluence. During an incident is the worst time to set up Datadog. Automated rollback (health checks that trip on elevated error rate) prevents bad deploys from ever serving significant traffic. Canary deploys catch 90% of deploy-caused regressions before full rollout.

Question 28

How would you implement a feature that allows users to recover their accounts via email?

Situation: user forgot password; needs to regain access without calling support. Flow must be secure (no account takeover), user-friendly (works on mobile), and resistant to enumeration (attacker can't discover which emails are registered).

Approach: request → email token → verify → set new password. Token is single-use, time-bound, and stored as a hash.

import crypto from "node:crypto";
import bcrypt from "bcrypt";
import { z } from "zod";

// 1. REQUEST — always return the same shape, regardless of whether email exists
const ResetRequestSchema = z.object({
  email: z.string().trim().toLowerCase().email(),
}).strict();

app.post("/auth/password-reset/request",
  authLimiter,
  validate({ body: ResetRequestSchema }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const { email } = req.body;
    const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { email } });

    // ALWAYS respond the same way to prevent enumeration
    res.status(202).json({
      message: "If that email is registered, a reset link has been sent.",
    });

    // Only if user exists, actually send. Run AFTER response to avoid timing leaks.
    if (user) {
      setImmediate(async () => {
        try {
          const raw = crypto.randomBytes(32).toString("base64url");
          const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(raw).digest("hex");
          await db.passwordResetToken.create({
            data: {
              userId:    user.id,
              tokenHash: hash,
              expiresAt: new Date(Date.now() + 60 * 60 * 1000),   // 1 hour
            },
          });
          await sendEmail(email, {
            subject: "Reset your password",
            body:    `Click to reset: ${APP_URL}/reset?token=${raw}&u=${user.id}`,
          });
        } catch (err) { log.error({ err }, "password reset send failed"); }
      });
    }
  }
);

// 2. VERIFY — apply new password
const ResetConfirmSchema = z.object({
  userId:   z.string().uuid(),
  token:    z.string().min(20).max(100),
  password: z.string().min(10).max(128),
}).strict();

app.post("/auth/password-reset/confirm",
  authLimiter,
  validate({ body: ResetConfirmSchema }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const { userId, token, password } = req.body;
    const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(token).digest("hex");

    const rec = await db.passwordResetToken.findFirst({
      where: {
        userId, tokenHash: hash,
        usedAt: null,
        expiresAt: { gt: new Date() },
      },
    });
    if (!rec) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Invalid or expired token" });

    // apply atomically — mark token used + update password + revoke sessions
    const passwordHash = await bcrypt.hash(password, 12);
    await db.$transaction([
      db.user.update({ where: { id: userId }, data: { passwordHash } }),
      db.passwordResetToken.update({ where: { id: rec.id }, data: { usedAt: new Date() } }),
      db.refreshToken.updateMany({
        where: { userId }, data: { revokedAt: new Date() },
      }),     // force re-login on all devices
    ]);

    // notify the user the password changed (detect account takeover)
    sendEmail(user.email, { subject: "Your password was changed", body: "..." });

    res.json({ success: true });
  }
);

Trade-offs: email is not strong auth by itself — anyone with access to the email can reset the password. For high-value accounts (financial, healthcare), layer MFA on top: require a TOTP or passkey challenge to complete reset. Magic links (one-click login) are a UX win but should be paired with step-up MFA for sensitive actions. Rate-limit reset requests per email and per IP to prevent spam and enumeration via timing. Revoke existing sessions on password change so a compromised session doesn't survive. Email providers sometimes mangle URLs; use short, URL-safe tokens and test with Gmail/Outlook. Passkeys (WebAuthn) eliminate this flow entirely for supporting accounts — they're the 2026 direction.

Question 29

Describe how you would use middleware to handle common tasks like logging and authentication in your API.

Situation: many endpoints need the same cross-cutting logic — authenticate, log, set request ID, parse body, validate schema, handle errors. Duplicating in every handler invites bugs and inconsistency.

Approach: Express middleware chain, installed in the right order, handles these concerns once. Handlers focus on business logic.

import express from "express";
import helmet from "helmet";
import cors from "cors";
import compression from "compression";
import { pinoHttp } from "pino-http";
import crypto from "node:crypto";
import { AsyncLocalStorage } from "node:async_hooks";

const app = express();
const requestContext = new AsyncLocalStorage();

// ORDER MATTERS — each middleware runs for every request in this order
// 1. Security headers — before anything else
app.use(helmet());

// 2. CORS — before auth so preflights work
app.use(cors(corsOptions));

// 3. Compression — before body parsing
app.use(compression());

// 4. Request ID — threads through async chain via ALS
app.use((req, res, next) => {
  const id = req.headers["x-request-id"] || crypto.randomUUID();
  req.id = id;
  res.setHeader("X-Request-Id", id);
  requestContext.run({ reqId: id }, next);
});

// 5. Structured logging — captures request/response
app.use(pinoHttp({
  redact: ["req.headers.authorization", "req.headers.cookie"],
  customProps: (req) => ({ reqId: req.id, userId: req.user?.sub }),
}));

// 6. Body parsing — with size limits
app.use(express.json({ limit: "100kb" }));
app.use(express.urlencoded({ extended: true, limit: "100kb" }));

// 7. Rate limiting — before expensive auth work
app.use(rateLimiter);

// 8. Authentication — extracts req.user
app.use("/api", authenticate);

// 9. Route handlers
app.use("/api/users", usersRouter);
app.use("/api/posts", postsRouter);

// 10. 404 fallback
app.use((req, res) => res.status(404).json({ error: "Not found" }));

// 11. Error handler — must be LAST, four arguments
app.use((err, req, res, next) => {
  req.log.error({ err, reqId: req.id }, "Unhandled error");
  if (res.headersSent) return next(err);
  const status = err.status || 500;
  res.status(status).type("application/problem+json").json({
    type:     err.type || "about:blank",
    title:    status >= 500 ? "Internal error" : err.message,
    status,
    reqId:    req.id,
  });
});

Per-route middleware: schema validation, role checks, and feature flags attach per-route rather than globally.

import { z } from "zod";

const validate = (schema) => (req, res, next) => {
  const r = schema.safeParse(req.body);
  if (!r.success) return res.status(400).json({ error: r.error.issues });
  req.body = r.data;
  next();
};

router.post("/",
  requireRole("admin"),
  validate(CreateUserSchema),
  createUser
);

Trade-offs: middleware order is the silent bug factory — CORS before auth, error handler last, compression before body parsing. Document the chain in code with numbered comments. Don't put business logic in middleware — it ends up hidden and hard to test; keep middleware focused on infrastructure (auth, logging, parsing). AsyncLocalStorage threads request context through every async call so logs from downstream code can reference reqId and userId without passing them explicitly. For serverless platforms that don't use Express, the same patterns apply via handler wrappers (middy on AWS Lambda). Test middleware in isolation with supertest; integration-test the chain end-to-end.

Question 30

You need to design an API for a messaging platform. Describe the endpoints and data structures you would use.

Situation: design a messaging API supporting 1:1 and group chats, messages with text/media, delivery receipts, typing indicators, and history. Must feel real-time and work offline.

Approach: REST for CRUD on conversations and messages; WebSocket for live events (new messages, typing, presence); object storage for media; push notifications for offline recipients.

// REST endpoints
POST   /conversations                 → create 1:1 or group
  body: { type: "direct" | "group", memberIds: [...], name?: "..." }
GET    /conversations                 → list user's conversations (paginated)
GET    /conversations/:id             → details (members, name, settings)
PATCH  /conversations/:id             → rename, add/remove members
POST   /conversations/:id/messages    → send a message (idempotency-key supported)
GET    /conversations/:id/messages?before=... → paginated history
POST   /conversations/:id/read        → mark read up to message N
POST   /conversations/:id/typing      → signal typing (rate-limited)
DELETE /messages/:id                  → soft-delete own message

// WebSocket — one connection per client, multiplexed channels
GET    /ws  (with JWT auth)           → subscribe to user's conversations

// events from server
{ "type": "message.new",     "conversationId": "c1", "message": {...} }
{ "type": "message.read",    "conversationId": "c1", "userId": "u2", "upTo": "m_99" }
{ "type": "typing.start",    "conversationId": "c1", "userId": "u2" }
{ "type": "conversation.updated", "conversationId": "c1", "changes": {...} }
// data model (simplified)
Conversation { id, type: "direct" | "group", name?, createdAt, lastMessageAt }
ConversationMember { conversationId, userId, role, lastReadMessageId, joinedAt }
Message { id, conversationId, senderId, content, media[]?, replyToId?, createdAt, deletedAt? }

// hot partition indexes
// (conversationId, createdAt DESC) — fetch recent messages
// (userId, lastMessageAt DESC) via ConversationMember → Conversation join — user's inbox sorted by activity

Send flow:

  1. Client generates a clientMsgId (UUID) and sends POST with Idempotency-Key; API persists message, pushes via WebSocket to all conversation members online, queues push notifications for offline members.
  2. Sender receives { status: "sent", messageId, createdAt }; replaces local tempId with real id.
  3. Recipients' clients see WebSocket message.new; mark conversation unread, update last-message preview.
  4. Recipient opens conversation → POST /read updates lastReadMessageId; server pushes message.read to sender's other devices.

Trade-offs: managed services (Sendbird, Stream, Twilio Conversations, CometChat) handle the hard parts (presence, delivery guarantees, global fan-out) and are worth considering over building from scratch. For rolling your own, use Redis pub/sub or NATS/Kafka for multi-instance fan-out; PostgreSQL stores durable messages; S3 stores media via pre-signed uploads. Exactly-once delivery is impossible — clients dedupe via clientMsgId. For E2E encryption (Signal, WhatsApp), the server never sees plaintext — the Signal Protocol / MLS handles key agreement. Offline support requires client-side SQLite/IndexedDB cache + sync-on-connect. Typing indicators should be rate-limited to ~1/sec per conversation; otherwise they flood the network.

Question 31

A client needs real-time updates from your API. How would you implement a subscription mechanism to provide these updates?

Situation: dashboard needs live metrics (requests/sec, errors, revenue counter) that update every second without page refresh. Clients are browsers; future: mobile apps.

Approach: GraphQL subscriptions over WebSocket for typed, schema-driven real-time — clients subscribe to specific fields. Alternative: plain SSE with custom event types for simpler setups. Both fan out via Redis/pub-sub from event producers.

// Schema
type Subscription {
  metricsUpdated(dashboardId: ID!): DashboardMetrics!
  orderCreated:                    Order!     # global feed
  userPresence(userId: ID!):       PresenceStatus!
}

type DashboardMetrics {
  requestsPerSec: Int!
  errorsPerSec:   Int!
  p99LatencyMs:   Float!
  revenueToday:   Float!
  ts:             DateTime!
}
// Apollo Server subscriptions via graphql-ws + Redis PubSub
import { createServer } from "node:http";
import { WebSocketServer } from "ws";
import { useServer } from "graphql-ws/use/ws";
import { RedisPubSub } from "graphql-redis-subscriptions";

const pubsub = new RedisPubSub({
  publisher: redis, subscriber: redis.duplicate(),
});

const resolvers = {
  Subscription: {
    metricsUpdated: {
      subscribe: (_, { dashboardId }, ctx) => {
        if (!ctx.user) throw new Error("Unauthorized");
        return pubsub.asyncIterator(`dashboard:${dashboardId}:metrics`);
      },
      resolve: (payload) => payload,
    },
    orderCreated: {
      subscribe: (_, __, ctx) => {
        if (ctx.user?.role !== "admin") throw new Error("Forbidden");
        return pubsub.asyncIterator("order:created");
      },
      resolve: (order) => order,
    },
  },
  Mutation: {
    createOrder: async (_, args, ctx) => {
      const order = await db.order.create({ data: args });
      pubsub.publish("order:created", order);
      pubsub.publish(`dashboard:${args.dashboardId}:metrics`, await calcMetrics());
      return order;
    },
  },
};

const httpServer = createServer(expressApp);
const wss = new WebSocketServer({ server: httpServer, path: "/graphql" });
useServer({
  schema,
  context: async (ctx) => ({
    user: await verifyJwt(ctx.connectionParams?.authorization),
  }),
}, wss);

Producers push to Redis from any service; all subscribed clients receive. A metrics aggregator reads from Prometheus/Datadog every second and publishes to the dashboard:*:metrics channel.

Trade-offs: GraphQL subscriptions are powerful but add complexity — schema, resolvers, auth on both connection and each subscribe. For simple one-way streams, SSE wins on simplicity (EventSource in browsers auto-reconnects with Last-Event-Id). WebSockets are needed for bidirectional (chat) or when sending data both ways in one connection. Connection limits: each WebSocket holds memory; scale horizontally and route fanout via Redis. Backpressure: a slow client shouldn't block publishers — use bounded queues and drop old messages if a client falls far behind. Auth: verify JWT on connection + on each subscribe (users can revoke tokens). Managed options: Pusher, Ably, Liveblocks, Supabase Realtime, PartyKit abstract the infrastructure — good for greenfield; less so for deep integration with existing systems.

Question 32

How would you ensure that your API can handle spikes in traffic without degrading performance?

Situation: product launch, marketing email blast, or viral event drives traffic 10-100x baseline. Normal capacity can't handle it; must survive without downtime and recover quickly after.

Approach: defense in depth — autoscaling, caching, queueing, rate limiting, graceful degradation. Each reduces load on the layer behind it.

  • CDN & edge cache: serve static assets and cacheable GETs from edge; origin sees only the uncacheable traffic. 90%+ of traffic never hits your API for well-cached content.
  • Autoscaling: HPA on CPU and RPS; pre-warm instances before known spikes (email blast at 9am = scale up at 8:45am via scheduled scaling).
  • Provisioned concurrency for serverless (Lambda) to eliminate cold starts during spikes.
  • Database read replicas absorb read traffic; route analytics queries to a separate warehouse.
  • Queue-based load leveling: write-heavy endpoints (sign-ups, orders) accept into a queue; workers process at sustainable rate. Client sees "processing" briefly rather than 503.
  • Rate limiting & quotas per user/IP to prevent one client (or bot) from consuming shared capacity.
  • Graceful degradation: disable expensive features (personalized recommendations, complex analytics) under load; serve core functionality only.
  • Circuit breakers around downstream services so a slow dependency doesn't cascade.
// feature flags for graceful degradation
const degradedMode = await redis.get("degraded:mode");

app.get("/feed", async (req, res) => {
  if (degradedMode === "aggressive") {
    // serve the generic top-10 feed, not personalized
    return res.set("Cache-Control", "public, max-age=300")
              .json(await getGlobalFeed());
  }
  // normal mode: personalized feed (expensive)
  return res.json(await getPersonalizedFeed(req.user.sub));
});

// load-shedding middleware — drop low-priority requests first
app.use((req, res, next) => {
  if (cpuPercent() > 90 && req.headers["x-priority"] !== "high") {
    return res.status(503)
              .set("Retry-After", "30")
              .json({ error: "Server overloaded; retry" });
  }
  next();
});

Load testing before the spike: k6, Artillery, or Gatling simulate expected and 2x expected load. Validate that autoscaling triggers, DB doesn't brown out, error rate stays acceptable. Chaos drills verify runbook accuracy.

Trade-offs: capacity has a cost — running 10x headroom always is expensive; autoscaling is cheaper but scales slowly (new instances take 60-90s to be healthy). For known spikes, pre-scale. For unpredictable spikes, over-provision by 30-50% and autoscale on top. Database is usually the weak link — compute scales horizontally easily; DBs have connection limits and write bottlenecks. Address with read replicas, connection pooling (PgBouncer, RDS Proxy), and sharding if truly large. Graceful degradation preserves user experience during genuine overload: slow is better than broken, incomplete is better than 500. Feature flags let operators disable expensive paths in seconds without a deploy.

Question 33

Describe how you would implement a custom validation mechanism for incoming API requests.

Situation: need consistent request validation across 100+ endpoints: string lengths, numeric ranges, enums, nested objects, arrays, cross-field rules. Hand-rolling per-endpoint validation produces inconsistency and bugs.

Approach: a schema library (Zod, Valibot, Ajv, Yup, or class-validator) with a factory middleware. Schemas double as TypeScript types and OpenAPI fragments.

import { z } from "zod";

// factory — one validator, per-route schemas
export function validate(schemas) {
  return (req, res, next) => {
    const issues = [];

    for (const loc of ["body", "query", "params"]) {
      if (!schemas[loc]) continue;
      const r = schemas[loc].safeParse(req[loc]);
      if (!r.success) {
        for (const issue of r.error.issues) {
          issues.push({
            location: loc,
            field:    issue.path.join("."),
            code:     issue.code,
            message:  issue.message,
          });
        }
      } else {
        req[loc] = r.data;         // coerced + cleaned
      }
    }

    if (issues.length) {
      return res.status(400)
                .type("application/problem+json")
                .json({
                  type:   "https://api.example.com/problems/validation-error",
                  title:  "Validation failed",
                  status: 400,
                  errors: issues,
                });
    }
    next();
  };
}

// reusable schema pieces
const Id = z.string().uuid();
const Email = z.string().trim().toLowerCase().email().max(254);
const Pagination = z.object({
  limit:  z.coerce.number().int().min(1).max(100).default(20),
  cursor: z.string().optional(),
});

// composable + cross-field rules
const CreateEventSchema = z.object({
  title:     z.string().trim().min(1).max(200),
  startsAt:  z.coerce.date(),
  endsAt:    z.coerce.date(),
  capacity:  z.number().int().min(1).max(10_000),
  venue:     z.object({
    name:    z.string().trim().min(1).max(200),
    address: z.string().trim().min(1).max(500),
  }),
  tags:      z.array(z.string().trim().min(1).max(30)).max(10).default([]),
}).strict().refine(
  (v) => v.endsAt > v.startsAt,
  { message: "endsAt must be after startsAt", path: ["endsAt"] }
);

app.post("/events",
  requireAuth,
  validate({ body: CreateEventSchema }),
  async (req, res) => res.status(201).json(
    await db.event.create({ data: { ...req.body, organizerId: req.user.sub } })
  )
);

Trade-offs: Zod's TypeScript inference is excellent (type Event = z.infer<typeof CreateEventSchema>), so schemas are the single source of truth. Ajv (JSON Schema) is faster for very hot paths and integrates cleanly with OpenAPI specs but has less ergonomic TypeScript. Valibot is newer, ~10x smaller than Zod — good for browser bundles. Coercion (z.coerce.number()) is essential for query params which arrive as strings. .strict() rejects unknown fields, a defense against mass-assignment (client sending { email, role: "admin" }). Aggregate all validation errors in one response rather than failing on the first — better UX. For internationalized messages, plug in translated validators. Generate OpenAPI from schemas via zod-to-openapi so docs stay in sync with code.

Question 34

Your API needs to support different user roles with varying permissions. How would you implement this?

Situation: users have roles (viewer, editor, admin, billing-owner); permissions vary per endpoint and per resource (e.g., editor can edit their own posts, not others'). Need consistent enforcement and flexible policy.

Approach: RBAC for coarse role-to-permission mapping; ownership checks at the resource level; policy engine for complex cross-resource rules.

// 1. simple RBAC — role-to-permission matrix
const PERMISSIONS = {
  viewer:  ["posts:read", "comments:read"],
  editor:  ["posts:read", "posts:write", "comments:read", "comments:write"],
  admin:   ["posts:*", "comments:*", "users:read", "users:write"],
  billing: ["invoices:read", "invoices:write"],
};

function hasPermission(role, required) {
  const perms = PERMISSIONS[role] || [];
  return perms.some((p) =>
    p === required || p === required.split(":")[0] + ":*"
  );
}

export const requirePermission = (perm) => (req, res, next) => {
  if (!hasPermission(req.user.role, perm)) return res.sendStatus(403);
  next();
};

// 2. ownership checks — per-resource authorization
async function loadOwnPost(req, res, next) {
  const post = await db.post.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!post) return res.sendStatus(404);

  const isAdmin = req.user.role === "admin";
  const isOwner = post.authorId === req.user.sub;
  const isCollaborator = post.collaborators?.includes(req.user.sub);

  if (!isAdmin && !isOwner && !isCollaborator) {
    return res.sendStatus(404);     // 404 (not 403) hides existence
  }
  req.post = post;
  next();
}

// combine in routes
router.get("/posts/:id",
  requirePermission("posts:read"),
  loadOwnPost,
  (req, res) => res.json(req.post)
);
router.patch("/posts/:id",
  requirePermission("posts:write"),
  loadOwnPost,
  validate({ body: PostUpdateSchema }),
  updatePost
);

// 3. for complex rules — use a policy engine (OPA, Cedar, OpenFGA)
// Example: "user can approve invoices if amount <= their approval limit AND they're in the same org"
import { Cedar } from "@cedar-policy/cedar-wasm";

const policies = `
  permit (
    principal,
    action == Action::"approveInvoice",
    resource
  ) when {
    principal.orgId == resource.orgId &&
    principal.approvalLimit >= resource.amount
  };
`;

const allowed = await cedar.isAuthorized({
  principal: { type: "User", id: req.user.sub, attrs: req.user },
  action:    { type: "Action", id: "approveInvoice" },
  resource:  { type: "Invoice", id: invoice.id, attrs: invoice },
  policies,
});
if (!allowed) return res.sendStatus(403);

Trade-offs: RBAC is fine for most apps; roles are user-friendly ("assign admin role") and easy to reason about. ABAC (attribute-based) handles nuanced rules (time-of-day, location, resource attributes) but is harder to audit. ReBAC (relationship-based, like Google's Zanzibar, OpenFGA, SpiceDB) is best for collaboration apps where "who can view this doc" depends on sharing relationships — the common failure mode of RBAC. Always defense-in-depth: middleware checks + row-level-security in Postgres + application ownership checks. A bug in one layer shouldn't leak data. Test extensively: every role × every endpoint × edge cases (disabled account, expired role, deleted resource). For hierarchical orgs (team → project → resource), cache the user's permission set to avoid N queries per request.

Question 35

How would you design an API that allows clients to schedule tasks and reminders?

Situation: users schedule reminders ("remind me to call Mom on Saturday at 10am") and recurring tasks ("send invoice every 1st of the month"). Must fire at the right time in the user's timezone, survive restarts, and retry on failure.

Approach: a scheduled-job system (BullMQ, Temporal, Agenda, or platform-native like AWS EventBridge) persists scheduled work and executes at the right time. API exposes CRUD on schedules; underlying engine does the timing.

// API endpoints
POST   /reminders                     → create
GET    /reminders                     → list user's reminders
GET    /reminders/:id                 → details
PATCH  /reminders/:id                 → edit (snooze, retitle)
DELETE /reminders/:id                 → cancel
POST   /reminders/:id/dismiss         → mark completed (for recurring: skip next)

// Data model
Reminder {
  id, userId, title, body,
  startsAt: DateTime,        // UTC — authoritative timestamp
  timezone: String,          // "America/Los_Angeles" — for RRULE eval & display
  rrule?:   String,          // iCal RRULE for recurrence
  nextFireAt: DateTime,      // computed; the next scheduled occurrence
  channel:  "push" | "email" | "sms" | "webhook",
  active: Boolean,
}
// use BullMQ with scheduled jobs
import { Queue } from "bullmq";
import { RRule } from "rrule";
const remindQueue = new Queue("reminders", { connection: redis });

app.post("/reminders", requireAuth, validate({ body: CreateReminderSchema }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const { title, body, startsAt, timezone, rrule, channel } = req.body;

    const reminder = await db.reminder.create({
      data: {
        userId: req.user.sub,
        title, body,
        startsAt: new Date(startsAt),
        timezone, rrule, channel,
        nextFireAt: new Date(startsAt),
        active: true,
      },
    });

    // schedule the first firing
    const delay = reminder.nextFireAt.getTime() - Date.now();
    await remindQueue.add("fire", { reminderId: reminder.id },
      { delay: Math.max(0, delay), jobId: `remind:${reminder.id}` }
    );

    res.status(201).json(reminder);
  }
);

// WORKER — fires at the scheduled time
new Worker("reminders", async (job) => {
  const r = await db.reminder.findUnique({ where: { id: job.data.reminderId } });
  if (!r || !r.active) return;

  // dispatch the notification
  await sendNotification(r.userId, { title: r.title, body: r.body, channel: r.channel });
  await db.reminderFiring.create({ data: { reminderId: r.id, firedAt: new Date() } });

  // re-schedule next occurrence if recurring
  if (r.rrule) {
    const rule = RRule.fromString(r.rrule);
    const next = rule.after(new Date(), false);
    if (next) {
      await db.reminder.update({ where: { id: r.id }, data: { nextFireAt: next } });
      await remindQueue.add("fire", { reminderId: r.id },
        { delay: next.getTime() - Date.now(), jobId: `remind:${r.id}:${next.toISOString()}` });
    } else {
      await db.reminder.update({ where: { id: r.id }, data: { active: false } });
    }
  }
}, { connection: redis });

Trade-offs: Timezone handling is error-prone — always store UTC timestamps; use the user's timezone for RRULE evaluation and display. A reminder "every Monday at 9am" means 9am in their zone; DST changes shift the UTC instant — recompute nextFireAt each occurrence. Idempotency: a jobId per occurrence prevents duplicates if the worker restarts mid-scheduling. Missed fires: on worker restart, scan for nextFireAt <= now && !fired and fire with a "delayed by X" note if within grace period, else skip. Scale: BullMQ handles millions of delayed jobs; for heavier workloads (billions), Temporal or AWS EventBridge Scheduler are purpose-built. For precise recurring schedules, rrule.js implements iCal RRULE correctly including complex rules like "last Friday of the month." Test with timezones like America/Los_Angeles across DST transitions — the bug-catcher.

Question 36

A client reports that your API is slow. How would you diagnose and improve the performance?

Situation: user reports "the API is slow." Vague complaint; could be one endpoint, one user, one region, or one query param. Need to localize then fix.

Approach: measure before changing anything. Define "slow," find the slow thing, profile it, fix the bottleneck.

  1. Reproduce or observe: ask which endpoint, which user, what time. Look at traces/metrics for that window.
  2. Measure with RED: rate, errors, duration per endpoint. Find the p95/p99 outliers.
  3. Profile a slow trace: distributed trace shows where time went — DB, external call, compute, queue wait.
  4. Reproduce in isolation: same request with curl or Postman; time each part.
  5. Fix by category below.
  6. Verify: re-measure; confirm improvement; add regression test.

Bottleneck taxonomy and fixes:

Symptom in traceCauseFix
Many small DB spansN+1 queriesEager-load / DataLoader / JOIN
Single long DB spanMissing index or bad query planEXPLAIN ANALYZE; add index; denormalize
Long external-service spanSlow upstreamCache response; circuit-break; async
Long compute spanCPU work (serialization, parsing, cryptography)Profile with 0x/clinic.js; streaming; algorithmic fix
Gaps between spansConnection pool wait, event loop lagTune pool; move work off event loop
Slow only for some usersSkewed data (power user with 10k items)Pagination; filter default; different query plan
Slow only in one regionCross-region DB callRead replica per region; regional sharding
// profile a hot endpoint with clinic.js
// npx clinic doctor -- node server.js
// load-test with autocannon -c 100 -d 30 http://localhost:3000/slow

// or attach performance marks for fine-grained timing
app.get("/slow", async (req, res) => {
  performance.mark("start");

  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  performance.mark("db-done");

  const enriched = await enrichFromApi(user);
  performance.mark("api-done");

  performance.measure("db", "start", "db-done");
  performance.measure("api", "db-done", "api-done");
  // measures in req.log or Prometheus

  res.json(enriched);
});

Trade-offs: the instinct is to start coding optimizations; resist. The biggest performance win is usually a better data-access pattern — one missing index fixes what no amount of code tuning will. Caching (in-process LRU → Redis → CDN) multiplies effective capacity but adds staleness and invalidation burden; add TTL + explicit invalidation on writes. Async offloading (move work to background queues) reduces p99 for the request while doing the same total work. Profile first, optimize second — most "obvious" bottlenecks are wrong; measurement finds the real one. Add SLOs after fixing so regressions are caught automatically next time.

Question 37

Describe how you would implement a logging and monitoring system for your API to detect and respond to issues.

Situation: operate an API reliably: detect failures fast, debug quickly, know which customers are affected, meet SLOs. Need logging + metrics + tracing working together.

Approach: three pillars — structured logs, metrics, distributed traces. Each answers different questions; together they cover detection, alerting, debugging, and capacity planning.

// 1. Structured logging — pino for speed, JSON for grepability
import pino from "pino";
const log = pino({
  level: process.env.LOG_LEVEL || "info",
  redact: ["req.headers.authorization", "req.headers.cookie", "*.password", "*.ssn"],
  mixin: () => ({ env: process.env.NODE_ENV, service: "api" }),
});

app.use((req, res, next) => {
  req.log = log.child({ reqId: req.id, userId: req.user?.sub });
  res.on("finish", () => {
    req.log.info({
      method: req.method, path: req.route?.path ?? req.path,
      status: res.statusCode, durMs: Date.now() - req.startTime,
    }, "request completed");
  });
  next();
});

// 2. Metrics — Prometheus client library
import client from "prom-client";
const httpDuration = new client.Histogram({
  name: "http_request_duration_seconds",
  help: "HTTP request latency",
  labelNames: ["method", "route", "status"],
  buckets: [0.01, 0.05, 0.1, 0.5, 1, 5],
});
app.use((req, res, next) => {
  const end = httpDuration.startTimer();
  res.on("finish", () => end({
    method: req.method,
    route:  req.route?.path ?? "unknown",
    status: res.statusCode,
  }));
  next();
});
app.get("/metrics", async (req, res) => {
  res.set("Content-Type", client.register.contentType);
  res.end(await client.register.metrics());
});

// 3. Tracing — OpenTelemetry auto-instrumentation
import { NodeSDK } from "@opentelemetry/sdk-node";
import { getNodeAutoInstrumentations } from "@opentelemetry/auto-instrumentations-node";
new NodeSDK({
  instrumentations: [getNodeAutoInstrumentations()],
}).start();
// Now every Express request, Postgres query, Redis call, and fetch()
// produces a span linked to its parent — visible in Jaeger/Tempo/Datadog.

Alerting strategy:

  • SLO burn-rate alerts: alert when error budget burns fast — e.g., "at this rate, we'll exhaust the monthly SLO in 1 hour."
  • Latency anomaly: p99 2x baseline for 5 min.
  • Error spike: 5xx rate above 1% for 5 min.
  • Saturation: CPU, memory, DB connections above 85% sustained.
  • External deps: upstream error rate or latency; synthetic probe failures.
  • Business metrics: signups per hour, orders per minute — catch silent failures (API is fine but feature broken).

Trade-offs: don't alert on everything — alert fatigue makes every alert ignored. Alert on SLO burn and symptoms users feel, not on every subsystem anomaly. Log sampling for high-traffic successful paths (keep 1%); log everything for errors. Trace sampling: head-based (fixed %) for normal traffic; tail-based (sample interesting traces after they complete) for better signal. Retention: traces for days (expensive), metrics for months (cheap), logs vary. Cost management: Datadog/New Relic/Splunk bills can dwarf infrastructure; evaluate Grafana Cloud, self-hosted Loki+Tempo+Prometheus, or ClickHouse-based (Signoz) for alternatives. Tie all three pillars via traceId so a slow endpoint alert → trace → logs → code fix takes minutes.

Question 38

Your API needs to be accessible via mobile devices. What considerations would you make to ensure optimal performance and usability?

Situation: mobile app consumes the API over variable connections (4G, Wi-Fi, airplane Wi-Fi, EDGE). Battery, bandwidth, and latency budgets are tight; app spends 50%+ of its lifetime offline or backgrounded.

Approach: design for constraints — minimize round-trips, small payloads, support offline, efficient auth, push over poll.

  • Backend For Frontend (BFF): one endpoint per screen aggregating everything needed. GET /mobile/home returns profile + feed + unread counts + badges in a single round-trip.
  • Compact payloads: field selection via GraphQL or sparse fieldsets (?fields=id,title,thumb); omit null/default values; gzip/brotli compression.
  • Delta sync: GET /feed?since=cursor returns only changes; full replace is expensive on metered connections.
  • Idempotency: mutations carry client-generated UUID; safe to retry after network flake without duplicating.
  • Offline queue: client caches pending mutations; replays on reconnect with idempotency keys.
  • Push, not poll: APNs/FCM wake the app when data changes; polling drains battery.
  • Adaptive media: API returns multiple image/video sizes; client picks based on network class.
  • OAuth 2.1 + PKCE: no embedded secrets; refresh tokens in secure keystore.
  • Long TTL for static data: product catalog, categories — cache for hours, invalidate via push.
// BFF endpoint — single request drives a whole screen
app.get("/mobile/home", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const [user, feed, unread, badges, newVersion] = await Promise.all([
    db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: req.user.sub }, select: { id: true, name: true, avatar: true } }),
    fetchFeed(req.user.sub, { limit: 20 }),
    db.notification.count({ where: { userId: req.user.sub, readAt: null } }),
    db.badge.findMany({ where: { userId: req.user.sub } }),
    checkAppVersion(req.headers["x-app-version"]),
  ]);

  res.set("Cache-Control", "private, max-age=30").json({
    user, feed, unreadNotifications: unread, badges,
    appUpdate: newVersion,      // hint client to show "update available"
  });
});

// delta sync — client sends last-seen cursor
app.get("/feed", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const since = req.query.since ? new Date(req.query.since) : null;
  const items = await db.post.findMany({
    where: {
      feedUserId: req.user.sub,
      ...(since && { updatedAt: { gt: since } }),
    },
    take: 50,
    orderBy: { updatedAt: "desc" },
  });
  res.json({
    data: items,
    nextSince: items[0]?.updatedAt?.toISOString(),
    hasMore: items.length === 50,
  });
});

Trade-offs: BFF adds an aggregation layer but dramatically improves mobile UX; maintain per-client BFFs (web, mobile, smart TV) if needs diverge. GraphQL excels here for field selection but adds query-cost overhead; persisted queries (send query ID, server looks up) reduce payload and improve security. App versioning matters: old app versions linger for months; never break them abruptly; instrument SDK version so you know when usage is low enough to sunset. Sync libraries (WatermelonDB, Replicache, PowerSync, Couchbase Lite) handle offline-first patterns; good for complex apps, overkill for simple ones. Battery debugging: audit background activity, sync frequency, WebSocket keepalives — users notice battery drain more than any other issue.

Question 39

How would you implement a feature that allows users to share data from your API with other users?

Situation: users want to share their documents, playlists, or photo albums with others — some recipients have accounts, some don't; permissions vary (view / comment / edit).

Approach: model shares as first-class resources; authenticated users get record-level grants, anonymous recipients get time-bound signed links.

// data model
// shares: id, resourceType, resourceId, granteeType, granteeId, permission, expiresAt, createdBy

// 1. share with registered user
app.post("/documents/:id/shares", requireAuth, loadOwnDocument, async (req, res) => {
  const { email, permission = "view" } = req.body;          // view|comment|edit
  if (!["view","comment","edit"].includes(permission)) return res.sendStatus(400);

  const grantee = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { email } });
  const share = await db.share.create({
    data: {
      resourceType: "document", resourceId: req.params.id,
      granteeType:  grantee ? "user" : "invited_email",
      granteeId:    grantee?.id ?? null,
      granteeEmail: grantee ? null : email,
      permission,
      createdBy:    req.user.sub,
    },
  });

  if (grantee) await notify(grantee.id, { type: "document_shared", resourceId: req.params.id });
  else         await sendInviteEmail(email, share.id);

  res.status(201).json(share);
});

// 2. anonymous signed share link
app.post("/documents/:id/links", requireAuth, loadOwnDocument, async (req, res) => {
  const { permission = "view", expiresIn = 7*86400 } = req.body;
  const token = crypto.randomBytes(24).toString("base64url");
  const tokenHash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(token).digest("hex");

  await db.shareLink.create({
    data: {
      resourceType: "document", resourceId: req.params.id,
      permission, tokenHash,
      expiresAt: new Date(Date.now() + expiresIn*1000),
      createdBy: req.user.sub,
    },
  });

  res.json({ url: `${APP_URL}/d/${req.params.id}?t=${token}` });
});

// 3. authorization check (used by every read/write)
async function canAccess(userId, resourceType, resourceId, required) {
  const doc = await db.document.findUnique({ where: { id: resourceId } });
  if (doc.ownerId === userId) return true;
  const share = await db.share.findFirst({
    where: { resourceType, resourceId, granteeType: "user", granteeId: userId,
             OR: [{ expiresAt: null }, { expiresAt: { gt: new Date() } }] },
  });
  return share && permissionIncludes(share.permission, required);
}

Trade-offs: record-level permissions scale to millions of grants if indexed properly ((granteeId, resourceType) + (resourceType, resourceId)). For graph-like permissions (folders containing files, nested sharing), use a relationship-based system (SpiceDB, OpenFGA, Zanzibar-style). Shareable links are risky — anyone with the URL gets access; mitigate with short TTLs, optional passwords, access logs ("this link was opened 47 times"), and revocation. Audit trail: log every share creation, access, and permission change; users expect to see "who saw what." Email invitations that pre-create accounts require careful flow so the invitee doesn't get duplicate accounts when they sign up.

Question 40

Describe how you would handle data synchronization between your API and a third-party service.

Situation: API must sync data with a third party (Salesforce CRM, QuickBooks, HubSpot) continuously — creates, updates, and deletes must flow in both directions without drift.

Approach: event-driven push when the third party supports webhooks; polling with incremental cursors when it doesn't. Use an outbox for outbound changes, idempotency for inbound.

// OUTBOUND — outbox pattern ensures we never lose a change
async function updateContact(id, data) {
  return db.$transaction(async (tx) => {
    const updated = await tx.contact.update({ where: { id }, data });
    await tx.outboxEvent.create({
      data: {
        type: "contact.updated",
        payload: { contactId: id, data },
      },
    });
    return updated;
  });
}

// worker — reads outbox, pushes to third party, marks processed
async function processOutbox() {
  const events = await db.outboxEvent.findMany({
    where: { processedAt: null }, take: 100, orderBy: { id: "asc" },
  });

  for (const event of events) {
    try {
      await salesforce.updateContact(event.payload);
      await db.outboxEvent.update({
        where: { id: event.id }, data: { processedAt: new Date() },
      });
    } catch (err) {
      if (err.retryable) continue;                  // leave for retry
      await db.outboxEvent.update({
        where: { id: event.id },
        data: { failedAt: new Date(), lastError: err.message },
      });
    }
  }
}

// INBOUND — webhook receiver
app.post("/webhooks/salesforce", verifySignature, async (req, res) => {
  const { id: eventId, type, data } = req.body;

  // idempotent — skip if we've seen this event id
  const existing = await db.inboundEvent.findUnique({ where: { providerId: eventId } });
  if (existing) return res.sendStatus(200);

  await db.$transaction([
    db.inboundEvent.create({ data: { providerId: eventId, type, payload: data } }),
    // apply to our DB based on type
  ]);

  // process async to ack webhook quickly
  await queue.add("sync.salesforce", { eventId });
  res.sendStatus(200);
});

// INBOUND fallback — polling when webhooks unavailable
async function pollSalesforce() {
  const state = await db.syncState.findUnique({ where: { key: "salesforce_contacts" } });
  const changes = await salesforce.queryChanged({ since: state?.cursor });
  for (const change of changes) {
    await applyInboundChange(change);
  }
  await db.syncState.upsert({
    where: { key: "salesforce_contacts" },
    create: { key: "salesforce_contacts", cursor: changes.nextCursor },
    update: { cursor: changes.nextCursor },
  });
}

Trade-offs: conflict resolution is the hard part — if the same record changes in both systems between syncs, which wins? Options: last-write-wins (simple, lossy); merge per-field (complex but preserves more); always-external-wins (third party is source of truth); always-internal-wins (your system is); manual review for conflicts (surface to user). Deletes are dangerous — a deleted third-party record should probably disable rather than delete your copy; irreversible operations need confirmation. Rate limits on the third-party API constrain throughput; use batch endpoints where available. Rebuilds: provide a "full resync" that fetches everything and reconciles — essential for recovering from bugs or extended outages. Libraries like Nango, Merge.dev, Integration.app abstract these patterns across many providers.

Question 41

Your API needs to support offline capabilities for mobile apps. How would you design this?

Situation: mobile app must work offline — compose messages, edit documents, browse recent data, sync when connectivity returns.

Approach: local-first architecture. Client keeps an authoritative local store; API is a sync mechanism, not the source of truth at runtime. Client replays queued mutations on reconnect; server provides delta sync.

// API surface for sync
// 1. Pull changes since last cursor
GET /v1/sync/documents?since={cursor}&limit=500
→ {
  "changes": [
    { "op": "upsert", "document": { "id": "...", "version": 42, "data": {...}, "updatedAt": "..." } },
    { "op": "delete", "id": "..." }
  ],
  "nextCursor": "opaque",
  "hasMore": false
}

// 2. Push client mutations
POST /v1/sync/mutations
  Headers: Idempotency-Key: client-generated-batch-id
  Body: {
    "mutations": [
      { "id": "m1", "op": "update", "documentId": "...", "patch": {...}, "baseVersion": 41 },
      { "id": "m2", "op": "create", "tempId": "temp_xyz", "data": {...} }
    ]
  }
→ {
  "results": [
    { "mutationId": "m1", "status": "applied", "newVersion": 43 },
    { "mutationId": "m2", "status": "applied", "serverId": "doc_abc123" }
  ]
}

Server-side implementation:

app.post("/v1/sync/mutations", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const idempKey = req.headers["idempotency-key"];
  const existing = await redis.get(`idem:${idempKey}`);
  if (existing) return res.json(JSON.parse(existing));

  const results = [];
  for (const m of req.body.mutations) {
    if (m.op === "update") {
      // optimistic concurrency — version must match
      const doc = await db.document.updateMany({
        where: { id: m.documentId, version: m.baseVersion, ownerId: req.user.sub },
        data:  { ...applyPatch(m.patch), version: { increment: 1 } },
      });
      results.push(doc.count === 1
        ? { mutationId: m.id, status: "applied", newVersion: m.baseVersion + 1 }
        : { mutationId: m.id, status: "conflict", serverVersion: await getVersion(m.documentId) });
    } else if (m.op === "create") {
      const doc = await db.document.create({ data: { ...m.data, ownerId: req.user.sub } });
      results.push({ mutationId: m.id, status: "applied", serverId: doc.id, tempId: m.tempId });
    }
  }
  const response = { results };
  await redis.setex(`idem:${idempKey}`, 86400, JSON.stringify(response));
  res.json(response);
});

Trade-offs: conflict resolution is the fundamental offline problem. Options: optimistic concurrency (reject if version changed — simple, user reconciles); operational transformation (OT — Google Docs style, complex); CRDTs (Yjs, Automerge — mathematically converge, excellent for collaborative editing, larger payload). For form-like apps, optimistic concurrency is enough; for real-time collaboration, CRDTs. TempIDs — offline-created records get client-generated IDs; the sync response maps them to server IDs; client updates local references. Delta payload size: since= cursor returns only changes; for first sync, provide a paginated snapshot. Libraries: PowerSync (backed by Postgres), Replicache, WatermelonDB, Dexie Cloud, Convex — handle sync protocol for you. Hard problem; buy when possible.

Question 42

How would you implement a custom error handling mechanism to provide meaningful feedback to API consumers?

Situation: API error responses are inconsistent across endpoints — some return {error: "string"}, some return status 200 with error body, some leak stack traces. Consumers struggle to integrate.

Approach: centralize error handling with RFC 7807 Problem Details shape; category-based HTTP status codes; stable machine-readable codes; human-readable messages; request trace IDs.

// error hierarchy — all errors inherit from ApiError
export class ApiError extends Error {
  constructor({ status, code, title, detail, issues, headers }) {
    super(detail || title);
    Object.assign(this, { status, code, title, detail, issues, headers });
  }
}

export class ValidationError extends ApiError {
  constructor(issues) {
    super({
      status: 422, code: "validation_failed",
      title: "Request validation failed",
      detail: "One or more fields are invalid",
      issues,
    });
  }
}

export class NotFoundError extends ApiError {
  constructor(resource) {
    super({ status: 404, code: "not_found", title: `${resource} not found` });
  }
}

export class RateLimitError extends ApiError {
  constructor(retryAfter) {
    super({
      status: 429, code: "rate_limit_exceeded",
      title: "Rate limit exceeded",
      headers: { "Retry-After": String(retryAfter) },
    });
  }
}

// central error middleware — last in the chain
app.use((err, req, res, next) => {
  const reqId = req.id;                           // set by request-id middleware

  if (err instanceof ApiError) {
    if (err.headers) for (const [k, v] of Object.entries(err.headers)) res.set(k, v);
    return res.status(err.status).type("application/problem+json").json({
      type:     `https://api.example.com/problems/${err.code}`,
      title:    err.title,
      status:   err.status,
      detail:   err.detail,
      instance: req.originalUrl,
      code:     err.code,
      issues:   err.issues,
      traceId:  reqId,
    });
  }

  // unknown error — never leak internals
  log.error({ err, reqId, path: req.path }, "Unhandled error");
  res.status(500).type("application/problem+json").json({
    type:     "https://api.example.com/problems/internal_error",
    title:    "An unexpected error occurred",
    status:   500,
    traceId:  reqId,
  });
});

// usage
app.get("/users/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!user) throw new NotFoundError("User");
  res.json(user);
});

Trade-offs: RFC 7807 is the standard shape; one-off shapes create friction for SDK authors. Machine-readable codes (validation_failed) must be stable across versions — clients switch on them; changing breaks integrations. Human messages can and should be tuned for clarity; translate per Accept-Language if you serve international. Don't leak internals — stack traces, SQL errors, filesystem paths all belong in logs (indexed by traceId), not responses. Field-level validation errors let forms highlight specific inputs: issues: [{ field: "email", code: "required", message: "Email is required" }]. Retry signals: Retry-After on 429/503; RateLimit-* headers on 429 per RFC 9331. A well-designed error system pays for itself in reduced support tickets and faster integration.

Question 43

Describe how you would use API documentation tools like Swagger to create comprehensive documentation for your API.

Situation: developers need to discover, understand, and integrate with the API without Slack conversations. Need comprehensive, always-current documentation.

Approach: spec-first (OpenAPI 3.1) as the source of truth; generate docs, SDKs, and tests from the same spec; include examples, errors, rate limits, and a try-it console.

# openapi.yaml excerpt
openapi: 3.1.0
info:
  title: Example API
  version: "2.0.0"
  description: |
    The Example API lets you manage users, orders, and payments.
    ## Authentication
    Use `Authorization: Bearer <token>`. Obtain tokens via [OAuth 2.0](/docs/auth).
    ## Rate limits
    1000 requests/min per API key.

servers:
  - url: https://api.example.com/v2
    description: Production
  - url: https://sandbox.api.example.com/v2
    description: Sandbox (no real charges)

paths:
  /orders:
    post:
      summary: Create order
      operationId: createOrder
      tags: [Orders]
      security:
        - bearerAuth: [orders:write]
      requestBody:
        required: true
        content:
          application/json:
            schema:
              $ref: '#/components/schemas/CreateOrderRequest'
            example:
              items: [{ sku: "SKU-123", qty: 2 }]
              shippingAddressId: "addr_abc"
      responses:
        '201':
          description: Order created
          content:
            application/json:
              schema:
                $ref: '#/components/schemas/Order'
        '422':
          $ref: '#/components/responses/ValidationError'
        '429':
          $ref: '#/components/responses/RateLimited'

components:
  responses:
    ValidationError:
      description: Request body failed validation
      content:
        application/problem+json:
          schema:
            $ref: '#/components/schemas/Problem'
  securitySchemes:
    bearerAuth:
      type: http
      scheme: bearer
      bearerFormat: JWT

Toolchain:

  • Render docs: Redoc, Scalar, Stoplight Elements, Swagger UI, or docusaurus + openapi-docusaurus.
  • Generate SDKs: openapi-generator, oapi-codegen (Go), orval (TypeScript) produce client libraries.
  • Mock server: Prism, Microcks serve spec-based responses for frontend development.
  • Contract testing: Schemathesis, Dredd verify the real server matches the spec.
  • Lint: Spectral catches style violations in CI.
  • Breaking-change detection: oasdiff on every PR.
  • Try-it console: Stoplight Elements lets users execute calls against sandbox directly from docs.

Trade-offs: spec-first vs code-first: spec-first (write spec, generate server stub) forces design before implementation but requires team buy-in; code-first (decorators like @Get("/users") in Nest/FastAPI/tsoa) is easier but docs drift from reality if decorators lag. Quality matters: Stripe's docs are a competitive moat; most APIs underinvest. Include request/response examples, error scenarios, guides ("How to integrate payments in 10 minutes"), and changelogs. Generate SDKs even if you have few customers — integration time drops 5-10x when developers don't write HTTP clients themselves. Beyond OpenAPI: AsyncAPI for events, GraphQL has built-in introspection, Protobuf generates gRPC docs. The common thread: one source of truth, generate everything else.

Question 44

Your API must integrate with multiple external services. How would you manage and coordinate these integrations?

Situation: order flow must call inventory, pricing, payment, shipping, and email services — each with different auth, rate limits, availability. One slow dependency causes cascade failures.

Approach: facade service that encapsulates integration concerns; circuit breakers, timeouts, retries, caching; asynchronous where possible; graceful degradation.

// facade client per integration — normalized interface
import CircuitBreaker from "opossum";

class InventoryClient {
  constructor() {
    this.breaker = new CircuitBreaker(
      async (skus) => this._fetch(skus),
      {
        timeout: 2000,
        errorThresholdPercentage: 50,
        resetTimeout: 10_000,
        rollingCountTimeout: 60_000,
      },
    );
    this.breaker.fallback(async (skus) => {
      // degrade: return cached availability from last successful call
      return (await this._fromCache(skus)) ?? skus.map((s) => ({ sku: s, available: null, stale: true }));
    });
  }

  async _fetch(skus) {
    const res = await fetch(`${INV_URL}/stock?skus=${skus.join(",")}`, {
      headers: { "Authorization": `Bearer ${await getToken("inventory")}` },
      signal: AbortSignal.timeout(2000),
    });
    if (!res.ok) throw new Error(`Inventory ${res.status}`);
    const data = await res.json();
    await this._cacheWrite(data);
    return data;
  }

  checkStock(skus) { return this.breaker.fire(skus); }
}

// orchestrator — uses facades
app.post("/orders", requireAuth, async (req, res, next) => {
  try {
    const { items, shippingAddressId } = req.body;

    // parallel read — all independent
    const [stock, prices, address] = await Promise.all([
      inventoryClient.checkStock(items.map((i) => i.sku)),
      pricingClient.getPrices(items.map((i) => i.sku)),
      addressClient.getAddress(shippingAddressId),
    ]);

    // validate before charging
    const outOfStock = items.filter((i) => {
      const s = stock.find((x) => x.sku === i.sku);
      return !s || (s.available !== null && s.available < i.qty);
    });
    if (outOfStock.length) throw new ApiError({ status: 409, code: "out_of_stock", issues: outOfStock });

    // charge — must succeed before committing order
    const charge = await paymentClient.charge({ amount: total(items, prices), userId: req.user.sub });

    // persist
    const order = await db.order.create({ data: { ... } });

    // async — don't block response on shipping/email
    await queue.add("order.created", { orderId: order.id });

    res.status(201).location(`/orders/${order.id}`).json(order);
  } catch (err) { next(err); }
});

Trade-offs: synchronous vs asynchronous: for the happy path, the user waits — minimize by running parallel calls, caching recent data, and deferring non-critical work (email, webhook delivery) to background queues. Circuit breakers prevent cascade failures: one slow service doesn't bring down yours. Timeouts at every layer are non-negotiable — request, connection, read, integration-specific. Retries with jitter + idempotency: retry transient failures, but only on idempotent operations (POST with Idempotency-Key). Observability: every external call gets its own trace span; dashboards per integration show p95/99 latency and error rate. When a third party degrades, you see it before customers do. For complex orchestrations, consider workflow engines (Temporal, Inngest, AWS Step Functions) — they encode long-running sequences as durable code surviving crashes.

Question 45

How would you implement a retry mechanism for failed API requests to improve reliability?

Situation: network blips, upstream hiccups, and rate limits cause API calls to fail intermittently. Need retries that recover without amplifying load during outages.

Approach: exponential backoff with jitter; retry only transient failures; bounded attempts; respect Retry-After; idempotency-safe.

import { setTimeout as sleep } from "node:timers/promises";

// retry only for these — 4xx (except 429/408) are client errors, won't self-heal
const RETRYABLE_STATUS = new Set([408, 429, 500, 502, 503, 504]);
const RETRYABLE_ERRORS = new Set(["ECONNRESET", "ETIMEDOUT", "ENOTFOUND", "EAI_AGAIN"]);

export async function fetchWithRetry(url, options = {}) {
  const { retries = 3, baseDelayMs = 200, maxDelayMs = 10_000, timeoutMs = 5000 } = options;

  let lastErr;
  for (let attempt = 0; attempt <= retries; attempt++) {
    try {
      const res = await fetch(url, {
        ...options,
        signal: AbortSignal.timeout(timeoutMs),
      });

      if (res.ok) return res;

      // 4xx non-retryable — fail fast
      if (!RETRYABLE_STATUS.has(res.status)) return res;

      // RESPECT Retry-After header
      const retryAfter = res.headers.get("Retry-After");
      if (retryAfter && attempt < retries) {
        const delay = isNaN(retryAfter)
          ? Math.max(0, Date.parse(retryAfter) - Date.now())
          : Number(retryAfter) * 1000;
        await sleep(Math.min(delay, maxDelayMs));
        continue;
      }

      lastErr = new Error(`HTTP ${res.status}`);
    } catch (err) {
      if (!RETRYABLE_ERRORS.has(err.code) && err.name !== "TimeoutError") throw err;
      lastErr = err;
    }

    // final attempt already used → throw
    if (attempt === retries) break;

    // full jitter — uniform random 0..backoff
    const backoff = Math.min(maxDelayMs, baseDelayMs * 2 ** attempt);
    const delay   = Math.random() * backoff;
    await sleep(delay);
  }

  throw lastErr;
}

// usage with idempotency
const result = await fetchWithRetry("https://api.stripe.com/v1/charges", {
  method: "POST",
  headers: {
    "Authorization":   `Bearer ${STRIPE_KEY}`,
    "Idempotency-Key": `charge_${orderId}`,       // same key → same result even if retried
    "Content-Type":    "application/x-www-form-urlencoded",
  },
  body: new URLSearchParams({ amount: "2000", currency: "usd", source: "tok_visa" }),
  retries: 4,
});

Trade-offs: Idempotency is non-negotiable for POST retries. Without it, retrying a charge can charge the customer twice. Every write endpoint that's retried must accept an Idempotency-Key header and deduplicate. Full jitter vs exponential: "exponential backoff" alone causes thundering herds during outages — when 10,000 clients all retry at 1s, 2s, 4s, they hammer simultaneously. Jitter spreads them out. Retry budget: Netflix's Concurrency Limits and Envoy's outlier detection cap the percentage of requests that can be retries — prevents retries from multiplying load during brownouts. Don't retry too long: user-facing requests have budget ≤ a few seconds; retrying 30 times over 60s is worse than failing fast. Background jobs have more room but still bounded. Libraries: got, undici, axios-retry, cockatiel implement these patterns well.

Question 46

Describe how you would use an API proxy to enhance security and manage traffic for your API.

Situation: want to add features (rate limiting, auth, logging, rewriting, analytics, A/B testing) to an existing API without modifying every service. Proxy it.

Approach: API gateway (Kong, Envoy, Tyk, AWS API Gateway, Azure APIM) or reverse proxy (Nginx, Traefik) in front of your services. Centralizes cross-cutting concerns; services stay focused on business logic.

# Kong declarative config
_format_version: "3.0"
services:
  - name: orders
    url: http://orders-svc:8080
    plugins:
      - name: jwt                                   # validates JWT before forwarding
        config:
          key_claim_name: kid
          secret_is_base64: false
      - name: rate-limiting
        config:
          minute: 600
          policy: redis
          redis_host: redis.internal
      - name: request-transformer
        config:
          add:
            headers: ["X-Gateway-Version: 2"]
      - name: correlation-id                        # adds X-Request-Id if missing
      - name: prometheus                            # exposes metrics
    routes:
      - name: orders-api
        paths: ["/v2/orders"]
        strip_path: false
        methods: [GET, POST, PATCH, DELETE]

Capabilities the proxy provides:

  • Authentication offload: JWT validated once at the edge; services receive X-User-Id header they can trust.
  • Rate limiting & throttling centralized — one configuration per service.
  • Request/response transformation: strip internal headers, canonicalize paths, translate error formats.
  • Caching: GET responses cached at the edge with purge-on-write via surrogate keys.
  • Load balancing & health checks: automatic failover, circuit breaking per upstream.
  • TLS termination: certificate management in one place; backend services speak plain HTTP inside the mesh.
  • Observability: consistent access logs, tracing, metrics across all services.
  • WAF / bot protection: block common attacks and automated abuse.
  • Canary / A/B routing: "send 5% of traffic to v2" based on header or user ID hash.
  • Mock / override: serve test responses for specific clients without deploying changes.
// Node-based reverse proxy using Fastify + http-proxy (when a gateway is overkill)
import Fastify from "fastify";
import httpProxy from "@fastify/http-proxy";

const app = Fastify({ logger: true });

app.register(httpProxy, {
  upstream:     "http://orders-svc:8080",
  prefix:       "/orders",
  replyOptions: { rewriteRequestHeaders: (req, headers) => ({
    ...headers,
    "x-user-id": req.user?.sub,                    // trusted: set by our auth middleware
    "x-request-id": req.id,
  }) },
});

Trade-offs: extra hop adds 1-5ms latency; mitigated by co-locating gateway with services. Single point of failure — run multiple gateway instances behind a load balancer. Configuration complexity grows with many routes; manage as code (GitOps). Service mesh vs gateway: gateway handles north-south (client → service); service mesh (Istio, Linkerd) handles east-west (service → service). Both are proxies; they solve different problems. For public APIs, a gateway is standard; for internal microservices, a mesh is increasingly common. Don't over-abstract: business logic belongs in services; gateway handles transport and security. Keep plugins generic.

Question 47

Your API needs to support transactional operations. How would you design this to ensure data consistency?

Situation: a single API call must perform multiple operations atomically — create an order, reserve inventory, charge payment, send confirmation. If any step fails, partial changes mustn't persist.

Approach: for single-DB operations, use a transaction; for multi-service flows, use the saga pattern with compensating actions or workflow engine.

// single-DB transactional endpoint (easy case)
app.post("/transfers", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { fromAccountId, toAccountId, amount } = req.body;

  const result = await db.$transaction(async (tx) => {
    const from = await tx.account.findUnique({ where: { id: fromAccountId } });
    if (from.ownerId !== req.user.sub) throw new ApiError({ status: 403, code: "forbidden" });
    if (from.balance < amount) throw new ApiError({ status: 409, code: "insufficient_funds" });

    await tx.account.update({
      where: { id: fromAccountId }, data: { balance: { decrement: amount } },
    });
    await tx.account.update({
      where: { id: toAccountId }, data: { balance: { increment: amount } },
    });
    const transfer = await tx.transfer.create({
      data: { fromAccountId, toAccountId, amount, status: "completed" },
    });
    return transfer;
  }, { isolationLevel: "Serializable" });

  res.status(201).json(result);
});

// multi-service saga — orchestrated, with compensations
async function createOrderSaga(userId, items) {
  const state = { userId, items, completed: [] };
  try {
    state.reservation = await inventory.reserve({ items });
    state.completed.push("reservation");

    state.charge = await payment.charge({ userId, amount: total(items) });
    state.completed.push("charge");

    state.order = await orders.create({ userId, items, chargeId: state.charge.id });
    state.completed.push("order");

    state.shipment = await shipping.schedule({ orderId: state.order.id });
    state.completed.push("shipment");

    return state.order;
  } catch (err) {
    // compensate in REVERSE order
    if (state.completed.includes("shipment")) await shipping.cancel(state.shipment.id);
    if (state.completed.includes("order"))    await orders.cancel(state.order.id);
    if (state.completed.includes("charge"))   await payment.refund(state.charge.id);
    if (state.completed.includes("reservation")) await inventory.release(state.reservation.id);
    throw err;
  }
}

Trade-offs: DB transactions give strong ACID guarantees but only within one DB. Across microservices, distributed transactions (2PC) are technically possible but rarely used — they reduce availability and don't fit horizontally-scaled systems. Sagas are the 2026 standard: each step has a compensating action; on failure, compensations run backward. Orchestrated (central coordinator) is simpler to reason about; choreographed (each service publishes events and subscribes to others) scales better but is harder to debug. Workflow engines (Temporal, Cadence, AWS Step Functions, Inngest) encode sagas as durable code that survives crashes — the compensation logic is in one place. Eventual consistency is the price: between charge and order creation, you've taken money but the order isn't visible; compensation fires if the order fails. Document this clearly to users ("Your payment is processing"). For simple reversible flows, manual compensation is fine; for complex orchestrations (60-step loan approval), use Temporal.

Question 48

How would you implement a custom rate limiting strategy to prioritize certain users or endpoints?

Situation: premium users shouldn't hit the same rate limits as free users; certain expensive endpoints (search, exports) need tighter limits than read endpoints. Need composable, tier-aware rate limiting.

Approach: layered limiters — per-user, per-endpoint, per-resource — keyed by tier in Redis with atomic Lua checks.

// tier configuration — loaded from DB/cache per request
const TIERS = {
  free:       { perMin: 60,    perDay: 10_000,    costMultiplier: 1 },
  pro:        { perMin: 600,   perDay: 500_000,   costMultiplier: 1 },
  enterprise: { perMin: 6000,  perDay: Infinity,  costMultiplier: 1 },
};

const ENDPOINT_COST = {
  "GET /users":      1,
  "GET /search":     10,      // expensive
  "POST /exports":   100,     // very expensive
  "POST /ai/chat":   50,      // charges compute
};

// atomic multi-limit check
const LUA_MULTI = `
  local now = tonumber(ARGV[1])
  for i=1,#KEYS do
    local key   = KEYS[i]
    local limit = tonumber(ARGV[i*3 - 1])
    local ttl   = tonumber(ARGV[i*3])
    local cost  = tonumber(ARGV[i*3 + 1])
    local cur   = redis.call('INCRBY', key, cost)
    if cur == cost then redis.call('EXPIRE', key, ttl) end
    if cur > limit then
      return { 0, i, cur - cost, limit }          -- denied, layer, current, limit
    end
  end
  return { 1 }
`;

export function tieredRateLimit() {
  return async (req, res, next) => {
    const tier = req.user?.plan ?? "free";
    const cfg  = TIERS[tier];
    const endpointKey = `${req.method} ${req.route?.path || req.path}`;
    const cost = (ENDPOINT_COST[endpointKey] ?? 1) * cfg.costMultiplier;

    const userId = req.user?.sub ?? req.ip;
    const keys = [
      `rl:user:${userId}:min:${Math.floor(Date.now()/60_000)}`,
      `rl:user:${userId}:day:${new Date().toISOString().slice(0,10)}`,
    ];
    const args = [
      String(Date.now()),
      cfg.perMin, 60, cost,
      cfg.perDay, 86400, cost,
    ];

    const result = await redis.eval(LUA_MULTI, keys.length, ...keys, ...args);
    if (result[0] === 0) {
      const [, layer, current, limit] = result;
      res.set({
        "RateLimit-Limit":     String(limit),
        "RateLimit-Remaining": "0",
        "Retry-After":         layer === 1 ? "60" : "3600",
      });
      return res.status(429).json({
        error: "rate_limit_exceeded",
        layer: layer === 1 ? "per-minute" : "per-day",
        tier, cost,
      });
    }
    next();
  };
}

Trade-offs: cost-weighted (expensive endpoints charge more) is more fair than equal-cost — GitHub GraphQL uses this pattern to prevent deeply-nested queries from exhausting quotas. Observability: emit metrics with tier + endpoint dimensions so you see who's hitting limits; upsell opportunities appear naturally. Burst allowance: token bucket (not sliding window) lets users send short bursts — customers love this and it doesn't compromise overall throughput. Admin bypass: staff accounts skip limits; internal traffic is subject to its own limits (not unlimited) so bugs don't DDoS. Grace during upgrade: when a user upgrades tier, reset or raise their current-window count; nobody enjoys paying $99/month and still getting 429 for the next hour. Transparent limits in headers, docs, and dashboards.

Question 49

Describe how you would handle schema changes in your API without breaking existing clients.

Situation: schema needs to evolve — new required field, renamed resource, type change — without breaking existing clients still on old versions.

Approach: expand-and-contract pattern. Keep old and new shapes in parallel long enough for clients to migrate; remove old only when usage drops to zero.

PhaseServerClients
1. ExpandAccept both shapes on write; return both fields on readMix of old and new in use
2. MigrateBoth shapes still supported; deprecation headers on old pathsNew clients use new; old migrate gradually
3. MeasureTrack usage of old shape via request logsUsage should approach zero
4. ContractRemove old shapeNo clients affected (verified by logs)
// EXAMPLE — renaming "fullName" → split into "firstName"/"lastName"

// PHASE 1: expand — accept both, return both
app.post("/users", validate({ body: UserCreateSchema }), async (req, res) => {
  const { fullName, firstName, lastName, ...rest } = req.body;

  // coerce whichever shape was sent
  const first = firstName ?? (fullName?.split(" ")[0] ?? "");
  const last  = lastName  ?? (fullName?.split(" ").slice(1).join(" ") ?? "");

  const user = await db.user.create({ data: { firstName: first, lastName: last, ...rest } });
  res.status(201).json(serializeWithBoth(user));
});

function serializeWithBoth(user) {
  return {
    id: user.id,
    firstName: user.firstName,
    lastName:  user.lastName,
    fullName:  `${user.firstName} ${user.lastName}`.trim(),    // keep for old clients
    email:     user.email,
  };
}

// PHASE 2: add deprecation signals
app.use("/v1/users", (req, res, next) => {
  res.set({
    "Deprecation": "true",
    "Sunset":      "Sun, 30 Jun 2026 23:59:59 GMT",
    "Link":        "<https://docs/migration/v2>; rel=\"deprecation\"",
  });
  next();
});

// PHASE 3: measure per-client usage
logger.info({
  endpoint: req.path, client: req.headers["x-client-id"], version: apiVersion,
  usedFullName: "fullName" in req.body,
}, "api.request");

// PHASE 4: contract (only after usage is zero for weeks)
// Remove the fullName handling from request parsing and response serialization.

Trade-offs: the parallel phase is long — typically 6-12 months for mobile clients (slow update cycles), 1-3 months for web, 3-24 months for enterprise partners. Budget for the maintenance burden: every bug fix during this window may need to work in both shapes. Deprecation comms: Deprecation and Sunset headers are machine-readable, but also email customers, write changelog entries, post blog. Track-and-nudge: identify top 10 clients still using old shape; contact directly. Database migrations are a separate concern — backward-compat storage (both old and new columns, or computed columns) until application fully cuts over; expand-migrate-contract at the DB level too. Alternative for mass breaks: version bump (v2) is clearer than many simultaneous expand-and-contracts — fewer parallel shapes to maintain. Balance: small changes via expand-and-contract; large redesigns via new version.

Question 50

Your API needs to provide data in real-time to a dashboard. How would you implement this?

Situation: internal dashboard needs to display real-time metrics (order rate, active users, revenue) as they happen. Polling every 5s isn't real-time enough and overloads the DB.

Approach: server publishes metrics to a stream (Redis, Kafka); dashboard subscribes via SSE or WebSocket; metrics come from events, not DB polling.

// architecture
// [business events] → [aggregator / windowing] → [Redis channel] → [SSE endpoint] → [dashboard]

// 1. emit metrics from domain events (outbox-style)
async function recordOrder(order) {
  await db.order.create({ data: order });
  await redis.xadd("events:orders", "*", "type", "created", "amount", order.total);
}

// 2. aggregator worker — windowed stats in Redis
async function aggregateLoop() {
  const lastId = "$";                                           // start at current
  while (true) {
    const streams = await redis.xread("BLOCK", 5000, "COUNT", 100, "STREAMS", "events:orders", lastId);
    if (!streams) continue;

    for (const [, events] of streams) {
      for (const [id, fields] of events) {
        const amount = Number(fields.find((_, i) => i % 2 === 0 && fields[i] === "amount") && fields[fields.indexOf("amount") + 1]);
        // rolling 1-min stats
        const minuteKey = `stats:ordersPerMin:${Math.floor(Date.now()/60_000)}`;
        await redis.multi()
          .hincrby(minuteKey, "count", 1)
          .hincrbyfloat(minuteKey, "revenue", amount)
          .expire(minuteKey, 300)
          .exec();
      }
    }

    // publish snapshot to subscribers
    const now = Math.floor(Date.now()/60_000);
    const stats = await redis.hgetall(`stats:ordersPerMin:${now}`);
    await redis.publish("dashboard:stats", JSON.stringify({ time: now, ...stats }));
  }
}

// 3. SSE endpoint — dashboard connects
app.get("/dashboard/stream", requireAdmin, async (req, res) => {
  res.set({
    "Content-Type":      "text/event-stream",
    "Cache-Control":     "no-store",
    "X-Accel-Buffering": "no",
  }).flushHeaders();

  // send current snapshot
  const now = Math.floor(Date.now()/60_000);
  const current = await redis.hgetall(`stats:ordersPerMin:${now}`);
  res.write(`event: snapshot\ndata: ${JSON.stringify(current)}\n\n`);

  // live updates
  const sub = redis.duplicate();
  await sub.subscribe("dashboard:stats");
  sub.on("message", (_c, payload) => res.write(`event: update\ndata: ${payload}\n\n`));

  const heart = setInterval(() => res.write(":heartbeat\n\n"), 20_000);
  req.on("close", () => { clearInterval(heart); sub.unsubscribe(); sub.quit(); res.end(); });
});

Trade-offs: event-driven is cheaper and fresher than polling: one DB write per event, one Redis publish, unlimited subscribers. Polling scales badly — 100 concurrent dashboards × 5s polls = 20 DB queries/sec for the same data. Windowed aggregation: rolling 1-min/5-min stats via Redis HINCRBY (O(1) per event) rather than SELECT COUNT(*) GROUP BY on a large table. Late events: if events arrive out of order (common with distributed systems), use tumbling windows with acceptance lag, or watermarks (Apache Flink pattern). Backfilling: dashboard shows last N minutes on connect; fetch from Redis or TimescaleDB/InfluxDB. Scale: single-instance Redis pub/sub handles 10k subscribers easily; for massive fan-out, use dedicated platforms (Ably, Pusher, Tinybird). For complex analytics: stream into ClickHouse or Tinybird — materialized views give real-time aggregates with SQL. For BI dashboards, Metabase and Grafana have live-data plugins.

Question 51

How would you ensure that your API is compliant with data privacy regulations like GDPR and CCPA?

Situation: API handles EU and California user data; GDPR, CCPA, (and for health data, HIPAA) impose legal requirements: lawful basis for processing, data subject rights, retention limits, breach notification, international transfers.

Approach: data minimization + encryption + auditability + automated rights tooling. Treat compliance as an architecture property, not a checkbox.

  1. Data inventory & classification: every field tagged (public / internal / PII / sensitive). Data catalog (DataHub, Atlan, OpenMetadata) documents flow and owners.
  2. Consent & lawful basis: endpoint captures user consent per purpose (marketing, analytics, profiling); timestamps and version the policy text accepted.
  3. Data subject rights endpoints:
// GDPR Art. 15 — right of access / data portability
app.get("/v1/me/export", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  // expensive: return 202 + job, worker writes JSON to S3 signed URL, emails link
