  const job = await queue.add("gdpr.export", { userId: req.user.sub });
  res.status(202).json({ jobId: job.id, message: "Download link will be emailed within 24h" });
});

// GDPR Art. 17 — right to erasure
app.post("/v1/me/delete", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  await db.deletionRequest.create({
    data: {
      userId: req.user.sub, requestedAt: new Date(),
      scheduledFor: new Date(Date.now() + 30*86400_000),    // 30-day grace
    },
  });
  await db.user.update({ where: { id: req.user.sub }, data: { disabled: true } });
  res.json({ deletionScheduled: true });
});

// CCPA — do-not-sell; GDPR — withdraw consent
app.post("/v1/me/preferences", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { marketing, analytics, profileSharing } = req.body;
  await db.userConsent.upsert({
    where: { userId: req.user.sub },
    create: { userId: req.user.sub, marketing, analytics, profileSharing, acceptedAt: new Date() },
    update: { marketing, analytics, profileSharing, updatedAt: new Date() },
  });
  res.json({ ok: true });
});
  1. Encryption: TLS 1.2+; at-rest encryption via KMS; column-level encryption for highly sensitive fields (SSN, health records).
  2. Tokenization for PCI data (Stripe/Adyen); API stores tok_xxx, never raw card data.
  3. Audit log: every PII access logged with actor, purpose, record ID; immutable retention for forensics.
  4. Retention & deletion automation: scheduled jobs enforce retention windows; soft-deleted records hard-deleted after legal hold expires.
  5. DPAs with every sub-processor; Schrems II analysis for US transfers; Standard Contractual Clauses where applicable.
  6. Breach runbook: predefined escalation, 72-hour notification rehearsed quarterly.

Trade-offs: "right to erasure" vs business records: orders must persist for tax/accounting (7 years); anonymize by nulling userId and replacing PII with tombstone. Backups are the hardest part: full deletion from point-in-time backups may take 30-90 days; document this explicitly in the privacy policy. Analytics & ML: aggregated stats are fine; training on raw PII requires explicit consent and must be re-trainable without that user. Data residency: EU data in EU regions (especially after Schrems II); geo-partition user data. Emerging laws (India DPDPA, Brazil LGPD, Virginia CDPA) are broadly GDPR-like — one good implementation satisfies many. Compliance is continuous: DPO reviews, quarterly privacy audits, incident drills.

Question 52

Describe how you would implement a feature that allows users to export their data from your API.

Situation: GDPR data portability requires users to export their data in a commonly-used machine-readable format. Customers also request this for "I'm leaving, I want my data."

Approach: async export job; aggregate from all relevant tables and services; deliver via signed URL; include enough context for import elsewhere.

// export endpoint — returns 202 + job ID
app.post("/v1/me/exports", requireAuth, rateLimit({ max: 3, windowMs: 86400_000 }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const job = await db.exportJob.create({
      data: {
        userId:    req.user.sub,
        status:    "queued",
        format:    req.body.format ?? "json",     // json | csv | tar
        requestedAt: new Date(),
      },
    });
    await queue.add("user.export", { jobId: job.id });
    res.status(202).location(`/v1/me/exports/${job.id}`).json(job);
  }
);

// poll job status
app.get("/v1/me/exports/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const job = await db.exportJob.findFirst({
    where: { id: req.params.id, userId: req.user.sub },
  });
  if (!job) return res.sendStatus(404);
  res.json(job);
});

// worker
queue.process("user.export", async (job) => {
  const { jobId } = job.data;
  const record = await db.exportJob.findUnique({ where: { id: jobId } });
  await db.exportJob.update({ where: { id: jobId }, data: { status: "processing" } });

  const userId = record.userId;
  // stream to a tar — huge users may have GB of data
  const archive = archiver("tar", { gzip: true });
  const key = `exports/${userId}/${jobId}.tar.gz`;
  const upload = new Upload({
    client: s3, params: { Bucket: EXPORT_BUCKET, Key: key, Body: archive, ServerSideEncryption: "AES256" },
  });

  // user profile
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: userId } });
  archive.append(JSON.stringify(user, null, 2), { name: "profile.json" });

  // posts — stream to avoid memory blow
  archive.append(stringifyStream(streamPosts(userId)), { name: "posts.jsonl" });

  // orders, messages, settings, media file references...
  for (const table of ["orders", "messages", "settings"]) {
    archive.append(stringifyStream(streamTable(table, userId)), { name: `${table}.jsonl` });
  }
  archive.append(JSON.stringify(makeIndex(userId)), { name: "index.json" });

  archive.finalize();
  await upload.done();

  const downloadUrl = await getSignedUrl(s3,
    new GetObjectCommand({ Bucket: EXPORT_BUCKET, Key: key }),
    { expiresIn: 7*86400 });                    // 7-day link

  await db.exportJob.update({
    where: { id: jobId },
    data: { status: "ready", downloadUrl, completedAt: new Date() },
  });

  await sendEmail(user.email, "Your data export is ready", downloadUrl);
});

Trade-offs: async is essential — a large user may have gigabytes of data; synchronous export times out. Format: JSON is universal but large; CSV per table suits spreadsheet users; the combination (tar.gz with per-entity JSONL + index) works best. Include a README describing the schema and field meanings. Rate limit exports — 1-3/day is plenty and prevents abuse. Include media URLs (signed, long-lived) rather than embedding GB of blobs; user can download separately. Cross-tenant leakage: one user's export should never include another user's data — carefully filter every query. Audit: log every export with who, when, which data; useful for security investigations. Scheduled expiry on the S3 bucket (30-90 day lifecycle rule) prevents exports accumulating forever.

Question 53

Your API needs to support complex queries with multiple filters and aggregations. How would you design this?

Situation: users want to slice data across many dimensions — "orders from EU customers with status=shipped, created this quarter, total > $100, grouped by product category, sorted by count desc." The endpoint must handle arbitrary combinations without SQL injection or DoS.

Approach: structured query DSL (validated); build Prisma/SQL query from allowlisted fields; execute with a hard timeout; paginate.

import { z } from "zod";

const FILTER_FIELDS = {
  status:     { type: "enum",   values: ["pending","shipped","delivered","cancelled"] },
  country:    { type: "string", maxLen: 2 },
  createdAt:  { type: "date" },
  total:      { type: "number" },
  categoryId: { type: "string" },
};
const AGG_FIELDS = ["categoryId", "country", "status", "day", "month"];
const METRIC_FIELDS = ["count", "total.sum", "total.avg", "total.min", "total.max"];

const OperatorSchema = z.enum(["eq","ne","in","gt","gte","lt","lte","between"]);

const QuerySchema = z.object({
  filters: z.array(z.object({
    field: z.string(),
    op:    OperatorSchema,
    value: z.any(),
  })).max(20).optional(),
  groupBy: z.array(z.string()).max(3).optional(),
  metrics: z.array(z.string()).max(5).optional(),
  sort:    z.object({ field: z.string(), dir: z.enum(["asc","desc"]).default("desc") }).optional(),
  limit:   z.number().int().min(1).max(1000).default(100),
  cursor:  z.string().optional(),
}).strict();

app.post("/orders/query", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const parsed = QuerySchema.safeParse(req.body);
  if (!parsed.success) return res.status(400).json({ error: parsed.error.issues });

  const q = parsed.data;

  // validate filter fields/operators/types
  for (const f of q.filters ?? []) {
    if (!FILTER_FIELDS[f.field]) return res.status(400).json({ error: `Unknown filter: ${f.field}` });
    // type check value per field meta
  }
  for (const g of q.groupBy ?? []) if (!AGG_FIELDS.includes(g)) return res.status(400).json({ error: "bad groupBy" });
  for (const m of q.metrics ?? []) if (!METRIC_FIELDS.includes(m)) return res.status(400).json({ error: "bad metric" });

  // build SQL safely — parameterized, with tenant isolation
  const sql = buildSafeAggregationQuery({
    table: "orders",
    filters: [...(q.filters ?? []), { field: "tenantId", op: "eq", value: req.user.tenantId }],
    groupBy: q.groupBy,
    metrics: q.metrics,
    sort:    q.sort,
    limit:   q.limit,
  });

  // hard timeout to bound DB impact
  const data = await db.$queryRawUnsafe(
    `SET LOCAL statement_timeout = '5s'; ${sql.text}`,
    ...sql.params,
  );
  res.json({ data });
});

Trade-offs: allowlist every field — never interpolate user input into SQL. Limit complexity: max filters, max groupBy depth, max metrics; otherwise a single query can consume minutes of DB CPU. Statement timeout (SET LOCAL statement_timeout in Postgres) bounds worst-case damage — the query is killed automatically. Index the obvious: (tenantId, createdAt), (tenantId, status, createdAt); without indexes, analytics queries scan entire tables. Cache aggregation results for common dashboards — "orders per day by region" for the last quarter is requested often but changes slowly; cache for 1-5 minutes. For heavy analytics, separate OLTP from OLAP: replicate to ClickHouse, BigQuery, or Snowflake; query there. The API endpoint that routes to OLAP can offer richer aggregations without affecting the transactional DB. Tools like Cube.js, Hasura, PostgREST, and Hightouch provide query DSLs over analytics stores out of the box.

Question 54

How would you implement a feature that allows users to upload and manage videos in your API?

Situation: users upload videos up to 5 GB — for feeds, tutorials, or streams. Need efficient upload, transcoding to multiple renditions, HLS/DASH packaging, CDN delivery, thumbnails, and moderation.

Approach: direct upload to object storage via multipart + pre-signed URLs; transcoding pipeline on cloud service; adaptive streaming via CDN.

// 1. init multipart upload
app.post("/videos/uploads", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { filename, size, contentType } = req.body;
  if (size > 5 * 1024 * 1024 * 1024) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Max 5 GB" });
  if (!["video/mp4","video/quicktime","video/webm"].includes(contentType))
    return res.status(400).json({ error: "Unsupported format" });

  const videoId = crypto.randomUUID();
  const key = `raw/${req.user.sub}/${videoId}/source`;

  const { UploadId } = await s3.send(new CreateMultipartUploadCommand({
    Bucket: RAW_BUCKET, Key: key, ContentType: contentType,
  }));

  const partCount = Math.ceil(size / (100 * 1024 * 1024));    // 100 MB parts
  const partUrls = await Promise.all(Array.from({ length: partCount }, (_, i) =>
    getSignedUrl(s3, new UploadPartCommand({
      Bucket: RAW_BUCKET, Key: key, UploadId, PartNumber: i+1,
    }), { expiresIn: 6*3600 })));

  await db.video.create({
    data: { id: videoId, userId: req.user.sub, status: "uploading", s3Key: key, uploadId: UploadId },
  });

  res.json({ videoId, uploadId: UploadId, key, partUrls, chunkSize: 100*1024*1024 });
});

// 2. complete upload — triggers transcoding
app.post("/videos/uploads/:videoId/complete", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { parts } = req.body;
  const video = await db.video.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.videoId } });
  if (video.userId !== req.user.sub) return res.sendStatus(403);

  await s3.send(new CompleteMultipartUploadCommand({
    Bucket: RAW_BUCKET, Key: video.s3Key, UploadId: video.uploadId,
    MultipartUpload: { Parts: parts.sort((a,b) => a.PartNumber - b.PartNumber) },
  }));

  await db.video.update({ where: { id: video.id }, data: { status: "processing" } });

  // trigger MediaConvert / Mux / Cloudflare Stream job
  await mediaconvert.createJob({
    input:  `s3://${RAW_BUCKET}/${video.s3Key}`,
    outputs: {
      hls:     { destination: `s3://${CDN_BUCKET}/hls/${video.id}/`,   renditions: ["240p","480p","720p","1080p"] },
      thumbs:  { destination: `s3://${CDN_BUCKET}/thumb/${video.id}/`, intervals: [5, "middle", -5] },
    },
    callbackUrl: `${API_URL}/webhooks/mediaconvert`,
  });

  res.status(202).json(video);
});

// 3. webhook from transcoder — update status, publish
app.post("/webhooks/mediaconvert", verifyAwsSig, async (req, res) => {
  const { jobId, status, outputs } = req.body;
  if (status === "COMPLETE") {
    await db.video.update({
      where: { transcodeJobId: jobId },
      data: {
        status: "ready",
        hlsUrl: outputs.hls.playlistUrl,
        thumbnailUrl: outputs.thumbs.urls[1],
        duration: outputs.duration,
      },
    });
  } else if (status === "ERROR") {
    await db.video.update({ where: { transcodeJobId: jobId }, data: { status: "failed" } });
  }
  res.sendStatus(200);
});

// 4. streaming URL
app.get("/videos/:id/stream", async (req, res) => {
  const video = await db.video.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!video || video.status !== "ready") return res.sendStatus(404);
  if (video.visibility === "private") { /* authorize */ }
  res.redirect(302, `${CDN_URL}/hls/${video.id}/master.m3u8`);
});

Trade-offs: build vs buy: Mux, Cloudflare Stream, Bunny Stream, api.video handle upload-to-playback end-to-end with a simple API — for most teams, cheaper than maintaining MediaConvert pipelines. Adaptive bitrate (HLS/DASH) is essential: mobile on cellular needs 480p, desktop on fiber gets 1080p. Thumbnails at multiple timestamps help UX. Content moderation: AWS Rekognition, Hive, Sightengine scan for nudity/violence on upload. Storage tiering: originals in Glacier Deep Archive after 30 days; delivery renditions on CDN. DRM: Widevine/FairPlay/PlayReady for premium content; token-signed URLs for lighter protection. Live streaming is a separate beast — RTMP ingest, HLS/LL-HLS output; managed services (Mux, Livepeer, Cloudflare Stream Live) remove months of work.

Question 55

Describe how you would handle API deprecation and communicate changes to your users.

Situation: a v1 endpoint is being replaced by v2; some behavior is changing. Need to manage this transition without breaking existing clients or hiding the change.

Approach: structured deprecation process: announce → serve deprecation headers → track adoption → communicate → brownout → retire.

// phase 1: deprecation headers on v1
app.use("/v1/legacy-endpoint", (req, res, next) => {
  res.set({
    "Deprecation": "true",                                      // RFC 8594
    "Sunset":      "Wed, 30 Jun 2026 23:59:59 GMT",
    "Link":        '<https://api.example.com/v2/new-endpoint>; rel="successor-version"',
    "Warning":     '299 - "Endpoint deprecated; use /v2/new-endpoint by 2026-06-30"',
  });

  // log every usage for adoption tracking
  logger.info({
    endpoint: req.path,
    clientId: req.headers["x-client-id"] || req.user?.clientId,
    userAgent: req.headers["user-agent"],
  }, "deprecated.endpoint.hit");

  next();
});

// phase 3: brownout — intentional short outages near end of life
// schedule: serve 503 for 15 min each day for the last 2 weeks, escalating
const BROWNOUT_SCHEDULE = [
  { start: "2026-06-16", duration: 15, dailyHour: 14 },      // 15-min brownout at 2pm UTC
  { start: "2026-06-23", duration: 60, dailyHour: 14 },
  { start: "2026-06-29", duration: 360, dailyHour: 0 },      // 6-hour brownout
];

app.use("/v1/legacy-endpoint", (req, res, next) => {
  if (inBrownoutWindow(BROWNOUT_SCHEDULE)) {
    return res.status(503).set("Retry-After", "600").json({
      error: "endpoint_deprecated",
      message: "This endpoint is in a scheduled brownout. Use /v2/new-endpoint. Final removal: 2026-06-30.",
      docs: "https://api.example.com/docs/migration/v2",
    });
  }
  next();
});

Communication plan:

  1. T-6 months: public changelog entry, email to developers, docs note. Migration guide with before/after examples.
  2. T-3 months: reminder email with usage report per client ("your app is still using v1 at 1,200 req/day").
  3. T-1 month: deprecation headers become louder; dashboards show countdown; direct outreach to top clients.
  4. T-2 weeks: brownouts begin; customers feel the transition without being surprised at cutover.
  5. T-0: remove. Any remaining calls receive 410 Gone with the migration URL.

Trade-offs: brownouts are controversial but effective: customers who haven't migrated despite 6 months of warnings often won't until they feel pain. Announce brownouts in advance with specific times; tie them to deprecation headers so automated clients can detect. Usage tracking per client makes communication targeted — spamming all customers about endpoints they don't use erodes trust. 410 Gone at the end, not 404 — 410 is permanent and tells clients to stop retrying. Some endpoints can't be retired (public API with partners on multi-year contracts) — live with them behind the gateway forever, isolated from maintenance burden. Internal vs external: internal APIs can move faster (quarters, not years); external public APIs require long horizons and genuine empathy for integrators.

Question 56

Your API needs to be accessible to users with disabilities. What considerations would you make to ensure accessibility?

Situation: API is a public product; users with visual, motor, or cognitive disabilities rely on screen readers, keyboard navigation, and assistive technologies. The API itself isn't perceived, but its usability depends on clear errors, predictable structure, and accessible documentation.

Approach: API design affects downstream UIs' ability to be accessible. Clear semantic responses, proper use of HTTP, and accessible documentation unblock client developers who build accessible UIs.

  • Semantic HTTP: status codes must match meaning — 401 vs 403, 404 vs 410, 409 for conflicts. Screen readers rely on assistive tech which consumes HTTP signals through the client UI.
  • Structured errors with machine-readable codes let clients present tailored messages (e.g., field: "email", code: "required" lets a form highlight the exact input and read a clear message via aria-describedby).
  • Rich error context: include a human-readable detail in addition to code — clients can surface this directly to screen readers.
  • Pagination with ARIA-friendly metadata: expose total, page, and hasMore so clients render "Page 3 of 10" rather than just "next".
  • Alt text / captions: if the API handles media (user-uploaded images or videos), accept and store altText, captionUrl; return them in responses; require on upload where it matters (user avatars, CMS images).
  • Language negotiation: Accept-Language support — clients in locales with better screen-reader support (e.g., Japanese VoiceOver) deliver localized strings.
// upload with required altText for images meant to be displayed
app.post("/media/images", requireAuth, validate({ body: z.object({
  key: z.string(),
  altText: z.string().trim().min(1).max(500),                // non-negotiable for display images
  caption: z.string().trim().max(2000).optional(),
}).strict() }), async (req, res) => {
  const media = await db.media.create({
    data: { ...req.body, userId: req.user.sub, mediaType: "image" },
  });
  res.status(201).json({
    id: media.id,
    url: media.cdnUrl,
    altText: media.altText,                                   // always returned
    caption: media.caption,
  });
});

Documentation accessibility:

  • Docs site meets WCAG 2.2 AA — semantic HTML, keyboard navigation, sufficient color contrast, no color-only signals.
  • Code examples in multiple languages; copy-to-clipboard buttons labeled for screen readers.
  • Headings hierarchy (h1 > h2 > h3) correctly nested — screen-reader users navigate by headings.
  • Interactive "try it" console: form inputs with proper labels, aria-live regions for responses, error messages associated with fields.
  • Alt text on diagrams; architecture illustrations with text alternatives.

Trade-offs: accessibility is often framed as "UI only" — but APIs shape what UIs can build. Returning a generic {"error": "bad"} forces the client to invent error language; returning structured codes lets the client speak accurately to users, including those relying on assistive tech. Required alt text at upload time is controversial (friction) but ensures downstream accessibility; alternative: optional on upload, but clients warned. Test the docs with a screen reader (NVDA, VoiceOver) — bugs jump out immediately. Accessibility, like security, is easier to build in than to retrofit.

Question 57

How would you implement a feature that allows users to generate and download reports from your API?

Situation: users want customized reports (PDF, CSV, Excel) exported from their data — sales reports, invoices, analytics summaries. Generation can take seconds to minutes.

Approach: async generation pattern — request creates a job, worker produces the file, client polls or receives webhook, downloads via signed URL.

// 1. request report — returns 202
app.post("/reports", requireAuth, validate({ body: ReportRequestSchema }), async (req, res) => {
  const { reportType, format = "pdf", params } = req.body;

  // validate params per reportType
  const config = REPORT_CONFIGS[reportType];
  if (!config) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Unknown report" });
  if (!config.formats.includes(format)) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Format unsupported" });

  const job = await db.reportJob.create({
    data: {
      userId: req.user.sub,
      reportType, format, params,
      status: "queued",
      requestedAt: new Date(),
    },
  });

  await queue.add("report.generate", { jobId: job.id }, { attempts: 2 });
  res.status(202).location(`/reports/${job.id}`).json(job);
});

// 2. job status
app.get("/reports/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const job = await db.reportJob.findFirst({
    where: { id: req.params.id, userId: req.user.sub },
  });
  if (!job) return res.sendStatus(404);

  // add a download URL if ready
  if (job.status === "ready" && job.s3Key) {
    job.downloadUrl = await getSignedUrl(s3, new GetObjectCommand({
      Bucket: REPORT_BUCKET, Key: job.s3Key,
      ResponseContentDisposition: `attachment; filename="${reportFilename(job)}"`,
    }), { expiresIn: 3600 });
  }
  res.json(job);
});

// 3. worker
queue.process("report.generate", async (job) => {
  const record = await db.reportJob.update({
    where: { id: job.data.jobId },
    data: { status: "processing" },
  });

  try {
    const stream = await generateReport(record);          // returns a Readable
    const key = `reports/${record.userId}/${record.id}.${record.format}`;
    await new Upload({
      client: s3,
      params: { Bucket: REPORT_BUCKET, Key: key, Body: stream, ContentType: mimeFor(record.format) },
    }).done();

    await db.reportJob.update({
      where: { id: record.id },
      data:  { status: "ready", s3Key: key, completedAt: new Date() },
    });

    // optional — webhook / email the user
    if (record.notifyOnComplete) {
      const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: record.userId } });
      await sendEmail(user.email, "Your report is ready", `${APP_URL}/reports/${record.id}`);
    }
  } catch (err) {
    await db.reportJob.update({
      where: { id: record.id },
      data: { status: "failed", error: err.message, failedAt: new Date() },
    });
    throw err;
  }
});

// report renderer — streaming where possible
async function generateReport(job) {
  if (job.reportType === "orders" && job.format === "csv") {
    const cursor = db.order.findManyStream({ where: whereFromParams(job.params) });
    return asCsvStream(cursor, ["id","customer","total","status","date"]);
  }
  if (job.format === "pdf") {
    // use Puppeteer / Playwright to render HTML → PDF, or pdfkit for declarative PDFs
    return renderPdfStream(job);
  }
  // ... other formats
}

Trade-offs: always async for reports — even "small" reports regress to slow once data grows; better to build the polling UX from day one. PDF generation is expensive: Puppeteer uses ~100-500 MB per render; isolate workers; consider dedicated services (DocRaptor, API2PDF) for simple cases. CSV/Excel streaming: write headers first, stream rows; SheetJS for .xlsx. Signed URL with short expiry (1 hour) prevents link-leak abuse. Retention: bucket lifecycle rule deletes reports after 30-90 days — users re-run if they need them again. Background pre-generation for common reports (daily sales rollups) — users get instant results if they request the already-computed one. Idempotency: if user retries the same report request, return the existing job rather than starting a duplicate (keyed by params hash).

Question 58

Describe how you would handle nested resources in a RESTful API.

Situation: API models hierarchical data — an organization with teams, teams with members, members with roles; or orders with line items, line items with adjustments. Must decide between nested URLs, sub-resource endpoints, or embedding.

Approach: nested URLs for "create/list within context"; flat URLs for "read/update/delete individual"; limited nesting depth (2-3 levels); optional embedding via query parameters for read efficiency.

// GOOD — nested for creation context, flat for individuals
POST /v1/organizations/{orgId}/teams            // create team in org
GET  /v1/organizations/{orgId}/teams            // list teams in org
GET  /v1/teams/{teamId}                         // read individual team (flat)
PATCH /v1/teams/{teamId}                        // update team (flat)
DELETE /v1/teams/{teamId}                       // delete team (flat)

POST /v1/teams/{teamId}/members
GET  /v1/teams/{teamId}/members
DELETE /v1/teams/{teamId}/members/{userId}      // membership is itself the resource

// LESS GOOD — too-deep nesting
GET /v1/organizations/{orgId}/teams/{teamId}/members/{userId}/roles/{roleId}

// BETTER — flatten once ID is known
GET /v1/memberships/{membershipId}/roles

Implementation pattern:

// middleware loads and authorizes parent context
async function loadOrg(req, res, next) {
  const org = await db.organization.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.orgId } });
  if (!org) return res.sendStatus(404);
  const member = await db.orgMembership.findFirst({
    where: { orgId: org.id, userId: req.user.sub },
  });
  if (!member) return res.sendStatus(404);
  req.org = org; req.orgMember = member;
  next();
}

// nested endpoint
app.post("/v1/organizations/:orgId/teams",
  requireAuth, loadOrg,
  (req, res, next) => req.orgMember.role === "admin" ? next() : res.sendStatus(403),
  validate({ body: TeamCreateSchema }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const team = await db.team.create({
      data: { ...req.body, orgId: req.org.id, createdBy: req.user.sub },
    });
    res.status(201).location(`/v1/teams/${team.id}`).json(team);
  }
);

// embedding on read — client chooses detail level
app.get("/v1/teams/:teamId", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const include = new Set((req.query.include || "").split(",").filter(Boolean));
  const team = await db.team.findUnique({
    where: { id: req.params.teamId },
    include: {
      members:      include.has("members"),
      organization: include.has("organization"),
      recentActivity: include.has("activity") ? { take: 20, orderBy: { createdAt: "desc" } } : false,
    },
  });
  if (!team) return res.sendStatus(404);
  // authorization
  const allowed = await canReadTeam(req.user.sub, team);
  if (!allowed) return res.sendStatus(404);
  res.json(team);
});

Trade-offs: nested URLs show ownership structure (URL reads like the relationship), but depth becomes awkward past 2 levels. Flat URLs for individuals mean the ID is globally unique and URLs are shorter; natural for PATCH/DELETE. Embedding vs multiple requests: ?include=members,activity lets one request fetch what a screen needs; alternative is 3 round-trips. Embedding is handy but watch out for payload explosion — allow nesting depth, cap collection sizes within embeds. HAL / JSON:API / Relay standardize embedding with links and relationships for consistency. GraphQL solves this elegantly by design — client queries the graph it needs. For REST, a pragmatic middle ground works: nested creation endpoints, flat individual endpoints, optional include/fields for efficient reads, clear authorization on every layer.

Question 59

Your API needs to support WebRTC for real-time communication. How would you implement this?

Situation: need peer-to-peer real-time audio/video communication (video calls, screen share) alongside the existing REST API. WebRTC handles the media; the API brokers signaling and access control.

Approach: API issues tokens for a TURN/STUN server; clients exchange SDP offers via WebSocket or HTTP; media flows directly peer-to-peer (or through a SFU for multi-party).

// 1. Authenticate for a call session — issue short-lived TURN credentials
app.post("/calls/:roomId/access", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { roomId } = req.params;
  const room = await db.callRoom.findUnique({ where: { id: roomId } });
  if (!room || !canJoin(req.user.sub, room)) return res.sendStatus(403);

  // generate HMAC'd TURN credentials (time-bounded)
  const ttl = 3 * 3600;
  const user = `${Math.floor(Date.now()/1000) + ttl}:${req.user.sub}`;
  const cred = crypto.createHmac("sha1", TURN_SECRET).update(user).digest("base64");

  // log participant
  await db.callParticipant.create({
    data: { roomId, userId: req.user.sub, joinedAt: new Date() },
  });

  res.json({
    iceServers: [
      { urls: "stun:stun.example.com:3478" },
      { urls: ["turn:turn.example.com:3478?transport=udp", "turns:turn.example.com:5349?transport=tcp"],
        username: user, credential: cred },
    ],
    token: issueCallToken(req.user.sub, roomId, ttl),        // for signaling ws
    sfuEndpoint: room.isGroup ? `wss://sfu.example.com/room/${roomId}` : null,
  });
});

// 2. Signaling via WebSocket — relay SDP offers/answers/ICE candidates
const wss = new WebSocketServer({ server, path: "/signaling" });

wss.on("connection", async (ws, req) => {
  const token = new URL(req.url, "http://x").searchParams.get("token");
  const { userId, roomId } = verifyCallToken(token);
  if (!userId) return ws.close(1008);

  const sub = redis.duplicate();
  await sub.subscribe(`call:${roomId}`);
  sub.on("message", (_c, msg) => {
    const data = JSON.parse(msg);
    if (data.to === userId || data.to === "*") ws.send(msg);
  });

  ws.on("message", async (raw) => {
    const msg = JSON.parse(raw);
    // forward to other peers
    await redis.publish(`call:${roomId}`, JSON.stringify({
      from: userId, ...msg,
    }));
  });

  ws.on("close", async () => {
    await redis.publish(`call:${roomId}`, JSON.stringify({ type: "peer-left", from: userId }));
    sub.unsubscribe(); sub.quit();
  });
});

// 3. Call history + recording REST endpoints
app.get("/calls", requireAuth, listCalls);
app.get("/calls/:id/recording", requireAuth, streamRecording);

Trade-offs: P2P vs SFU (Selective Forwarding Unit): 1:1 calls are cheap P2P (no server bandwidth); 3+ participants need a SFU (mediasoup, Janus, LiveKit, Pion) to avoid O(N²) connections and CPU. Managed services (LiveKit Cloud, Twilio Video, Daily.co, Agora, 100ms) abstract the SFU and scaling entirely. TURN for NAT traversal — roughly 20% of calls fail pure-P2P behind corporate firewalls; TURN relays cost bandwidth but make the service usable. Recording: server-side mixing via an SFU bot; storage and retention policies matter. End-to-end encryption: default in WebRTC for media; additional E2EE with Insertable Streams for zero-knowledge architectures (Jitsi, Zoom). Security: TURN credentials must be short-lived; signaling tokens scoped to room + user; rate-limit call creation to prevent abuse. For most products, use managed infrastructure; build your own SFU only with serious scale or compliance reasons.

Question 60

How would you handle user authentication and authorization for a multi-tenant API?

Situation: SaaS with thousands of tenants (customers). Each tenant has its own users, roles, and data; no tenant can ever see another's data. Need auth that binds users to tenants and scales cleanly.

Approach: auth issues tokens carrying both user and tenant context; every query filters by tenant; data isolation enforced at multiple layers.

// JWT shape for multi-tenant
// {
//   sub: "user_abc",
//   tenantId: "tnt_xyz",
//   roles: ["admin"],
//   scopes: ["users:read", "users:write"],
//   exp: ...
// }

// tenant resolution — subdomain, header, or path
function resolveTenant(req) {
  // option A: subdomain acme.app.example.com
  const host = req.hostname;
  if (host.endsWith(".app.example.com")) {
    return host.split(".")[0];
  }
  // option B: header X-Tenant
  if (req.headers["x-tenant"]) return req.headers["x-tenant"];
  // option C: path prefix /t/:tenantId/...
  return req.params.tenantId;
}

// auth middleware verifies token AND matches tenant
function requireAuth() {
  return async (req, res, next) => {
    const [, token] = (req.headers.authorization || "").split(" ");
    try {
      const payload = jwt.verify(token, AUTH_KEY, { algorithms: ["RS256"] });
      const requestedTenant = resolveTenant(req);
      if (payload.tenantId !== requestedTenant) {
        return res.status(403).json({ error: "Cross-tenant access denied" });
      }
      req.user = payload;
      req.tenantId = payload.tenantId;
      next();
    } catch {
      res.sendStatus(401);
    }
  };
}

// every query filters by tenantId — automatic via Prisma extension
const prisma = basePrisma.$extends({
  query: {
    $allModels: {
      async $allOperations({ args, query, model, operation }) {
        const tenantId = asyncLocalStorage.getStore()?.tenantId;
        if (!tenantId) throw new Error("Missing tenant context");
        // inject tenantId filter
        if (["findMany","findFirst","findUnique","updateMany","deleteMany","count"].includes(operation)) {
          args.where = { ...args.where, tenantId };
        }
        if (operation === "create" && !args.data.tenantId) {
          args.data = { ...args.data, tenantId };
        }
        return query(args);
      },
    },
  },
});

// belt & suspenders — DB row-level security
// ALTER TABLE users ENABLE ROW LEVEL SECURITY;
// CREATE POLICY tenant_isolation ON users
//   USING (tenant_id = current_setting('app.tenant_id')::uuid);

app.use((req, res, next) => {
  asyncLocalStorage.run({ tenantId: req.tenantId, userId: req.user.sub }, next);
});

Isolation strategies (from cheap to paranoid):

ModelIsolationCost
Shared DB, tenantId columnApp-level filterLowest — fine for 99% of SaaS
Shared DB, RLS policiesDB-enforcedLow — DB bug-proofs app bugs
Schema per tenantStrongMedium — migrations run many times
DB per tenantStrongestHigh — enterprise / compliance-heavy

Trade-offs: bug-prone filtering is the biggest risk: one missing where tenantId = ? and you leak cross-tenant data. Defense in depth: app-level filter + RLS + integration tests that assert cross-tenant isolation. Auth flows: signup creates a tenant + admin user; invitations are per-tenant; SSO configured per-tenant (SAML/OIDC). User in multiple tenants: model membership as the bridge; tokens name the active tenant; switching tenant re-mints the token. Per-tenant resource limits (rate limits, storage quotas) tracked by tenantId. Auditing: every access logs both userId and tenantId. Super-admin access (support engineers debugging tenant issues) is special — elevated tokens with full audit trail, time-bounded, require approval. Tools like WorkOS, Auth0 B2B, Clerk Organizations bundle multi-tenant auth patterns.

Question 61

Describe how you would implement a feature that allows users to tag and categorize their data.

Situation: users organize their data with tags and categories — notes, photos, documents, bookmarks. Need flexible tagging, fast querying, autocompletion.

Approach: many-to-many between items and tags; normalize tag names; index for lookups; autocomplete via prefix search.

// schema — tags per user (or per tenant), items have tags via junction
// Tag: id, userId, name, color, normalizedName (lowercased, trimmed)
// ItemTag: itemId, tagId, taggedAt

// CREATE / UPDATE tags on an item (replace all)
app.put("/items/:id/tags", requireAuth, loadOwnItem,
  validate({ body: z.object({ tags: z.array(z.string().trim().min(1).max(50)).max(30) }) }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const userId = req.user.sub;
    const wanted = [...new Set(req.body.tags.map((t) => t.toLowerCase()))];

    await db.$transaction(async (tx) => {
      // get or create each tag
      const tags = await Promise.all(wanted.map((name) =>
        tx.tag.upsert({
          where:  { userId_normalizedName: { userId, normalizedName: name } },
          create: { userId, name, normalizedName: name },
          update: {},
        })
      ));

      // replace associations
      await tx.itemTag.deleteMany({ where: { itemId: req.item.id } });
      await tx.itemTag.createMany({
        data: tags.map((t) => ({ itemId: req.item.id, tagId: t.id })),
      });
    });

    const updated = await db.item.findUnique({
      where: { id: req.item.id },
      include: { tags: { include: { tag: true } } },
    });
    res.json(updated);
  }
);

// FILTER by tag combinations
// GET /items?tags=work,urgent&tagsOp=and
app.get("/items", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const tagNames = String(req.query.tags || "").split(",").filter(Boolean);
  const op = req.query.tagsOp === "and" ? "every" : "some";

  const items = await db.item.findMany({
    where: {
      userId: req.user.sub,
      tags: tagNames.length ? {
        [op === "every" ? "every" : "some"]: {
          tag: { normalizedName: { in: tagNames.map((t) => t.toLowerCase()) } },
        },
      } : undefined,
    },
    include: { tags: { include: { tag: true } } },
    take: 100, orderBy: { createdAt: "desc" },
  });
  res.json({ data: items });
});

// AUTOCOMPLETE — prefix search
app.get("/tags/suggest", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const q = String(req.query.q || "").trim().toLowerCase();
  if (q.length < 1) return res.json({ data: [] });

  const tags = await db.tag.findMany({
    where: {
      userId: req.user.sub,
      normalizedName: { startsWith: q },
    },
    orderBy: [{ useCount: "desc" }, { normalizedName: "asc" }],
    take: 10,
    select: { id: true, name: true, color: true, useCount: true },
  });
  res.json({ data: tags });
});

// RENAME a tag across all items
app.patch("/tags/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const tag = await db.tag.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!tag || tag.userId !== req.user.sub) return res.sendStatus(404);

  const newName = req.body.name.trim();
  await db.tag.update({
    where: { id: tag.id },
    data: { name: newName, normalizedName: newName.toLowerCase() },
  });
  res.sendStatus(204);
});

Trade-offs: normalization (normalizedName lowercased) prevents duplicates like "Work" vs "work"; unique constraint on (userId, normalizedName). Unique tag per user vs shared system tags: shared can be more efficient storage but risks collisions and privacy issues; per-user is simpler. Autocomplete at scale: prefix-match on normalizedName with an index; for fuzzy search (typos), Postgres pg_trgm or Meilisearch/Typesense. Flat tags vs hierarchical categories: tags (many-to-many, no hierarchy) are flexible; categories (one-to-many tree) are rigid but good for browsing; some systems offer both. Usage counting (useCount) orders autocomplete usefully but needs maintenance on tag/untag; alternative is a materialized count updated by a cron. Tag merging: support combining duplicates into one. Colors and emojis as visual aids are small UX wins. For content-heavy systems (Notion, Obsidian), consider adding ACL on tags themselves — shared tags across a workspace.

Question 62

Your API needs to integrate with a payment gateway. How would you handle this integration?

Situation: users pay via credit card, Apple Pay, ACH; need to accept one-time payments, subscriptions, and refunds; must meet PCI-DSS without handling raw card numbers.

Approach: integrate a payment gateway (Stripe, Adyen, Braintree) that tokenizes cards. Your API orchestrates; the gateway handles sensitive data.

// 1. client tokenizes card via Stripe.js / Elements — raw PAN never touches your server
//    Stripe.js returns a payment method ID (pm_abc)

// 2. API creates the charge using the PM ID
import Stripe from "stripe";
const stripe = new Stripe(process.env.STRIPE_SECRET_KEY, { apiVersion: "2025-10-16" });

app.post("/orders", requireAuth, async (req, res, next) => {
  const { items, paymentMethodId, shippingAddressId } = req.body;

  // idempotency — prevents duplicate charges on retries
  const idempKey = req.headers["idempotency-key"] || crypto.randomUUID();

  // pre-compute total server-side (never trust client totals)
  const total = await calculateTotal(items, req.user.sub);

  try {
    // create PaymentIntent — Stripe handles 3DS, SCA, authentication
    const intent = await stripe.paymentIntents.create({
      amount:   total.cents,
      currency: total.currency,
      customer: req.user.stripeCustomerId,
      payment_method: paymentMethodId,
      confirm: true,                                       // attempt immediately
      off_session: false,
      return_url: `${APP_URL}/checkout/complete`,
      metadata: { userId: req.user.sub },
    }, { idempotencyKey: `order-${idempKey}` });

    // Stripe may require 3DS challenge
    if (intent.status === "requires_action") {
      return res.status(402).json({
        requiresAction: true,
        clientSecret:   intent.client_secret,              // client uses to complete 3DS
      });
    }

    if (intent.status !== "succeeded") {
      return res.status(402).json({ error: "Payment failed", code: intent.last_payment_error?.code });
    }

    // persist order
    const order = await db.order.create({
      data: {
        userId: req.user.sub,
        items, total: total.cents,
        paymentIntentId: intent.id,
        status: "paid",
      },
    });
    res.status(201).json(order);
  } catch (err) {
    if (err.type === "StripeCardError") {
      return res.status(402).json({ error: err.message, code: err.code });
    }
    next(err);
  }
});

// 3. webhook receiver — source of truth for payment state
app.post("/webhooks/stripe",
  express.raw({ type: "application/json" }),
  async (req, res) => {
    let event;
    try {
      event = stripe.webhooks.constructEvent(
        req.body, req.headers["stripe-signature"], process.env.STRIPE_WEBHOOK_SECRET,
      );
    } catch { return res.sendStatus(400); }

    // dedupe by event.id
    const seen = await redis.set(`stripe:evt:${event.id}`, "1", "NX", "EX", 86400);
    if (!seen) return res.sendStatus(200);

    switch (event.type) {
      case "charge.refunded":
        await handleRefund(event.data.object);
        break;
      case "payment_intent.succeeded":
        await confirmOrder(event.data.object.id);
        break;
      case "invoice.payment_failed":
        await markSubscriptionPastDue(event.data.object);
        break;
    }
    res.sendStatus(200);
  }
);

Trade-offs: PCI scope matters. Using Stripe.js / Elements keeps your server outside the PCI PAN scope entirely — you only handle tokens. Full server-side processing pulls you into PCI-DSS Level 1 (~6-12 months of audit work). Idempotency on all charge operations — network errors on POST /orders must not double-charge. Webhooks are the source of truth for async payment state (refunds, disputes, subscription changes) — never trust client-side "payment succeeded" alone. 3DS / SCA (Europe PSD2) is mandatory for many EU transactions; use the gateway's built-in flow. Subscriptions: Stripe Billing / Paddle / Lemon Squeezy handle prorations, dunning, tax — don't reinvent. Refund policies in code: full refund within X days automatically, partial refund requires approval. Reconciliation: daily job compares your orders to Stripe's charges; discrepancies alert. Tax: Stripe Tax, Avalara, or TaxJar handle sales tax/VAT.

Question 63

How would you design an API to support real-time collaboration between multiple users?

Situation: users collaborate on shared documents in real-time — cursors, selections, edits propagate within milliseconds. Must handle concurrent edits without lost updates.

Approach: CRDTs (Conflict-free Replicated Data Types) via Yjs or Automerge; sync via WebSocket with presence protocol.

// server — WebSocket backed by y-websocket
import { setupWSConnection } from "y-websocket/bin/utils";
import { LeveldbPersistence } from "y-leveldb";
import { WebSocketServer } from "ws";

const persistence = new LeveldbPersistence("./yjs-db");

const wss = new WebSocketServer({ server, path: "/yjs" });

wss.on("connection", async (conn, req) => {
  // parse documentId + auth token from URL
  const url = new URL(req.url, "http://x");
  const docName = url.searchParams.get("doc");
  const token = url.searchParams.get("token");

  const user = await verifyAccessToken(token);
  const doc = await db.document.findUnique({ where: { id: docName } });

  if (!user || !canEditDocument(user, doc)) {
    conn.close(1008, "Unauthorized");
    return;
  }

  // attach yjs — handles sync protocol, awareness
  setupWSConnection(conn, req, {
    docName,
    gc: true,
    persistence,
    // filter operations by permission if needed
  });

  // audit
  logger.info({ userId: user.sub, docName }, "collaborative.session.started");
});

// REST companion endpoints
// POST /documents          — create
// GET  /documents/:id      — fetch snapshot (serialized CRDT state)
// PATCH /documents/:id     — rename, move (metadata, not content)
// GET  /documents/:id/history — periodic snapshots

// snapshot job — run every N minutes to save recovery points
async function snapshotDocument(docId) {
  const state = await persistence.getYDoc(docId);
  const snapshot = Y.encodeStateAsUpdate(state);
  await db.documentSnapshot.create({
    data: {
      documentId: docId,
      state: snapshot,
      size: snapshot.byteLength,
      createdAt: new Date(),
    },
  });
}

// client (browser) using yjs
// import * as Y from "yjs";
// import { WebsocketProvider } from "y-websocket";
// import { MonacoBinding } from "y-monaco";
// const ydoc = new Y.Doc();
// const provider = new WebsocketProvider("wss://api/yjs", docId, ydoc, {
//   params: { token: authToken },
// });
// const yText = ydoc.getText("content");
// new MonacoBinding(yText, monacoEditor.getModel(), new Set([monacoEditor]), provider.awareness);

Architecture components:

  • CRDT engine (Yjs, Automerge): conflict resolution is mathematical; no server-side OT transforms needed.
  • Transport: WebSocket for updates; awareness protocol for cursors, selections, user presence (ephemeral).
  • Persistence: snapshot CRDT state periodically + append updates since last snapshot. On reconnect, server sends only missing updates (state vector).
  • Fan-out: one server instance per room is simplest; for many concurrent rooms, shard by docId or use Redis pub/sub.
  • Offline support: client keeps local CRDT; changes sync on reconnect automatically.

Trade-offs: CRDTs vs OT: OT (Google Docs' classic approach) is smaller wire format but requires server-side transformation of every edit; complex and non-trivial to implement correctly. CRDTs have larger payloads but converge regardless of order — perfect for offline-first and P2P; Yjs's text CRDT is highly optimized (often beats OT in practice). Managed services: Liveblocks, PartyKit, Yjs with Tiptap Cloud, Supabase Realtime provide collaboration out of the box — save months of work. Access control: WebSocket handshake validates token and doc permission; once connected, the protocol assumes trust — verify membership on every reconnect. Large documents / long histories: garbage-collect old CRDT operations periodically; maintain named snapshots. Per-user permissions within a doc (read-only vs edit for different sections) is hard with CRDTs — usually handled at document level; for section-level, separate CRDTs per section.

Question 64

Describe how you would implement a custom caching strategy to improve API performance.

Situation: API reads are dominated by hot items but the cache hit rate is mediocre (40%) and cache misses cause DB spikes. Need a smarter caching strategy.

Approach: multi-tier cache (in-process + Redis); request coalescing (single-flight); cache warming for predictable hot keys; stale-while-revalidate to hide origin latency.

// multi-tier cache with singleflight
import LRU from "lru-cache";

const l1 = new LRU({ max: 10_000, ttl: 10_000 });           // 10s in-process
const inflight = new Map();                                 // singleflight

async function getCached(key, fetcher, { ttlMs = 60_000, swrMs = 300_000 } = {}) {
  // 1. L1 — process-local, sub-millisecond
  const local = l1.get(key);
  if (local && local.expiresAt > Date.now()) return local.value;

  // 2. L2 — Redis
  const cached = await redis.get(key);
  if (cached) {
    const { value, storedAt } = JSON.parse(cached);
    const age = Date.now() - storedAt;
    l1.set(key, { value, expiresAt: Date.now() + 10_000 });

    // stale-while-revalidate: serve stale but refresh in background
    if (age > ttlMs && age < ttlMs + swrMs) {
      queueMicrotask(() => refresh(key, fetcher, ttlMs).catch(() => {}));
    }
    return value;
  }

  // 3. fetch from origin — singleflight prevents thundering herd
  if (inflight.has(key)) return inflight.get(key);

  const promise = (async () => {
    try {
      const value = await fetcher();
      await redis.setex(key, Math.floor((ttlMs + swrMs) / 1000),
        JSON.stringify({ value, storedAt: Date.now() }));
      l1.set(key, { value, expiresAt: Date.now() + 10_000 });
      return value;
    } finally {
      inflight.delete(key);
    }
  })();
  inflight.set(key, promise);
  return promise;
}

// cache-warm hot content on a schedule
async function warmPopular() {
  const top = await db.product.findMany({
    where: { popular: true },
    take: 200,
    orderBy: { views30d: "desc" },
  });
  for (const product of top) {
    await getCached(`product:${product.id}`, () => Promise.resolve(product),
      { ttlMs: 600_000, swrMs: 600_000 });
  }
}
setInterval(warmPopular, 5 * 60_000);

// TAG-based invalidation on writes
async function updateProduct(id, data) {
  const updated = await db.product.update({ where: { id }, data });
  await Promise.all([
    redis.del(`product:${id}`),
    redis.del(`product:slug:${updated.slug}`),
    cdn.purgeTag(`product:${id}`),
    cdn.purgeTag(`category:${updated.categoryId}`),
  ]);
  l1.delete(`product:${id}`);
  return updated;
}

Key techniques:

  • Multi-tier: L1 (in-process LRU, 10ms TTL) absorbs repeated hits within one request or within a second; L2 (Redis) shared across instances; L3 (CDN) at the edge.
  • Single-flight: when 100 concurrent requests miss the cache for the same key, one fetches from origin; 99 await the same promise.
  • Stale-while-revalidate: serve yesterday's answer instantly, refresh in background; users never wait for origin during cache churn.
  • Tag-based invalidation: Fastly/Cloudflare Cache Tags, or manual — a product update invalidates product:id AND category:id AND any collections that embed it.
  • Cache warming: populate known-hot entries before traffic peaks (e.g., before deploy, after flush).

Trade-offs: staleness budget varies by data type — product prices must be accurate (short TTL + invalidation); blog posts tolerate minutes (long TTL). L1 consistency: in-process caches diverge across instances; acceptable for short TTLs. Cache stampede protection: singleflight is essential; without it, a cold cache during traffic surge means every request stampedes the DB. Cost: Redis memory isn't free; evict aggressively with allkeys-lru; monitor hit rate per prefix. Observability: emit cache hit/miss metrics per endpoint; a 40% hit rate is visible and actionable.

Question 65

Your API needs to support multiple authentication methods. How would you design this?

Situation: API must support multiple auth methods — username/password, OAuth (Google/GitHub), SSO (SAML, OIDC), passwordless (magic link), API keys for server-to-server, mobile PKCE. Users mix and match.

Approach: unify behind session-issuing identity service; every auth method produces the same session/token; API only understands the session.

// authentication service pattern
// 1. /auth/password         — email + password → session
// 2. /auth/oauth/google     — redirect flow → session
// 3. /auth/magic-link       — email a signed URL → session
// 4. /auth/saml             — SAML assertion → session
// 5. /auth/api-keys         — Authorization: Bearer sk_xxx

// password login
app.post("/auth/password", rateLimitByEmail, async (req, res) => {
  const { email, password } = req.body;
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { email: email.toLowerCase() } });
  if (!user || !await argon2.verify(user.passwordHash, password)) {
    // timing-safe; same error for missing user + bad password
    return res.status(401).json({ error: "invalid_credentials" });
  }
  if (user.totpEnabled) {
    return res.json({ requires: "totp", challenge: issueChallenge(user.id) });
  }
  return res.json(await issueSession(user.id, req));
});

// magic link — passwordless
app.post("/auth/magic-link/request", rateLimitByEmail, async (req, res) => {
  const { email } = req.body;
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { email } });
  if (user) {
    const token = crypto.randomBytes(24).toString("base64url");
    const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(token).digest("hex");
    await db.magicLink.create({
      data: { userId: user.id, tokenHash: hash, expiresAt: new Date(Date.now() + 15*60_000) },
    });
    await sendEmail(email, "Sign in", `${APP_URL}/auth/magic-link/confirm?t=${token}`);
  }
  res.sendStatus(200);                              // same response regardless (no enumeration)
});

// OAuth / OIDC — openid-client handles the spec
app.get("/auth/oauth/google", (req, res) => {
  const codeVerifier = generators.codeVerifier();
  req.session.oauth = { codeVerifier, provider: "google" };
  res.redirect(googleClient.authorizationUrl({
    scope: "openid email profile",
    code_challenge: generators.codeChallenge(codeVerifier),
    code_challenge_method: "S256",
  }));
});

// API keys — for server-to-server
// Client sends: Authorization: Bearer sk_live_abc123...
export async function authenticate(req, res, next) {
  const [, token] = (req.headers.authorization || "").split(" ");
  if (!token) return res.sendStatus(401);

  if (token.startsWith("sk_")) {
    // API key — hash and look up
    const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(token).digest("hex");
    const key  = await db.apiKey.findUnique({ where: { tokenHash: hash }, include: { user: true } });
    if (!key || key.revokedAt) return res.sendStatus(401);
    req.user = { sub: key.user.id, via: "api_key", keyId: key.id, scopes: key.scopes };
  } else {
    // JWT session
    try {
      const payload = await jose.jwtVerify(token, JWKS);
      req.user = { sub: payload.sub, via: "session", scopes: payload.scopes };
    } catch {
      return res.sendStatus(401);
    }
  }
  next();
}

Trade-offs: unify session representation: once authenticated, downstream code sees req.user — doesn't care how the user got there. This lets you add auth methods without touching every endpoint. Account linking: user signs up with email/password; later logs in with Google — link by email (carefully, to prevent account takeover: verify the email independently before linking). MFA orthogonal to the first factor: any first factor can trigger a second. Rate limiting per method — passwords need aggressive per-email+IP limits (brute force); magic links need per-email limits (email bombing). Scopes differ per method: API keys are narrower than sessions by default (no impersonation, no billing). SaaS identity platforms (Auth0, Clerk, WorkOS, Stytch, Supabase Auth) bundle all these methods; for most products, cheaper than in-house. Audit: every login logs method + IP + device; suspicious pattern detection; anomaly alerts.

Question 66

How would you handle the migration of a large dataset to a new version of your API?

Situation: API v1 has millions of records with schema A; v2 requires schema B. Need to migrate all historical data without downtime and keep both versions working during the transition.

Approach: dual-write during transition; backfill async in batches; shadow reads to verify; cut over incrementally.

  1. Add new columns/tables side-by-side — no destructive changes yet.
  2. Dual-write: application writes both old and new shapes in the same transaction.
  3. Backfill old data into new shape using idempotent batch jobs.
  4. Shadow read: v2 reads new shape; compare with old in logs; detect discrepancies.
  5. Flip reads to new shape behind a feature flag, percentage-based.
  6. Stop dual-writing once confident; new shape becomes source of truth.
  7. Drop old after grace period.
// migration: "address" field (single string) → "address" object (street, city, zip, country)

// PHASE 1: add new columns, keep old
// ALTER TABLE users ADD COLUMN address_street TEXT, ADD COLUMN address_city TEXT, ...

// PHASE 2: dual-write
async function updateAddress(userId, input) {
  const parts = parseAddress(input.addressString);              // best-effort parser
  await db.user.update({
    where: { id: userId },
    data: {
      addressString:  input.addressString,                       // old
      addressStreet:  parts.street,                              // new
      addressCity:    parts.city,
      addressZip:     parts.zip,
      addressCountry: parts.country,
    },
  });
}

// PHASE 3: backfill worker — idempotent, resumable
async function backfillAddresses() {
  const BATCH = 500;
  let cursor = await redis.get("migration:addr:cursor") ?? "0";

  while (true) {
    const users = await db.user.findMany({
      where: { id: { gt: cursor }, addressStreet: null, addressString: { not: null } },
      take: BATCH,
      orderBy: { id: "asc" },
    });
    if (users.length === 0) break;

    for (const user of users) {
      const parts = parseAddress(user.addressString);
      await db.user.update({
        where: { id: user.id },
        data:  { addressStreet: parts.street, addressCity: parts.city,
                 addressZip: parts.zip, addressCountry: parts.country },
      });
    }

    cursor = users[users.length - 1].id;
    await redis.set("migration:addr:cursor", cursor);
    await sleep(100);                                            // throttle
  }
}

// PHASE 4: shadow read — compare new vs parsed-old
function readAddress(user) {
  const newAddr = { street: user.addressStreet, city: user.addressCity, /* ... */ };

  if (process.env.SHADOW_COMPARE === "true") {
    queueMicrotask(() => {
      const parsed = parseAddress(user.addressString);
      if (!deepEqual(newAddr, parsed)) {
        log.warn({ userId: user.id, newAddr, parsed }, "address.migration.mismatch");
      }
    });
  }
  return newAddr;
}

Trade-offs: the backfill is the longest phase — for 100M rows at 500/sec, that's 55 hours; run in background with low priority; avoid spikes by rate-limiting. Batching size: too large = long-running transactions that block; too small = overhead dominates; usually 100-1000 per batch. Cursor-based resumption: store progress in Redis/DB so crashes don't restart from zero. Rollback strategy: dual-write means old shape is always current — if new shape breaks, roll back reads to old. Observability: shadow-read discrepancy rate is the key metric; when it hits zero, you're safe to cut over. Schema version per row: some migrations need a column like schema_version so workers can detect partially-migrated rows. Zero-downtime migrations require disciplined phases — rushing a big-bang migration is the fastest path to a very bad week.

Question 67

Describe how you would implement a custom analytics dashboard to monitor API usage.

Situation: product team wants a dashboard showing API usage per customer — request volume, errors, latency, top endpoints, revenue impact — in near-real-time for ops and quarterly rollups for account managers.

Approach: log every request to a time-series store; batch-aggregate for dashboards; expose via API or embed via iframe.

// 1. structured request log — emit per-request from middleware
app.use((req, res, next) => {
  const start = process.hrtime.bigint();
  res.on("finish", () => {
    const durationMs = Number(process.hrtime.bigint() - start) / 1e6;
    logger.info({
      type:        "api.request",
      ts:          Date.now(),
      method:      req.method,
      path:        req.route?.path || req.path,         // routes, not raw URLs (low cardinality)
      status:      res.statusCode,
      durationMs,
      userId:      req.user?.sub,
      tenantId:    req.user?.tenantId,
      apiKeyId:    req.user?.keyId,
      bytesOut:    Number(res.getHeader("Content-Length") || 0),
      userAgent:   req.headers["user-agent"],
    }, "api.request");
  });
  next();
});

// 2. pipe logs to time-series store
//    Option A: ClickHouse directly (best price/perf for this scale)
//    Option B: Vector/Fluent Bit → Kafka → ClickHouse
//    Option C: Tinybird (ClickHouse as a service), Axiom, Honeycomb

// 3. aggregation queries — rolled up in materialized views
// CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW usage_hourly_mv
// ENGINE = SummingMergeTree
// ORDER BY (tenantId, hour, endpoint)
// AS SELECT tenantId, toStartOfHour(ts) AS hour, path AS endpoint,
//           count() AS requests,
//           sumIf(1, status >= 500) AS errors_5xx,
//           quantile(0.99)(durationMs) AS p99,
//           sum(bytesOut) AS bytes
//    FROM api_requests
//    GROUP BY tenantId, hour, endpoint;

// 4. dashboard API — expose pre-aggregated data
app.get("/admin/analytics/usage", requireAdmin, async (req, res) => {
  const { tenantId, start, end, groupBy = "hour" } = req.query;
  const rows = await ch.query({
    query: `
      SELECT ${groupBy === "day" ? "toDate(ts)" : "toStartOfHour(ts)"} as bucket,
             count() as requests,
             sumIf(1, status >= 500) as errors,
             quantile(0.99)(durationMs) as p99
      FROM api_requests
      WHERE tenantId = {t:String} AND ts BETWEEN {s:DateTime} AND {e:DateTime}
      GROUP BY bucket ORDER BY bucket
    `,
    query_params: { t: tenantId, s: start, e: end },
    format: "JSONEachRow",
  });
  res.json({ data: await rows.json() });
});

// 5. per-customer dashboards via embeddable links
app.post("/admin/analytics/embed-token", requireAdmin, (req, res) => {
  const token = jwt.sign(
    { tenantId: req.body.tenantId, resource: "dashboard:usage", exp: Math.floor(Date.now()/1000)+3600 },
    EMBED_SECRET,
  );
  res.json({ url: `${DASHBOARD_URL}/embed?token=${token}` });
});

Trade-offs: data volume at scale: a medium API at 1000 req/s produces ~80M rows/day — Postgres will struggle; ClickHouse/Druid/Pinot handle billions cheaply. Cardinality matters: group by route (/users/:id) not path (/users/12345) to keep groups bounded. Sampling for very-high-volume APIs: keep 10% of successful requests, 100% of errors. Pre-aggregation: materialized views or scheduled rollups make dashboards fast; raw logs kept only for drill-down and short retention. Managed alternatives: Tinybird (ClickHouse), Axiom, Honeycomb, Datadog RUM, Grafana Cloud — removes most operational burden. BI tools (Metabase, Superset, Grafana, Preset, Lightdash) connect to analytics stores and build dashboards without custom code. Customer-facing dashboards need strict tenant isolation — always filter by tenantId server-side; never trust client-supplied IDs.

Question 68

Your API needs to support dynamic pricing based on user behavior. How would you implement this?

Situation: pricing changes with user behavior — returning customers see loyalty discounts; cart abandoners see a nudge; high-intent buyers pay full. Must be fair, legal, and fast.

Approach: server-side price calculation based on user context; pricing engine evaluates rules; results cached briefly for consistency.

// price calculation service
export async function calculatePrice(userId, productId, context = {}) {
  // load inputs
  const [user, product, history, cart] = await Promise.all([
    db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: userId }, include: { segments: true } }),
    db.product.findUnique({ where: { id: productId } }),
    getPurchaseHistory(userId),
    getCart(userId),
  ]);

  const signals = {
    userId,
    userTier:        user.tier,                        // free/pro/enterprise
    segments:        user.segments.map((s) => s.name),
    purchaseCount:   history.count,
    lastPurchaseDays: history.daysSinceLast,
    cartAgeMinutes:  cart?.ageMinutes ?? 0,
    referrer:        context.referrer,
    country:         context.country,
    timestamp:       Date.now(),
  };

  let price = product.basePrice;
  const adjustments = [];

  // rules engine — ordered, composable
  const rules = await db.pricingRule.findMany({
    where: { productId, active: true }, orderBy: { priority: "asc" },
  });

  for (const rule of rules) {
    if (evaluateCondition(rule.condition, signals)) {
      const delta = applyAction(rule.action, price, signals);
      if (delta !== 0) {
        price = Math.max(rule.floor ?? 0, price + delta);
        adjustments.push({ ruleId: rule.id, name: rule.name, delta });
      }
    }
  }

  // audit every priced event — traceable pricing is legally required in many jurisdictions
  await db.priceCalculation.create({
    data: {
      userId, productId, basePrice: product.basePrice,
      finalPrice: price, adjustments, signals,
    },
  });

  return { price, currency: product.currency, adjustments, validUntil: Date.now() + 15*60_000 };
}

// GET /products/:id/price — always server-computed, cached per user briefly
app.get("/products/:id/price", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const cacheKey = `price:${req.user.sub}:${req.params.id}`;
  const cached = await redis.get(cacheKey);
  if (cached) return res.json(JSON.parse(cached));

  const result = await calculatePrice(req.user.sub, req.params.id, {
    country: req.headers["cf-ipcountry"],
    referrer: req.get("referer"),
  });
  await redis.setex(cacheKey, 60, JSON.stringify(result));   // short TTL; prices change
  res.json(result);
});

// POST /cart — validates the priced amount hasn't drifted
app.post("/cart/items", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { productId, priceExpected } = req.body;
  const live = await calculatePrice(req.user.sub, productId);
  if (Math.abs(live.price - priceExpected) > 0.01) {
    return res.status(409).json({
      error: "price_changed",
      oldPrice: priceExpected, newPrice: live.price,
    });
  }
  // add to cart at live price, not client-supplied price
  await db.cartItem.create({ data: { userId: req.user.sub, productId, price: live.price } });
  res.status(201).json({ ok: true });
});

Trade-offs: price must be server-authoritative — never trust client-supplied prices; always recompute at checkout. Consistency: show the same price to the user over the browsing session (cached 5-15 min) — changing prices between cart-add and checkout erodes trust. Fairness & legality: dynamic pricing that targets protected attributes (race, gender, location-as-proxy) is illegal in many places (EU DSA, US consumer protection). Audit pricing logic; have legal review; keep explanations available on request. Transparency: show strike-through original price when discounted; explain why ("Loyalty discount"). Abuse: prevent users from price-shopping via incognito vs logged-in with sanity checks. A/B testing pricing requires special care — different users seeing different prices for the same product is exactly the behavior regulations scrutinize. Document the experimental design; require ethics review. Pricing engines (Stigg, Orb, Metronome, Lago) handle usage-based and tiered pricing out of the box; for rules-based dynamic pricing, custom engines remain common.

Question 69

How would you handle API security in a public cloud environment?

Situation: API runs on AWS/GCP/Azure. Multi-tenant cloud exposes new threats: shared hardware, public endpoints, misconfigured IAM, supply-chain vulnerabilities. Must secure end-to-end.

Approach: defense in depth — every layer has its own controls and audit; assume each layer can be compromised.

LayerControls
NetworkPrivate VPC; public endpoints only through ALB/Gateway; Security Groups as allowlists; no public DB
EdgeWAF with managed rulesets (OWASP, bot, account takeover); DDoS protection (Cloudflare, AWS Shield); rate limiting
ComputeImmutable infrastructure; minimal images (distroless); no SSH to prod; break-glass via SSM Session Manager with audit
IdentityIAM roles per service (no long-lived keys); OIDC federation for CI; least-privilege reviewed quarterly
SecretsSecrets Manager / Vault; rotate automatically; never in code or env files committed
DataEncryption at rest (KMS/customer-managed keys); encryption in transit (TLS 1.2+); column-level encryption for PII
ApplicationInput validation; output encoding; parameterized queries; CSRF tokens; CSP for any served HTML
Supply chainDependency scanning (Dependabot, Snyk); SBOM generation (Syft); image scanning (Trivy); signed images (cosign)
ObservabilityCentralized logs; audit trail of privileged ops; anomaly detection; SIEM integration
// terraform: minimum-viable hardened API service
resource "aws_security_group" "api" {
  name_prefix = "api-"
  vpc_id      = aws_vpc.main.id

  ingress {                                   # only from ALB
    from_port       = 8080
    to_port         = 8080
    protocol        = "tcp"
    security_groups = [aws_security_group.alb.id]
  }
  egress { from_port=0 to_port=0 protocol="-1" cidr_blocks=["0.0.0.0/0"] }
}

resource "aws_iam_role" "api_task" {            # per-service role
  assume_role_policy = data.aws_iam_policy_document.ecs_trust.json
}

resource "aws_iam_role_policy_attachment" "api_secrets" {
  role       = aws_iam_role.api_task.name
  policy_arn = aws_iam_policy.api_secrets.arn    # only reads api/* secrets
}

resource "aws_wafv2_web_acl" "api" {
  scope = "REGIONAL"
  default_action { allow {} }
  rule {                                       # AWS managed common protections
    name = "AWSManagedRulesCommonRuleSet"
    priority = 1
    override_action { none {} }
    statement { managed_rule_group_statement {
      name = "AWSManagedRulesCommonRuleSet"; vendor_name = "AWS"
    } }
    visibility_config { sampled_requests_enabled = true; metric_name = "common"; cloudwatch_metrics_enabled = true }
  }
}

Trade-offs: zero-trust networking is the direction of travel: no implicit trust between services; mTLS everywhere; short-lived credentials; SPIFFE/SPIRE for workload identity. Customer-managed keys (CMK) give enterprises the reassurance that they can revoke at any time; adds key-management complexity. Private APIs for internal use: services reachable only from within VPC; developers access via VPN/SSO-bastion. Compliance certifications (SOC 2, ISO 27001, HIPAA, PCI) become marketable; involve third-party auditors annually. Threat modeling per feature: STRIDE / LINDDUN during design, not post-facto. Runtime security (Falco, Tetragon, AWS GuardDuty, Datadog Security) detects anomalies in pod/container behavior. Incident response plan tested quarterly: playbooks for credential leak, breach detection, ransomware, DDoS.

Question 70

Describe how you would implement a feature that allows users to create and manage their own API keys.

Situation: customers want to integrate via scripts and CI pipelines; they need to create, rotate, and revoke API keys themselves with scoped permissions.

Approach: user-facing key management endpoints; prefixed keys (sk_live_) hashed at rest; scoped permissions; expiry and rotation; usage visibility.

// schema: ApiKey { id, userId, tokenPrefix, tokenHash, scopes, expiresAt, lastUsedAt, revokedAt, name, createdIp }

// CREATE — returns plaintext ONCE, server stores only hash
app.post("/api-keys", requireAuth, validate({ body: z.object({
  name:    z.string().min(1).max(100),
  scopes:  z.array(z.string()).min(1).max(20),
  expiresInDays: z.number().int().min(1).max(365).optional(),
}) }), async (req, res) => {
  const scopesAllowed = getAllowedScopes(req.user);
  for (const s of req.body.scopes) {
    if (!scopesAllowed.has(s)) return res.status(400).json({ error: `Scope ${s} not permitted` });
  }

  const env = process.env.STAGE === "production" ? "live" : "test";
  const raw = crypto.randomBytes(24).toString("base64url");
  const plaintext = `sk_${env}_${raw}`;
  const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(plaintext).digest("hex");

  const key = await db.apiKey.create({
    data: {
      userId:    req.user.sub,
      name:      req.body.name,
      scopes:    req.body.scopes,
      tokenPrefix: plaintext.slice(0, 12),              // for identification in UI
      tokenHash: hash,
      expiresAt: req.body.expiresInDays
        ? new Date(Date.now() + req.body.expiresInDays*86400_000)
        : null,
      createdIp: req.ip,
    },
  });

  // notify user — creation of API keys is sensitive
  await sendEmail(req.user.email, "API key created", { keyId: key.id, name: key.name });
  await logAudit({ userId: req.user.sub, action: "apikey.create", targetId: key.id });

  res.status(201).json({
    key: plaintext,                                      // ONLY time plaintext is returned
    id:  key.id,
    name: key.name,
    scopes: key.scopes,
    expiresAt: key.expiresAt,
    warning: "Store this key securely; it will not be shown again.",
  });
});

// LIST — never returns plaintext, only metadata
app.get("/api-keys", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const keys = await db.apiKey.findMany({
    where: { userId: req.user.sub, revokedAt: null },
    select: { id: true, name: true, tokenPrefix: true, scopes: true,
              lastUsedAt: true, createdAt: true, expiresAt: true },
  });
  res.json({ data: keys.map((k) => ({ ...k, keyPreview: `${k.tokenPrefix}...` })) });
});

// REVOKE
app.delete("/api-keys/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const key = await db.apiKey.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!key || key.userId !== req.user.sub) return res.sendStatus(404);
  await db.apiKey.update({
    where: { id: key.id },
    data: { revokedAt: new Date(), revokedIp: req.ip },
  });
  await logAudit({ userId: req.user.sub, action: "apikey.revoke", targetId: key.id });
  res.sendStatus(204);
});

// middleware — verify API key on each request
export async function authenticateApiKey(req, res, next) {
  const [, token] = (req.headers.authorization || "").split(" ");
  if (!token?.startsWith("sk_")) return next();          // not an API key; try session auth

  const hash = crypto.createHash("sha256").update(token).digest("hex");
  const key = await redis.get(`apikey:${hash}`) ?? JSON.stringify(
    await db.apiKey.findUnique({ where: { tokenHash: hash } })
  );
  const parsed = JSON.parse(key);
  if (!parsed || parsed.revokedAt || (parsed.expiresAt && new Date(parsed.expiresAt) < new Date())) {
    return res.status(401).json({ error: "invalid_key" });
  }
  req.user = { sub: parsed.userId, via: "api_key", keyId: parsed.id, scopes: parsed.scopes };

  // update lastUsedAt asynchronously (throttled)
  queueMicrotask(() => throttledTouch(parsed.id));
  next();
}

Trade-offs: hash storage prevents breach impact — if DB leaks, keys can't be reused. Plaintext returned once; if user loses it, they rotate. Prefixes (sk_live_, sk_test_) let you grep code for keys accidentally committed and distinguish environments. GitHub secret scanning partnership detects leaked keys in public repos and auto-revokes — Stripe, AWS, and many others integrate; format prefixes make detection reliable. Scopes minimize blast radius: read-only scope for analytics pipelines; write scope only where needed. Expiry: default 90 days; customer overrides; expired keys auto-revoke. Rotation UX: allow creation of new key before revoking old (overlap window). Audit trail: every key usage logged with timestamp/IP; suspicious patterns alert. Webhook signing: separate signing keys from request keys; different threat model. Limits per user: 10-50 keys max prevents accidental runaway.

Question 71

Your API needs to support real-time geolocation tracking. How would you design this?

Situation: delivery app, ride-share, or field service needs real-time location from drivers/workers — update every few seconds, visible to dispatchers and customers, history stored for analytics.

Approach: clients batch-upload location samples over HTTP (or WebSocket for active tracking); server stores in time-series DB; subscribers receive updates via SSE/WebSocket; geospatial indexing for "drivers near me."

// 1. CLIENT sends location samples — batched for efficiency
// POST /locations
// { samples: [{ ts, lat, lng, accuracy, speed, heading }, ...] }
app.post("/locations", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const userId = req.user.sub;
  const samples = req.body.samples?.slice(0, 100) ?? [];       // limit batch size

  // clamp timestamps to prevent future-dating
  const now = Date.now();
  const cleaned = samples.filter((s) =>
    typeof s.lat === "number" && typeof s.lng === "number" &&
    Math.abs(s.lat) <= 90 && Math.abs(s.lng) <= 180 &&
    s.ts > now - 86400_000 && s.ts < now + 60_000
  );

  // time-series store: TimescaleDB or InfluxDB
  await db.$executeRaw`
    INSERT INTO location_samples (user_id, ts, lat, lng, accuracy, speed, heading)
    VALUES ${Prisma.join(cleaned.map((s) =>
      Prisma.sql`(${userId}, to_timestamp(${s.ts/1000}), ${s.lat}, ${s.lng}, ${s.accuracy ?? null}, ${s.speed ?? null}, ${s.heading ?? null})`
    ))}
  `;

  // latest location cache + geospatial index — Redis GEO
  const latest = cleaned[cleaned.length - 1];
  if (latest) {
    await redis.multi()
      .geoadd("drivers:active", latest.lng, latest.lat, userId)
      .hset(`driver:${userId}:last`, {
        lat: latest.lat, lng: latest.lng, ts: latest.ts, heading: latest.heading ?? "",
      })
      .expire(`driver:${userId}:last`, 60)                // expire if driver stops pinging
      .publish(`loc:${userId}`, JSON.stringify(latest))
      .exec();
  }

  res.sendStatus(204);
});

// 2. SUBSCRIBE — dispatcher / customer follows a specific driver
app.get("/drivers/:id/stream", requireAuth, authorizeDispatcher, async (req, res) => {
  res.set({
    "Content-Type":      "text/event-stream",
    "Cache-Control":     "no-store",
    "X-Accel-Buffering": "no",
  }).flushHeaders();

  // send latest known
  const last = await redis.hgetall(`driver:${req.params.id}:last`);
  if (last.ts) res.write(`event: location\ndata: ${JSON.stringify(last)}\n\n`);

  const sub = redis.duplicate();
  await sub.subscribe(`loc:${req.params.id}`);
  sub.on("message", (_, msg) => res.write(`event: location\ndata: ${msg}\n\n`));

  const heart = setInterval(() => res.write(":hb\n\n"), 20_000);
  req.on("close", () => { clearInterval(heart); sub.unsubscribe(); sub.quit(); });
});

// 3. NEARBY — drivers within radius
app.get("/drivers/nearby", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { lat, lng, radiusKm = 3 } = req.query;
  const results = await redis.geosearch(
    "drivers:active", "FROMLONLAT", lng, lat,
    "BYRADIUS", radiusKm, "km", "ASC", "COUNT", 50, "WITHCOORD",
  );
  res.json({
