    data: results.map(([driverId, [dLng, dLat]]) => ({ driverId, lat: Number(dLat), lng: Number(dLng) })),
  });
});

Trade-offs: frequency vs battery: 1Hz sampling drains phones; adaptive sampling (more when moving fast, less when stationary) saves battery significantly. Privacy: location is sensitive; store only as long as needed (trips archived, raw samples aged out); allow users to see and delete history; masking for shared views (customer sees driver direction but not full trail). Accuracy & filtering: GPS samples are noisy; Kalman filters or server-side smoothing clean trajectories before display. Geospatial queries at scale: Redis GEO handles thousands of active drivers; for millions, partition by region (city-level keys) or use PostGIS / H3 grid cells. Data retention: raw samples ~30 days in hot store; aggregated trips forever; legal minimums for dispute resolution (ride complaints). Bandwidth: SSE over HTTP/2 multiplexes well; WebSocket for bidirectional control (e.g., remote pause). Geofencing: triggers when driver enters a zone — implement server-side with spatial joins on PostGIS or Redis GEORADIUS checks.

Question 72

How would you implement a feature that allows users to schedule automated backups of their data?

Situation: customers want to schedule automatic backups of their data — daily full, hourly incremental — with retention rules, download access, and restore capability.

Approach: backup as a scheduled async job writing to object storage; per-customer retention policies; signed download links; tested restore procedure.

// schedule model
// BackupSchedule { userId, enabled, frequency: "hourly"|"daily"|"weekly", time, retention: int, includeMedia: bool }

// CREATE / UPDATE schedule
app.put("/backups/schedule", requireAuth, validate({ body: ScheduleSchema }), async (req, res) => {
  const s = await db.backupSchedule.upsert({
    where:  { userId: req.user.sub },
    create: { userId: req.user.sub, ...req.body },
    update: req.body,
  });
  res.json(s);
});

// TRIGGER — called by scheduler (e.g., pg-boss cron, Temporal, EventBridge)
async function runBackup(userId, type = "manual") {
  const schedule = await db.backupSchedule.findUnique({ where: { userId } });
  if (type !== "manual" && !schedule?.enabled) return;

  const backup = await db.backup.create({
    data: { userId, type, status: "running", startedAt: new Date() },
  });

  try {
    const key = `backups/${userId}/${backup.id}.tar.gz`;
    const archive = archiver("tar", { gzip: true, gzipOptions: { level: 6 } });
    const upload = new Upload({
      client: s3,
      params: { Bucket: BACKUP_BUCKET, Key: key, Body: archive, ServerSideEncryption: "aws:kms",
                SSEKMSKeyId: CMK_ARN, StorageClass: "STANDARD_IA" },
    });

    // stream tables per chunk
    for (const table of ["items","notes","categories","settings"]) {
      archive.append(stringifyJsonl(streamRows(table, userId)), { name: `${table}.jsonl` });
    }

    if (schedule?.includeMedia) {
      for await (const media of streamMediaMetadata(userId)) {
        archive.append(JSON.stringify(media), { name: `media/${media.id}.json` });
      }
    }

    // manifest
    archive.append(JSON.stringify({
      version: "1",
      userId,
      createdAt: new Date().toISOString(),
      tables: ["items","notes","categories","settings"],
    }, null, 2), { name: "manifest.json" });

    archive.finalize();
    const { Location } = await upload.done();
    const head = await s3.send(new HeadObjectCommand({ Bucket: BACKUP_BUCKET, Key: key }));

    await db.backup.update({
      where: { id: backup.id },
      data: {
        status: "ready", completedAt: new Date(),
        s3Key: key, sizeBytes: Number(head.ContentLength),
      },
    });

    // retention — trim old
    await trimBackupsToRetention(userId, schedule?.retention ?? 14);
  } catch (err) {
    await db.backup.update({
      where: { id: backup.id },
      data: { status: "failed", error: err.message, failedAt: new Date() },
    });
    throw err;
  }
}

// DOWNLOAD
app.get("/backups/:id/download", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const b = await db.backup.findFirst({
    where: { id: req.params.id, userId: req.user.sub, status: "ready" },
  });
  if (!b) return res.sendStatus(404);
  const url = await getSignedUrl(s3, new GetObjectCommand({
    Bucket: BACKUP_BUCKET, Key: b.s3Key,
    ResponseContentDisposition: `attachment; filename="backup-${b.id}.tar.gz"`,
  }), { expiresIn: 3600 });
  res.redirect(302, url);
});

// RESTORE — dangerous, so require explicit confirmation
app.post("/backups/:id/restore", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  if (req.body.confirm !== "RESTORE") return res.status(400).json({ error: "confirm=RESTORE required" });
  const job = await queue.add("backup.restore", { userId: req.user.sub, backupId: req.params.id });
  res.status(202).json({ jobId: job.id });
});

Trade-offs: incremental vs full: full backups are simpler but expensive; incrementals (only changes since last) save space but restore requires chain of incrementals on top of last full. For most SaaS, daily full is simpler and usually fine; incremental becomes important at TB scale. Encryption: server-side encryption with KMS (customer-managed keys for enterprise tier), additional client-side encryption if users hold the key (zero-knowledge backup — you can't read their data even if compelled). Retention tiers: recent backups in hot storage (STANDARD); older in cheaper (STANDARD_IA, GLACIER). Restore testing: the only backup that works is the one you've restored; quarterly drills verify. Partial restore: let users restore specific entities ("my Nov 10 backup's notes, not my settings"). Legal holds: retention can't delete records under active legal hold. Limits: rate-limit backup creation; prevent runaway storage costs; tie to user tier.

Question 73

Describe how you would handle version conflicts in a distributed API environment.

Situation: API is distributed across services/regions; same resource may be updated concurrently from different places. Version conflicts (one client overwrites another's changes) cause data loss.

Approach: optimistic concurrency via ETag/version; last-write-wins only when safe; CRDTs for fields that naturally merge; explicit conflict resolution for the rest.

// OPTIMISTIC CONCURRENCY via ETag / version number

// READ returns ETag
app.get("/documents/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const doc = await db.document.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!doc) return res.sendStatus(404);
  res.set("ETag", `W/"v${doc.version}"`).json(doc);
});

// UPDATE requires matching If-Match header
app.patch("/documents/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const expected = req.headers["if-match"];
  if (!expected) return res.status(428).json({ error: "If-Match header required" });

  const match = /^W\/"v(\d+)"$/.exec(expected);
  if (!match) return res.status(400).json({ error: "Invalid If-Match" });
  const expectedVersion = Number(match[1]);

  const result = await db.document.updateMany({
    where: { id: req.params.id, version: expectedVersion, ownerId: req.user.sub },
    data:  { ...req.body, version: { increment: 1 } },
  });

  if (result.count === 0) {
    const current = await db.document.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
    return res.status(412).json({
      error: "version_conflict",
      currentVersion: current?.version,
      yourVersion:    expectedVersion,
    });
  }

  const updated = await db.document.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  res.set("ETag", `W/"v${updated.version}"`).json(updated);
});

// LAST-WRITE-WINS with Lamport clock — acceptable for single-value fields where latest is fine
async function setUserPreference(userId, key, value) {
  const lamport = Date.now();                             // simplified; real LWW uses node-id tiebreakers
  await db.preference.upsert({
    where:  { userId_key: { userId, key } },
    create: { userId, key, value, lamport },
    update: {
      value:   value,
      lamport: { set: lamport },
      // NOTE: update only if new lamport is bigger
      // use raw SQL or conditional update for correct semantics
    },
  });
}

// FIELD-LEVEL MERGE — different fields can be merged independently
async function mergeDocumentUpdates(id, updates) {
  // compare-and-swap loop
  for (let attempt = 0; attempt < 3; attempt++) {
    const current = await db.document.findUnique({ where: { id } });
    const merged = { ...current };

    for (const [field, { value, clientVersion }] of Object.entries(updates)) {
      if (clientVersion >= (current.fieldVersions?.[field] ?? 0)) {
        merged[field] = value;
        merged.fieldVersions = { ...(merged.fieldVersions ?? {}), [field]: clientVersion + 1 };
      }
      // else: keep server value (newer wins on that field)
    }

    const result = await db.document.updateMany({
      where: { id, version: current.version },
      data:  { ...merged, version: { increment: 1 } },
    });
    if (result.count === 1) return merged;
  }
  throw new Error("Conflict: retries exhausted");
}

Trade-offs: optimistic locking is simple and correct for conflicting-edit prevention; users see a 412 and reload + reapply. User experience of 412 matters — a three-way merge UI (your version / server version / merged) is valuable for collaborative tools; a "reload and redo" dialog is acceptable for less-collaborative contexts. CRDTs remove conflicts entirely for data types that merge mathematically (counters, sets, text) — see Yjs, Automerge. Field-level versioning avoids full-document conflicts when users edit different fields simultaneously. Distributed timestamps alone aren't safe (clock skew); hybrid logical clocks (HLC) or vector clocks are the correct primitives. Read-your-writes: clients should see their own updates reflected immediately; sticky session to primary DB or read-your-writes consistency (from read replicas tagged with write LSN). Conflict analytics: track conflict rate per endpoint; if >1%, the data model probably needs redesign or UI adjustment.

Question 74

Your API needs to support voice commands and interactions. How would you implement this?

Situation: product wants voice interactions — Alexa skills, Google Assistant actions, in-app voice commands. The API must handle speech-to-text, intent matching, and text-to-speech responses.

Approach: offload ASR/TTS to specialized services (Google STT, Whisper, ElevenLabs, Deepgram); API exposes intent-based endpoints; voice platforms are adapters, not the core.

// intent-based API — consumed by both voice adapters and GUI
app.post("/intents/resolve", authenticateAny, validate({ body: z.object({
  intent:     z.string(),                         // "order.status", "task.create"
  entities:   z.record(z.any()).default({}),      // slots extracted by NLU
  sessionId:  z.string().optional(),
  locale:     z.string().default("en-US"),
}) }), async (req, res) => {
  const { intent, entities, sessionId, locale } = req.body;
  const handler = INTENT_HANDLERS[intent];
  if (!handler) return res.status(400).json({ error: "unknown_intent" });

  const result = await handler({ user: req.user, entities, sessionId });

  // respond in voice-and-visual format
  res.json({
    speech: t(result.speechKey, result.params, locale),      // plain text for TTS
    ssml:   t(result.ssmlKey, result.params, locale),        // with prosody / pauses
    visual: result.card,                                     // for screens
    actions: result.suggestedActions,                        // follow-up intents
    sessionState: result.sessionState,                       // multi-turn context
  });
});

// ASR/TTS pass-through — the API can chain for clients without native speech
app.post("/voice/transcribe",
  requireAuth,
  express.raw({ type: "audio/*", limit: "10mb" }),
  async (req, res) => {
    // Deepgram, Whisper, Google STT — streaming preferred for large audio
    const transcript = await deepgram.transcribe({ buffer: req.body, language: req.query.lang });
    res.json({ text: transcript.text, confidence: transcript.confidence });
  }
);

app.post("/voice/synthesize", requireAuth, validate({ body: z.object({
  text:  z.string().max(2000),
  voice: z.enum(["aria","joe","bella"]).default("aria"),
  format: z.enum(["mp3","wav"]).default("mp3"),
}) }), async (req, res) => {
  const stream = await elevenlabs.synthesize(req.body);
  res.type(`audio/${req.body.format}`).send(await streamToBuffer(stream));
});

// voice-platform adapters — translate to intent API
// ALEXA skill handler
app.post("/integrations/alexa", verifyAlexa, async (req, res) => {
  const { request, session } = req.body;
  if (request.type === "IntentRequest") {
    const mapped = {
      intent:    mapAlexaIntent(request.intent.name),
      entities:  extractSlots(request.intent.slots),
      sessionId: session.sessionId,
      locale:    request.locale,
    };
    const user = await resolveUserFromAlexaToken(session.user.accessToken);
    const result = await resolveIntent({ user, body: mapped });
    res.json(toAlexaResponse(result));
  }
});

Trade-offs: intent-first design — voice and visual are both UIs over the same API. An order.status intent works for voice ("where's my order") and GUI ("check order status" button). Coupling the API to any one voice platform is a trap. Latency: voice demands sub-1-second response; streaming speech synthesis and partial ASR are worth the complexity. Context across turns: session state tracks what the user was asking about; pass sessionId so "what about the second one?" works. Error UX: voice errors are hard (can't show a dialog); prefer "I didn't catch that, did you mean X or Y?" over bare failure. Privacy: audio is sensitive; server-side retention only as needed for debugging; explicit opt-in for improving models. Accents & languages: ASR quality varies wildly; measure per-locale error rate. LLM-powered NLU (GPT-4o, Claude) is increasingly standard for intent extraction; older intent/slot frameworks (Dialogflow, Rasa) are being replaced.

Question 75

How would you design an API that supports dynamic form generation and validation?

Situation: forms in the product vary per tenant or per record type — onboarding forms, case intake forms, configuration wizards. Hard-coding UIs doesn't scale. Need forms rendered from API-provided schema with server-side validation.

Approach: JSON Schema defines the form; clients render UIs from it; server validates submissions against the same schema; reusable across platforms.

// API returns a form descriptor
app.get("/forms/:formId", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const form = await db.form.findUnique({
    where: { id: req.params.formId },
    include: { fields: { orderBy: { order: "asc" } } },
  });
  if (!form) return res.sendStatus(404);

  // produce JSON Schema + UI hints
  const schema = {
    $schema: "https://json-schema.org/draft/2020-12/schema",
    title:   form.title,
    type:    "object",
    properties: {},
    required: [],
  };
  const uiSchema = {};

  for (const field of form.fields) {
    schema.properties[field.key] = buildFieldSchema(field);
    if (field.required) schema.required.push(field.key);
    uiSchema[field.key] = {
      "ui:widget":      field.widget,                     // text, textarea, select, date, file, ...
      "ui:placeholder": field.placeholder,
      "ui:help":        field.help,
      "ui:order":       field.order,
    };
  }

  res.json({
    schema,
    uiSchema,
    submitTo: `/forms/${form.id}/submissions`,
  });
});

function buildFieldSchema(f) {
  const base = { title: f.label, description: f.help };
  switch (f.type) {
    case "text":   return { ...base, type: "string", minLength: f.minLength, maxLength: f.maxLength,
                            pattern: f.pattern };
    case "number": return { ...base, type: "number", minimum: f.min, maximum: f.max };
    case "date":   return { ...base, type: "string", format: "date" };
    case "email":  return { ...base, type: "string", format: "email" };
    case "enum":   return { ...base, type: "string", enum: f.options.map((o) => o.value),
                            enumNames: f.options.map((o) => o.label) };
    case "array":  return { ...base, type: "array", items: buildFieldSchema(f.itemSchema), maxItems: f.maxItems };
    case "file":   return { ...base, type: "string", format: "data-url" };   // or pre-signed upload
    default: throw new Error("Unknown field type");
  }
}

// submission — server validates against the same schema
import Ajv from "ajv";
import addFormats from "ajv-formats";

const ajv = new Ajv({ allErrors: true, strict: true });
addFormats(ajv);

app.post("/forms/:formId/submissions", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const form = await db.form.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.formId }, include: { fields: true } });
  if (!form) return res.sendStatus(404);

  const schema = buildFullSchema(form);
  const validate = ajv.compile(schema);
  if (!validate(req.body)) {
    return res.status(422).json({
      error: "validation_failed",
      issues: validate.errors.map((e) => ({
        field:   e.instancePath.replace(/^\//, ""),
        code:    e.keyword,
        message: e.message,
      })),
    });
  }

  // custom business-rule validation — beyond pure JSON Schema
  const businessErrors = await runBusinessRules(form, req.body, req.user);
  if (businessErrors.length) return res.status(422).json({ error: "validation_failed", issues: businessErrors });

  const submission = await db.formSubmission.create({
    data: { formId: form.id, userId: req.user.sub, values: req.body, submittedAt: new Date() },
  });
  res.status(201).json(submission);
});

Trade-offs: JSON Schema is the lingua franca — clients like @rjsf/core, formily, json-schema-form render forms automatically; same schema validates server-side with Ajv. Pure schema isn't enough: conditional fields (show X only if Y=true), cross-field validation ("start < end"), async validation (uniqueness) need additional machinery — encoded in allOf/if/then, or handled via UI expressions + server-side business rules. Versioning: form schemas evolve; stored submissions reference the schema version they were submitted against so re-display works correctly. File uploads: for big files, don't data-url them in-form — use pre-signed upload flow; form stores the resulting object key. Performance: compile schemas once, cache the validator per formId. Editors: business users build forms in an admin UI; schema is stored as JSON and edited via drag-and-drop; developers extend with custom field types. Alternatives: Formio, Tally, Typeform, Fillout provide this end-to-end as a service.

Question 76

Describe how you would implement a custom notification system for your API.

Situation: users need notifications across channels — in-app, email, SMS, push, Slack — configurable per-event-type and per-user preference; some urgent (outage), some optional (weekly digest).

Approach: event-driven. Services emit domain events; a notification service subscribes, matches per-user preferences and routes to channels; delivery is async.

// domain service emits events
async function placeOrder(order) {
  await db.order.create({ data: order });
  await eventBus.publish("order.placed", {
    orderId: order.id,
    userId:  order.userId,
    total:   order.total,
  });
}

// notification service — subscribes and routes
eventBus.on("order.placed", async (event) => {
  const template = await db.notificationTemplate.findFirst({
    where: { eventType: "order.placed", active: true },
  });
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({
    where: { id: event.userId },
    include: { notificationPreferences: true, devices: true },
  });

  // determine channels based on preferences
  const channels = resolveChannels(user, template);

  for (const channel of channels) {
    await queue.add("notification.deliver", {
      channel, userId: user.id, templateId: template.id, eventData: event,
    }, {
      attempts: 5, backoff: { type: "exponential", delay: 1000 },
    });
  }

  // always write to in-app notification feed
  await db.notification.create({
    data: {
      userId: user.id, type: "order.placed", title: render(template.title, event),
      body: render(template.body, event), data: event, createdAt: new Date(),
    },
  });
});

function resolveChannels(user, template) {
  const channels = [];
  const prefs = user.notificationPreferences;
  const eventPref = prefs.find((p) => p.eventType === template.eventType);

  // urgency overrides preferences — outage alerts always go everywhere
  if (template.urgency === "critical") return ["email", "sms", "push", "inApp"];

  if (eventPref?.email   ?? template.defaults.email)  channels.push("email");
  if (eventPref?.sms     ?? template.defaults.sms)    channels.push("sms");
  if (eventPref?.push    ?? template.defaults.push && user.devices.length) channels.push("push");
  if (eventPref?.slack   ?? template.defaults.slack && user.slackWebhook) channels.push("slack");

  return channels;
}

// delivery worker
queue.process("notification.deliver", async (job) => {
  const { channel, userId, templateId, eventData } = job.data;
  const user = await db.user.findUnique({ where: { id: userId } });
  const template = await db.notificationTemplate.findUnique({ where: { id: templateId } });

  const delivery = await db.notificationDelivery.create({
    data: { userId, channel, templateId, status: "attempting" },
  });

  try {
    if (channel === "email") {
      await sendgrid.send({
        to: user.email,
        from: "noreply@example.com",
        subject: render(template.subject, eventData),
        html: render(template.htmlBody, eventData),
      });
    } else if (channel === "sms") {
      await twilio.messages.create({
        to: user.phone, from: TWILIO_FROM,
        body: render(template.smsBody, eventData),
      });
    } else if (channel === "push") {
      await pushService.send(user.id, {
        title: render(template.title, eventData),
        body:  render(template.body,  eventData),
      });
    } else if (channel === "slack") {
      await fetch(user.slackWebhook, {
        method: "POST", headers: { "Content-Type": "application/json" },
        body: JSON.stringify({ text: render(template.slackBody, eventData) }),
      });
    }

    await db.notificationDelivery.update({
      where: { id: delivery.id },
      data: { status: "delivered", deliveredAt: new Date() },
    });
  } catch (err) {
    await db.notificationDelivery.update({
      where: { id: delivery.id },
      data: { status: "failed", error: err.message, failedAt: new Date() },
    });
    throw err;                                        // retried by queue
  }
});

Trade-offs: templates with variables (Handlebars, Liquid) separate content from code — product managers edit copy without deploys. Preferences granularity: per-event-type × per-channel is the minimum; power users want quiet hours, frequency caps, category grouping. Deduplication: 10 "new comment" events in 1 minute should become one digest. Delivery receipts: track delivered vs opened vs clicked per channel; feed into analytics. Unsubscribe is legally required on email/SMS (CAN-SPAM, GDPR); include one-click unsubscribe headers. Managed platforms: Courier, Knock, Engagespot, Magicbell, Novu handle multi-channel routing, preferences, templates, and in-app feeds out of the box — saves building a miniature CRM. Audit trail: every notification attempt, outcome, and preference change logged for support and compliance.

Question 77

Your API needs to integrate with IoT devices. How would you handle this integration?

Situation: IoT devices (sensors, thermostats, cameras) send readings and receive commands through the API. Volume is high (thousands to millions of devices), connectivity unreliable, device credentials must be managed.

Approach: MQTT (or equivalent pub/sub) for telemetry and commands; API layer for provisioning, state, and dashboards; per-device credentials.

// DEVICE PROVISIONING via API
// Returns device credentials used to connect to the MQTT broker
app.post("/devices", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { serialNumber, deviceType, firmwareVersion } = req.body;

  // prevent duplicate registration — same serialNumber + owner
  const existing = await db.device.findFirst({
    where: { serialNumber, ownerId: req.user.sub },
  });
  if (existing) return res.status(409).json({ error: "already_registered" });

  const device = await db.device.create({
    data: {
      serialNumber, deviceType, firmwareVersion,
      ownerId: req.user.sub,
      mqttClientId: `dev_${crypto.randomUUID()}`,
    },
  });

  // issue X.509 cert OR generate MQTT username/password
  const cert = await ca.issueCertificate({ subject: { CN: device.mqttClientId },
                                            ttlDays: 365 });
  await db.deviceCredential.create({
    data: {
      deviceId: device.id,
      certFingerprint: cert.fingerprint,
      certExpiresAt: cert.notAfter,
    },
  });

  // topic ACL — device can only publish/subscribe to its own topics
  await aclStore.grant(device.mqttClientId, {
    publish:   [`tenants/${req.user.tenantId}/devices/${device.id}/telemetry/+`,
                `tenants/${req.user.tenantId}/devices/${device.id}/events`],
    subscribe: [`tenants/${req.user.tenantId}/devices/${device.id}/commands/+`,
                `tenants/${req.user.tenantId}/devices/${device.id}/config`],
  });

  res.status(201).json({
    deviceId: device.id,
    mqttEndpoint: "mqtts://mqtt.example.com:8883",
    clientId:  device.mqttClientId,
    certificatePem: cert.certPem,
    privateKeyPem:  cert.privateKeyPem,
    caCertPem:      cert.caPem,
  });
});

// COMMAND — publish from API to device via MQTT
app.post("/devices/:id/commands", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const device = await db.device.findFirst({ where: { id: req.params.id, ownerId: req.user.sub } });
  if (!device) return res.sendStatus(404);

  const commandId = crypto.randomUUID();
  await db.command.create({
    data: { id: commandId, deviceId: device.id, payload: req.body, status: "pending" },
  });

  await mqttClient.publishAsync(
    `tenants/${req.user.tenantId}/devices/${device.id}/commands/${req.body.type}`,
    JSON.stringify({ commandId, ...req.body }),
    { qos: 1 },
  );

  res.status(202).json({ commandId, status: "pending" });
});

// TELEMETRY INGEST — subscribe to all devices' telemetry topics
mqttClient.subscribe("tenants/+/devices/+/telemetry/#", { qos: 1 });
mqttClient.on("message", async (topic, payload) => {
  const [, tenantId, , deviceId, , metric] = topic.split("/");
  const data = JSON.parse(payload.toString());

  // write to time-series store
  await tsdb.write({
    measurement: metric,
    tags: { tenantId, deviceId },
    fields: data.values,
    time:   data.ts ? new Date(data.ts) : new Date(),
  });

  // anomaly detection
  if (data.values.temperature > 50) {
    await triggerAlert(deviceId, "high_temperature", data);
  }
});

// DEVICE STATE — current state query
app.get("/devices/:id/state", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const state = await tsdb.query(`
    SELECT last("value") FROM "temperature","humidity","battery"
    WHERE deviceId = :id AND time > now() - 1h GROUP BY metric
  `, { id: req.params.id });
  res.json(state);
});

Trade-offs: MQTT is the standard for IoT (low overhead, pub/sub, supports QoS, durable sessions) — managed: AWS IoT Core, HiveMQ Cloud, EMQX Cloud, Google Cloud IoT (EoL), Azure IoT Hub. Time-series DB (InfluxDB, TimescaleDB, QuestDB, ClickHouse) handles billions of data points; PostgreSQL drowns at this volume. Device identity: per-device X.509 certs (mTLS) are preferred over shared credentials — one device compromise doesn't breach all; AWS IoT Core, Azure IoT Hub handle CA. Firmware updates (OTA): API publishes update manifests; devices check and download from signed URL; rollback on failure; gradual rollout (5%, 50%, 100%). Offline devices: queue commands (retained or LWT messages in MQTT); devices process on reconnect. Scalability: brokers scale to millions of connections; ingest pipelines (Kafka in between MQTT and TSDB) smooth bursts. Edge computing (Greengrass, Azure Edge): devices process locally and sync aggregates, saving bandwidth for remote sites.

Question 78

How would you implement a feature that allows users to customize their API endpoints?

Situation: enterprise customers want to extend the API with custom endpoints — their own workflows, integrations, and business logic — without forking. Think "webhook + script runner + route handler."

Approach: sandboxed script execution (serverless runtime) mapped to user-defined routes; declarative routing in the customer's config; resource limits and audit.

// CUSTOMER declares an endpoint
// POST /orgs/:orgId/endpoints
// {
//   "path": "/inventory/sync",
//   "method": "POST",
//   "runtime": "js",
//   "code": "export default async function(ctx) { ... }",
//   "timeoutMs": 5000,
//   "scopes": ["inventory:write"]
// }

app.post("/orgs/:orgId/endpoints", requireOrgAdmin, validate({ body: EndpointSchema }),
  async (req, res) => {
    const { path, method, runtime, code, timeoutMs, scopes } = req.body;

    // validate: path under /custom/, no conflicts, code compiles
    if (!/^\/[a-z0-9\-_/]+$/.test(path)) return res.status(400).json({ error: "invalid_path" });
    if (await db.customEndpoint.findFirst({ where: { orgId: req.params.orgId, path, method } })) {
      return res.status(409).json({ error: "conflict" });
    }

    // pre-compile / lint — fail-fast if syntax broken
    try { await runtimeProvider[runtime].validate(code); }
    catch (err) { return res.status(400).json({ error: "code_invalid", detail: err.message }); }

    const endpoint = await db.customEndpoint.create({
      data: { ...req.body, orgId: req.params.orgId, createdBy: req.user.sub, version: 1 },
    });
    res.status(201).json(endpoint);
  }
);

// ROUTING — dispatcher matches org + path + method
app.all("/custom/:orgSlug/*", requireAuth, async (req, res, next) => {
  const org = await db.organization.findUnique({ where: { slug: req.params.orgSlug } });
  if (!org) return res.sendStatus(404);

  const path   = "/" + req.params[0];
  const method = req.method;
  const endpoint = await db.customEndpoint.findFirst({
    where: { orgId: org.id, path, method, active: true },
  });
  if (!endpoint) return res.sendStatus(404);

  // scope check — token must have permissions
  if (!endpoint.scopes.every((s) => req.user.scopes.includes(s))) {
    return res.status(403).json({ error: "insufficient_scope" });
  }

  try {
    const ctx = buildSandboxContext(req, endpoint);         // safe req/res surface
    const result = await runtimeProvider[endpoint.runtime].execute({
      code:      endpoint.code,
      ctx,
      timeoutMs: endpoint.timeoutMs,
      memoryMB:  128,
    });
    res.status(result.status ?? 200).set(result.headers).json(result.body);
  } catch (err) {
    if (err.code === "timeout") return res.status(504).json({ error: "script_timeout" });
    if (err.code === "oom")     return res.status(500).json({ error: "memory_limit" });
    next(err);
  }
});

// sandboxed context — tightly scoped. Options for runtimes:
// - isolated-vm (V8 isolates, Node-side)
// - Cloudflare Workers / Deno Deploy / Vercel Edge (multi-tenant at platform level)
// - Lambda with per-tenant functions (slow cold start, but ironclad isolation)
// - WebAssembly (wasmtime, WASI) — fastest startup, least mature tooling
function buildSandboxContext(req, endpoint) {
  return {
    request: {
      method:  req.method,
      headers: sanitizeHeaders(req.headers),
      query:   req.query,
      body:    req.body,
    },
    env:    endpoint.env,                       // per-endpoint env vars
    fetch:  allowlistedFetch(endpoint.allowedHosts),  // outbound calls restricted
    store:  scopedStore(endpoint.orgId),        // key-value with tenant quota
  };
}

Trade-offs: execution model is the hard part: untrusted code + multi-tenant = sandboxing is non-negotiable. isolated-vm (V8 isolates) gives ~5ms startup, strong isolation in-process; used by Cloudflare Workers. Container-per-tenant (Lambda) is ironclad but cold starts hurt; Firecracker microVMs (AWS Lambda internals, Fly Machines) are fast enough. WebAssembly (wasmtime) is the 2026 frontier: sub-millisecond startup, multi-language, capability-based security. Resource limits: CPU, memory, execution time, outbound bytes, outbound hosts — all must be enforced by the runtime, not trusted from the code. Observability: logs per invocation scoped to the endpoint owner; surface errors in their dashboard. Version history + rollback: code changes version; previous versions callable by ID. Alternatives: webhook-only (customer's code runs on their infrastructure; you call out) avoids sandboxing entirely but pushes operational burden to them. Low-code platforms (n8n, Workato, Zapier, Make) are fine for non-technical users; custom code endpoints target developer customers.

Question 79

Describe how you would handle large-scale data processing and aggregation in your API.

Situation: API ingests millions of events or records daily; reporting requires aggregates (counts, sums, percentiles, cohort analysis) across all of it. OLTP DB can't keep up; queries take minutes.

Approach: stream ingestion into a purpose-built analytics store; pre-compute common aggregations; serve dashboards from materialized views; batch reprocessing for backfills.

// ingest pipeline
//   [producer API] → [Kafka / Kinesis] → [batch writer] → [ClickHouse / BigQuery / Snowflake]
//                                                      ↘
//                                                       [real-time materialized views]

// PUBLISH events
app.post("/events", authenticateApiKey, async (req, res) => {
  const events = Array.isArray(req.body) ? req.body : [req.body];
  if (events.length > 1000) return res.status(400).json({ error: "batch_too_large" });

  const enriched = events.map((e) => ({
    eventId:   e.id ?? crypto.randomUUID(),
    receivedAt: new Date().toISOString(),
    tenantId:  req.user.tenantId,
    userId:    e.userId,
    type:      e.type,
    properties: e.properties ?? {},
    context:   e.context ?? {},
  }));

  // write to Kafka — decouples ingestion from storage
  await kafka.producer().sendBatch({
    topicMessages: [{
      topic: "events",
      messages: enriched.map((e) => ({ key: e.tenantId, value: JSON.stringify(e) })),
    }],
  });

  res.sendStatus(202);                                       // async — never block ingest on downstream
});

// ClickHouse materialized view — rolling metrics per hour
// CREATE MATERIALIZED VIEW events_hourly_mv
// ENGINE = SummingMergeTree
// ORDER BY (tenantId, hour, eventType)
// AS SELECT
//   tenantId,
//   toStartOfHour(receivedAt) AS hour,
//   type AS eventType,
//   count() AS cnt,
//   uniqExact(userId) AS uniqueUsers
// FROM events_raw
// GROUP BY tenantId, hour, eventType;

// QUERY endpoint — hits materialized view, not raw
app.get("/analytics/events", requireAdmin, async (req, res) => {
  const { tenantId, start, end, groupBy = "hour" } = req.query;

  const result = await ch.query({
    query: `
      SELECT ${groupBy}, eventType, sum(cnt) AS count, sum(uniqueUsers) AS unique_users
      FROM events_hourly_mv
      WHERE tenantId = {t:String} AND hour BETWEEN {s:DateTime} AND {e:DateTime}
      GROUP BY ${groupBy}, eventType
      ORDER BY ${groupBy} ASC
    `,
    query_params: { t: tenantId, s: start, e: end },
    format: "JSONEachRow",
  });

  res.json({ data: await result.json() });
});

// BACKFILL — long-running recompute via worker
app.post("/analytics/backfill", requireAdmin, async (req, res) => {
  const job = await queue.add("analytics.backfill", {
    from: req.body.from, to: req.body.to, metric: req.body.metric,
  });
  res.status(202).json({ jobId: job.id });
});

queue.process("analytics.backfill", async (job) => {
  // iterate day-by-day; process in parallel batches; persist progress
  const days = dateRange(job.data.from, job.data.to);
  for (const day of days) {
    await ch.exec(`
      INSERT INTO events_hourly_mv
      SELECT tenantId, toStartOfHour(receivedAt) AS hour, type,
             count(), uniqExact(userId)
      FROM events_raw_archive
      WHERE receivedAt >= {d:Date} AND receivedAt < {d:Date} + INTERVAL 1 DAY
      GROUP BY tenantId, hour, type
    `, { d: day });
    await job.updateProgress({ day, done: true });
  }
});

Trade-offs: OLTP vs OLAP: Postgres is fine for <100M rows; beyond, ClickHouse/BigQuery/Snowflake are 100-1000x faster for aggregations. Stream vs batch: Kafka/Kinesis decouple ingest from storage — downstream can be slow without dropping data. Materialized views: pre-aggregated data; queries are sub-second even over billions of rows. Exactly-once vs at-least-once: event pipelines typically at-least-once; idempotent inserts (upsert by eventId) prevent duplicates. Cost: BigQuery / Snowflake charge per byte scanned — partition tables, use clustering keys, query only recent data for dashboards. Real-time vs batch latency: real-time MVs serve live dashboards (<1s); hourly/daily batch jobs serve BI tools. Platforms: Tinybird, Ingest.dev, Rockset, Materialize offer managed pipelines. CDC (Debezium, Fivetran): replicate OLTP changes into analytics store automatically.

Question 80

Your API needs to support blockchain interactions. How would you design this?

Situation: app needs to interact with blockchains — Ethereum, Solana, Polygon, Bitcoin — reading balances, sending transactions, monitoring events. Abstract the complexity so product developers don't need to become blockchain experts.

Approach: abstraction layer over chain-specific SDKs; transactions queued through a signing service; event indexers feed application-level state.

// abstraction — one interface, multiple chains
// READ balance
app.get("/wallets/:address/balance", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { chain = "ethereum" } = req.query;
  const provider = providers[chain];

  const wei = await provider.getBalance(req.params.address);
  const usd = await priceOracle.toUsd({ chain, amount: wei });

  res.json({
    chain,
    address: req.params.address,
    balance: {
      native: ethers.formatEther(wei),          // for EVM chains
      usd,
    },
  });
});

// SEND transaction — async; client polls for status
app.post("/transactions", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { chain, to, amount, tokenContract } = req.body;

  // authorization: does user's custodial wallet have sufficient balance?
  const wallet = await db.userWallet.findFirst({
    where: { userId: req.user.sub, chain },
  });
  if (!wallet) return res.status(400).json({ error: "no_wallet" });

  // pre-flight: balance check, gas estimate, risk scoring
  const estimate = await estimateGas({ chain, from: wallet.address, to, amount, tokenContract });
  if (estimate.insufficientFunds) return res.status(400).json({ error: "insufficient_funds" });

  // record intent in DB FIRST — transaction is durable before we sign
  const tx = await db.transaction.create({
    data: {
      userId: req.user.sub, chain, from: wallet.address, to, amount,
      tokenContract, status: "pending_signature",
    },
  });

  // enqueue signing — signing service has exclusive access to private keys
  await queue.add("blockchain.sign_and_send", { transactionId: tx.id });

  res.status(202).location(`/transactions/${tx.id}`).json(tx);
});

// signing worker — runs in isolated environment
queue.process("blockchain.sign_and_send", async (job) => {
  const tx = await db.transaction.findUnique({ where: { id: job.data.transactionId } });

  // fetch private key from KMS / HSM — NEVER read by the main API
  const privKey = await hsm.unwrapKey({ wallet: tx.from });
  const provider = providers[tx.chain];
  const signer = new ethers.Wallet(privKey, provider);

  let txHash;
  try {
    const submitted = await signer.sendTransaction({
      to: tx.to, value: ethers.parseEther(tx.amount),
      nonce: await provider.getTransactionCount(tx.from, "pending"),
    });
    txHash = submitted.hash;
  } finally {
    privKey.wipe();                                         // clear from memory immediately
  }

  await db.transaction.update({
    where: { id: tx.id },
    data: { status: "submitted", txHash, submittedAt: new Date() },
  });

  // track confirmations
  await queue.add("blockchain.watch", { txHash, chain: tx.chain }, { delay: 15_000 });
});

// event indexer — subscribe to contract events and keep DB in sync
async function indexTransfers(chain) {
  const provider = providers[chain];
  const contract = new ethers.Contract(USDC_ADDRESS, ERC20_ABI, provider);

  let lastBlock = await db.indexState.findUnique({ where: { key: `${chain}:transfers` } });

  contract.on("Transfer", async (from, to, amount, event) => {
    if (event.blockNumber <= (lastBlock?.blockNumber ?? 0)) return;
    await db.transfer.create({
      data: {
        chain, txHash: event.transactionHash, blockNumber: event.blockNumber,
        from, to, amount: amount.toString(),
      },
    });
    await db.indexState.upsert({
      where: { key: `${chain}:transfers` },
      create: { key: `${chain}:transfers`, blockNumber: event.blockNumber },
      update: { blockNumber: event.blockNumber },
    });
  });
}

Trade-offs: key management is the deepest risk: leaked keys = permanent, irrevocable loss. Use HSMs (AWS CloudHSM, Fireblocks, Copper) or MPC wallets (Fordefi, Coinbase Prime); never store plaintext keys in application DBs. Custodial vs non-custodial: custodial (you hold keys) is simpler UX but enormous liability; non-custodial (user's wallet signs) safer but complex onboarding. 2026 standard: smart-contract wallets (ERC-4337 account abstraction) offer the best of both. Gas volatility: transactions fail if gas estimate is too low; retry logic with EIP-1559 priority fee adjustment. Finality: Bitcoin ≥6 confirmations; Ethereum ≥12; Solana nearly instant. Update state only after required confirmations. Indexers: managed (The Graph, Alchemy, QuickNode, Covalent) or self-hosted (Ponder, Subsquid). Chain reorgs: EVM chains can reorganize shallow blocks; indexers must handle (unwind / replay). Compliance: OFAC screening, travel rule for larger transfers, KYC/AML for custodial services. Blockchain interaction is easy to prototype; hard to production-harden.

Question 81

How would you implement a custom search engine for your API?

Situation: users search a large corpus (documents, products, listings); LIKE '%query%' doesn't work — slow, no relevance ranking, no typo tolerance. Need real search.

Approach: dedicated search engine (Elasticsearch/OpenSearch, Meilisearch, Typesense); sync data from primary DB; expose search endpoint with filters, facets, pagination, and relevance.

// 1. sync changes to search index via outbox + worker
async function reindex(item) {
  await searchClient.index({
    index: "items",
    id: item.id,
    document: {
      id: item.id,
      title: item.title,
      description: item.description,
      tags: item.tags,
      price: item.price,
      categoryId: item.categoryId,
      tenantId: item.tenantId,
      createdAt: item.createdAt.toISOString(),
      popularity: item.views30d,
    },
  });
}

// 2. search endpoint
app.get("/search", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const {
    q = "",
    filter = "",                                   // "category:shoes price:10..100"
    sort = "relevance",
    page = 1,
    perPage = 20,
  } = req.query;

  const from = (Number(page) - 1) * Number(perPage);
  const size = Math.min(100, Number(perPage));

  const parsedFilters = parseFilters(filter);       // → ES bool.filter clauses
  const sortClause = buildSort(sort);

  const result = await searchClient.search({
    index: "items",
    from, size,
    query: {
      bool: {
        must: q ? [{
          multi_match: {
            query: q,
            fields: ["title^3", "description", "tags^2"],
            fuzziness: "AUTO",
            prefix_length: 2,
          },
        }] : [{ match_all: {} }],
        filter: [
          { term: { tenantId: req.user.tenantId } },
          ...parsedFilters,
        ],
      },
    },
    sort: sortClause,
    aggs: {                                         // facets for sidebar
      categories: { terms: { field: "categoryId", size: 20 } },
      priceRanges: {
        range: { field: "price", ranges: [
          { to: 50 }, { from: 50, to: 100 }, { from: 100 }
        ] },
      },
    },
    highlight: {
      fields: { title: {}, description: {} },
      pre_tags: ["<mark>"], post_tags: ["</mark>"],
    },
  });

  res.json({
    data:     result.hits.hits.map((h) => ({ ...h._source, score: h._score, highlights: h.highlight })),
    total:    result.hits.total.value,
    page:     Number(page),
    perPage:  size,
    facets:   {
      categories: result.aggregations.categories.buckets,
      priceRanges: result.aggregations.priceRanges.buckets,
    },
    tookMs: result.took,
  });
});

// 3. autocomplete — faster, separate index tuned for prefix
app.get("/search/suggest", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const q = String(req.query.q || "").trim();
  if (q.length < 1) return res.json({ data: [] });

  const result = await searchClient.search({
    index: "items_suggest",
    size: 8,
    query: {
      bool: {
        must: [{ match_phrase_prefix: { title: { query: q, max_expansions: 10 } } }],
        filter: [{ term: { tenantId: req.user.tenantId } }],
      },
    },
    _source: ["id", "title"],
  });
  res.json({ data: result.hits.hits.map((h) => h._source) });
});

Trade-offs: index lag: search is eventually-consistent with the primary DB; write → visible in search in seconds. Acceptable for most cases; not for "I just created it, why isn't it searchable?" UX — surface created records directly from the primary DB for the owner's own view. Relevance tuning: field boosts, synonyms, language analyzers (stemming, stop words), custom ranking signals (popularity, recency) — these make the difference between "bad search" and "loved search." Typo tolerance: Elasticsearch's fuzziness: "AUTO", Typesense/Meilisearch default. Multi-tenant isolation: tenantId filter on every query; index aliases per tenant for very large tenants. Semantic search (2026 default): vector embeddings via OpenAI, Cohere, or open models indexed in Qdrant, pgvector, Weaviate, Pinecone; hybrid search combines keyword + vector for best relevance. Managed vs self-hosted: Algolia, Typesense Cloud, Meilisearch Cloud, Elastic Cloud, OpenSearch Serverless are all viable; rolling your own Elasticsearch cluster is a known operational trap for small teams.

Question 82

Describe how you would handle multi-region deployment for your API to ensure low latency.

Situation: users are global; latency from EU to us-east-1 is ~90ms. Want to serve requests from the region closest to the user, with automatic failover.

Approach: deploy identical stateless API in multiple regions; geo-DNS or anycast routing; data replicated globally with region-aware reads/writes.

// Route 53 latency-based routing
// All regions share the hostname api.example.com; Route 53 returns the closest.
// resource "aws_route53_record" "api_us_east" {
//   zone_id = var.zone_id; name = "api"; type = "A"
//   set_identifier = "us-east-1"; latency_routing_policy { region = "us-east-1" }
//   alias { name = aws_lb.us_east.dns_name; zone_id = aws_lb.us_east.zone_id; evaluate_target_health = true }
// }

// at the application level — detect region & write to closest write endpoint
const REGION = process.env.AWS_REGION;
const IS_WRITE_REGION = REGION === "us-east-1";      // for single-writer topology

// READ — local always
app.get("/products/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const product = await dbLocalReplica.product.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  res.set("X-Region", REGION).json(product);
});

// WRITE — if not in write region, forward
app.post("/products", requireAuth, async (req, res, next) => {
  if (!IS_WRITE_REGION) {
    const forwarded = await fetch(`https://us-east-1.internal.example.com/products`, {
      method: "POST",
      headers: {
        "Content-Type":  "application/json",
        "Authorization": req.headers.authorization,
        "X-Forwarded-For": req.ip,
      },
      body: JSON.stringify(req.body),
    });
    return res.status(forwarded.status).type("application/json").send(await forwarded.text());
  }

  const created = await dbPrimary.product.create({ data: req.body });
  res.status(201).json(created);
});

// health check endpoint used by DNS health checks
app.get("/healthz", async (req, res) => {
  try {
    await dbLocalReplica.$queryRaw`SELECT 1`;
    await redis.ping();
    res.json({ status: "ok", region: REGION });
  } catch (err) {
    res.status(503).json({ status: "unhealthy", error: err.message });
  }
});

Data strategies:

ModelTradeoff
Single-writer + read replicasSimple; writes from distant users slow
Per-region shards (user data pinned)Low latency; user's region is their home
Global distributed DB (Spanner, Cockroach, Yugabyte)Strong consistency globally; ~50-150ms writes
Multi-master eventually-consistent (DynamoDB Global Tables, Cassandra)Low-latency writes; conflict resolution required

Caching is the biggest win for reads: Cloudflare/Fastly CDN with surrogate-key purging serves most GET traffic from the edge in <50ms globally — skipping the origin entirely. Origin closer helps only cache misses and writes.

Trade-offs: cost doubles with each additional region; data transfer between regions is expensive. Start with 2 regions (NA + EU); add APAC when user metrics justify. Operational complexity: rolling deploys per region (never all at once — canary catches region-specific failures); observability aggregates across regions but preserves dimension. Compliance: GDPR data residency means EU user data stays in EU regions — partition at the user level, not the region level. Failover: DNS-based is slow (minutes); anycast (Cloudflare Workers) is sub-second; test quarterly. Multi-region is a commitment — easy to enter, painful to roll back once customers depend on low latency in each region.

Question 83

Your API needs to support image recognition and processing. How would you implement this?

Situation: users upload images; the API must classify, OCR, detect objects, or moderate content. The heavy lifting is ML inference — expensive, slow, and unpredictable — but the API must remain responsive.

Approach: separate the synchronous upload from the asynchronous analysis. Upload returns immediately with a job ID; a worker pulls from a queue, runs inference (managed service or self-hosted model), writes results, and fires a webhook. Clients poll or subscribe to SSE for status.

// POST /images — upload and queue
app.post("/images", requireAuth, upload.single("file"), async (req, res) => {
  const { Key } = await s3.send(new PutObjectCommand({
    Bucket: "uploads", Key: `img/${crypto.randomUUID()}`,
    Body: req.file.buffer, ContentType: req.file.mimetype,
  }));

  const job = await db.imageJob.create({
    data: { userId: req.user.id, s3Key: Key, status: "queued" },
  });

  await sqs.send(new SendMessageCommand({
    QueueUrl: process.env.IMAGE_QUEUE_URL,
    MessageBody: JSON.stringify({ jobId: job.id, s3Key: Key }),
  }));

  res.status(202).location(`/images/${job.id}`).json({ jobId: job.id, status: "queued" });
});

// Worker — pull, run inference, update job
async function processImage({ jobId, s3Key }) {
  await db.imageJob.update({ where: { id: jobId }, data: { status: "processing" } });

  // Cloud provider services (pick based on need):
  //   AWS Rekognition — labels, faces, text, moderation
  //   Google Vision / Cloud Vision — similar
  //   Azure AI Vision — OCR, spatial analysis
  //   Self-hosted — YOLOv8, CLIP, Segment Anything via TorchServe or Ray Serve
  const result = await rekognition.send(new DetectLabelsCommand({
    Image: { S3Object: { Bucket: "uploads", Name: s3Key } },
    MaxLabels: 20, MinConfidence: 75,
  }));

  await db.imageJob.update({
    where: { id: jobId },
    data: { status: "completed", labels: result.Labels, completedAt: new Date() },
  });

  await fireWebhook(jobId, "image.analyzed");
}

Trade-offs: managed services (Rekognition, Vision AI, Azure AI) have zero ops, per-call pricing, good general models — but no customization and data leaves your cloud. Self-hosted open models (YOLO for detection, CLIP for similarity, Tesseract for OCR, Segment Anything for masks) via Triton Inference Server, Ray Serve, or Modal give privacy and low marginal cost at scale but demand GPU ops. Hybrid: sensitive workloads on-prem, commodity on managed. Pre-resize images to 1024px max before inference — 4K photos waste GPU time without improving accuracy. Use signed URLs for result images so clients can display them without proxying through your API. Cache results by content hash — the same photo uploaded twice is cheap to serve the second time.

Question 84

How would you design an API to support live streaming of video content?

Situation: users stream live video (creator broadcasts, events, security cams). The API must accept ingest from broadcasters, transcode to adaptive bitrates, and distribute to viewers globally with sub-3-second latency.

Approach: use RTMP / WebRTC / SRT for ingest, HLS or LL-HLS for playback. The API itself manages metadata (streams, thumbnails, authorization, recordings) and signs short-lived viewer tokens. Media flows through a specialized media server — not your API.

// POST /streams — create a stream and return ingest credentials
app.post("/streams", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const streamKey = crypto.randomBytes(24).toString("base64url");
  const stream = await db.stream.create({
    data: {
      userId: req.user.id,
      title: req.body.title,
      streamKey: hashForStorage(streamKey),    // store hash only
      status: "idle",
      playbackId: crypto.randomUUID(),
    },
  });

  res.status(201).json({
    streamId: stream.id,
    ingestUrl: `rtmps://ingest.example.com/live`,
    streamKey,                                  // shown ONCE; user configures OBS/Larix
    playbackUrl: `https://cdn.example.com/hls/${stream.playbackId}/index.m3u8`,
  });
});

// GET /streams/:id/playback — signed viewer token for private streams
app.get("/streams/:id/playback", async (req, res) => {
  const stream = await db.stream.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!stream || stream.status !== "live") return res.sendStatus(404);

  if (stream.isPrivate && !await canView(req.user, stream)) return res.sendStatus(403);

  // short-lived signed CDN URL (Cloudflare / CloudFront signed URLs)
  const token = jwt.sign(
    { sub: stream.playbackId, exp: Math.floor(Date.now() / 1000) + 300 },
    process.env.CDN_SIGNING_KEY,
    { algorithm: "EdDSA" },
  );
  res.json({ playbackUrl: `https://cdn.example.com/hls/${stream.playbackId}/index.m3u8?token=${token}` });
});

// Media server webhook — notifies API on ingest connect/disconnect
app.post("/internal/stream-events", verifyHmac, async (req, res) => {
  const { event, playbackId, bitrate, duration } = req.body;
  if (event === "stream.started") {
    await db.stream.update({ where: { playbackId }, data: { status: "live", startedAt: new Date() } });
  } else if (event === "stream.ended") {
    await db.stream.update({ where: { playbackId }, data: { status: "ended", durationSec: duration } });
  }
  res.sendStatus(200);
});

Trade-offs: managed platforms (Mux, Cloudflare Stream, AWS IVS, LiveKit) handle ingest, transcode, CDN, and recording for $0.01-0.05 per minute — the right choice unless you're at YouTube scale. Self-hosted (NGINX-RTMP, Ant Media Server, Wowza, Ovenmedia) gives control but requires GPU fleets, CDN contracts, and DRM work. Latency tiers: HLS 15-30s, LL-HLS 2-4s, WebRTC 500ms. Choose by use case: livestreams tolerate HLS; betting, auctions, and video calls need WebRTC. Recording: separate concern — write the transcoded segments to S3 and stitch into VOD after stream ends. Chat + presence (needed alongside video) runs on Redis pub/sub or a managed service like Pusher/Ably — keep it out of the video path.

Question 85

Describe how you would implement a feature that allows users to set up custom alerts and notifications.

Situation: users want to be notified when specific events happen — a price drops, inventory restocks, a metric crosses a threshold, a keyword appears in a feed. The system must evaluate many alerts efficiently against streaming events.

Approach: model alerts as first-class resources with CRUD; match them against an event stream with predicate evaluation; deliver via email, push, SMS, or webhook with per-user channel preferences.

// POST /alerts — create an alert rule
const AlertSchema = z.object({
  name: z.string().min(1).max(100),
  condition: z.object({
    entity:   z.enum(["product", "metric", "article"]),
    field:    z.string(),
    operator: z.enum(["lt", "gt", "eq", "contains", "changed"]),
    value:    z.union([z.string(), z.number(), z.boolean()]),
  }),
  channels: z.array(z.enum(["email", "push", "sms", "webhook"])).min(1),
  cooldownMin: z.number().int().min(1).default(60),    // suppress dupes within window
});

app.post("/alerts", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const data = AlertSchema.parse(req.body);
  const alert = await db.alert.create({ data: { ...data, userId: req.user.id, enabled: true } });
  res.status(201).json(alert);
});

// Event-driven matcher — subscribe to the relevant event streams
// For low volume: naive scan. For high volume: Rete algorithm or streaming SQL (ksqlDB, Flink).
async function onEvent(event) {
  // Index alerts by (entity, field) so we don't scan every alert for every event
  const candidates = await redis.smembers(`alerts:${event.entity}:${event.field}`);
  for (const alertId of candidates) {
    const alert = JSON.parse(await redis.get(`alert:${alertId}`));
    if (!alert.enabled || !matches(alert.condition, event)) continue;

    // cooldown — don't repeatedly fire
    const fired = await redis.set(`cooldown:${alertId}`, "1", "EX", alert.cooldownMin * 60, "NX");
    if (!fired) continue;

    await deliveryQueue.add("notify", { alertId, event });
  }
}

// Delivery worker — fan-out per channel with the user's preferences
async function deliverNotification({ alertId, event }) {
  const alert = await db.alert.findUnique({ where: { id: alertId }, include: { user: true } });
  const tasks = alert.channels.map((ch) => {
    if (ch === "email")   return sesClient.sendEmail(formatEmail(alert, event));
    if (ch === "push")    return fcm.send({ token: alert.user.pushToken, notification: format(alert, event) });
    if (ch === "sms")     return twilio.messages.create({ to: alert.user.phone, body: formatSms(alert, event) });
    if (ch === "webhook") return signedPost(alert.user.webhookUrl, { alertId, event });
  });
  await Promise.allSettled(tasks);            // partial failure OK
}

Trade-offs: simple filter matching works for thousands of alerts with an indexed table. Beyond ~100k rules, switch to rule engines (Rete, Drools) or streaming SQL (ksqlDB, Apache Flink SQL, Materialize) which compile predicates into efficient dataflow. Cooldown prevents alert storms when a value oscillates around a threshold. Per-channel preferences + quiet hours are essential UX — nobody wants SMS at 3am. Digest mode (bundle alerts into one daily email) is often better for non-urgent alerts. For critical alerts (PagerDuty-style), escalation policies (try channel A, fall back to B after 5 min) matter; route via OpsGenie/PagerDuty/Incident.io rather than rolling your own.

Question 86

Your API needs to support AI and machine learning models. How would you integrate these capabilities?

Situation: the product needs AI — chat completion, embeddings, classification, image generation, speech-to-text. The API must expose these to clients reliably while managing cost, latency, and vendor risk.

Approach: build a thin gateway layer around model providers. Clients call your API; your API calls OpenAI/Anthropic/Mistral/your own model. The gateway handles auth, rate limiting, caching, cost attribution, PII redaction, retries, and streaming.

// POST /ai/complete — chat completion proxied with streaming
app.post("/ai/complete", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { messages, model = "claude-opus-4-7", stream = true } = req.body;

  // 1. Budget check per user/org
  const spent = await redis.incrByFloat(`aiSpend:${req.user.orgId}:${todayKey()}`, 0);
  if (spent > req.user.org.dailyBudget) {
    return res.status(429).json({ error: "daily_ai_budget_exceeded" });
  }

  // 2. Redact PII before it leaves the infra (opt-in per tenant)
  const redacted = req.user.org.redactPii ? await redact(messages) : messages;

  // 3. Cache deterministic prompts (embeddings, classification)
  const cacheKey = `ai:${hash(model + JSON.stringify(redacted))}`;
  const cached   = await redis.get(cacheKey);
  if (cached && !stream) return res.json(JSON.parse(cached));

  // 4. Call provider with streaming SSE
  const upstream = await anthropic.messages.stream({ model, messages: redacted, max_tokens: 4000 });

  res.setHeader("Content-Type", "text/event-stream");
  res.setHeader("Cache-Control", "no-cache");

  let output = "", tokensIn = 0, tokensOut = 0;
  upstream.on("text", (t) => { output += t; res.write(`data: ${JSON.stringify({ text: t })}\n\n`); });
  upstream.on("message", (m) => { tokensIn = m.usage.input_tokens; tokensOut = m.usage.output_tokens; });
  upstream.on("end", async () => {
    res.write("data: [DONE]\n\n"); res.end();
    const cost = pricing[model].input * tokensIn + pricing[model].output * tokensOut;
    await redis.incrByFloat(`aiSpend:${req.user.orgId}:${todayKey()}`, cost);
    await db.aiCall.create({ data: { userId: req.user.id, model, tokensIn, tokensOut, cost } });
  });
  upstream.on("error", (err) => { res.write(`event: error\ndata: ${err.message}\n\n`); res.end(); });
});

Trade-offs: provider choice: managed APIs (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, Cohere, Mistral) give fast iteration and automatic model updates; self-hosted (Llama/Qwen/DeepSeek via vLLM, TGI, Triton, Modal, Runpod) wins on volume economics and privacy. Use LiteLLM or OpenRouter as a portable abstraction so swapping providers is config, not code. Caching is the biggest cost lever — embeddings are deterministic (cache forever); classification with temperature=0 is cacheable; open-ended chat usually isn't. Prompt caching (Anthropic, OpenAI) reduces input cost 90% for repeated system prompts. Guardrails (input filtering, output moderation, structured output validation with Zod/JSON Schema) are non-negotiable for user-generated prompts. Observability: log every call with request ID, model, tokens, latency, cost — AI budgets surprise teams weekly. Use Langfuse, Helicone, or Braintrust for traces and eval.

Question 87

How would you handle API versioning for a rapidly evolving product?

Situation: the product evolves weekly. The API must support new shapes without breaking existing mobile clients that users haven't updated in months.

Approach: commit to additive-only changes by default; reserve versioning for rare unavoidable breaks. When a break is unavoidable, run old and new in parallel, migrate consumers, then sunset on a published timeline.

// Versioning strategy matrix — pick one and apply consistently
//
//   URL path     /v1/users           Easy to reason about; leaks into every log
//   Header       Api-Version: 2026-04-15     Per-request override; dated versions work well
//   Media type   Accept: application/vnd.company.v2+json      Most RESTful; least adopted
//
// Recommendation: dated header versions (Stripe model). Defaults to user's pinned version.

// Middleware — resolve version from multiple sources
function resolveVersion(req, res, next) {
  const explicit = req.get("Api-Version");               // request-level override
  const pinned   = req.user?.apiVersion;                 // account-level default
  const latest   = "2026-04-15";                         // current version

  req.apiVersion = explicit ?? pinned ?? latest;
  res.set("Api-Version", req.apiVersion);
  next();
}

// Version compatibility layer — one source of truth for transformations
const TRANSFORMS = {
  "2025-06-01": {
    // v1 had fullName; v2 split into firstName/lastName
    response: (user) => ({ ...user, fullName: `${user.firstName} ${user.lastName}` }),
    request:  (body) => {
      if (body.fullName && !body.firstName) {
        const [firstName, ...rest] = body.fullName.split(" ");
        return { ...body, firstName, lastName: rest.join(" ") };
      }
      return body;
    },
  },
  "2025-11-01": {
    response: (user) => user,    // this version added a new field; no transform needed
    request:  (body) => body,
  },
};

// Apply transforms — chain from oldest to current
function transformResponse(data, fromVersion) {
  const versions = Object.keys(TRANSFORMS).filter((v) => v >= fromVersion).sort();
  return versions.reduce((acc, v) => TRANSFORMS[v].response(acc), data);
}

Trade-offs: dated versioning (Stripe's "2024-10-28.acacia") lets you release small changes weekly and pin customers to the date they integrated. Semantic versioning in URLs (/v1, /v2) is coarser — every breaking change jumps the whole API. For a rapidly-evolving product, dated wins. Expand-and-contract for each change: add new field, dual-write, migrate consumers, contract old. Track usage per version in metrics; announce sunset only after old-version traffic approaches zero. Deprecation signals: return Deprecation and Sunset headers (RFC 8594) on old versions so clients see warnings in logs. Breaking change policy: minimum 6-12 months between announcement and sunset for B2B; longer for mobile. Compatibility tests in CI replay recorded requests from old versions against the new code — regressions in the transform layer surface before deploy.

Question 88

Describe how you would implement a custom data encryption mechanism for sensitive information.

Situation: certain fields (SSN, bank account, medical records, proprietary IP) must be encrypted at rest beyond the database's transparent encryption — a DBA snapshot shouldn't reveal them. Also needs to be decryptable for authorized reads with an audit trail.

Approach: envelope encryption. A Key Management Service (KMS) holds the key-encryption key (KEK); each record has its own data-encryption key (DEK) encrypted with the KEK. DEK is decrypted at read time. KEK never leaves KMS.

// Envelope encryption with AWS KMS + AES-256-GCM
import { KMSClient, EncryptCommand, DecryptCommand, GenerateDataKeyCommand } from "@aws-sdk/client-kms";
import crypto from "node:crypto";

const kms  = new KMSClient({});
const KEY_ID = process.env.KMS_KEY_ID;

export async function encryptField(plaintext, context) {
  // 1. Ask KMS for a fresh DEK — returns plaintext + encrypted forms
  const { Plaintext: dek, CiphertextBlob: edek } = await kms.send(
    new GenerateDataKeyCommand({
      KeyId: KEY_ID, KeySpec: "AES_256",
      EncryptionContext: context,                // e.g. { userId, fieldName } — binds DEK to context
    }),
  );

  // 2. Encrypt plaintext with DEK (AES-GCM is AEAD — confidentiality + integrity)
  const iv     = crypto.randomBytes(12);
  const cipher = crypto.createCipheriv("aes-256-gcm", dek, iv);
  const ct     = Buffer.concat([cipher.update(plaintext, "utf8"), cipher.final()]);
  const tag    = cipher.getAuthTag();

  // 3. Zero out DEK from memory; persist edek+iv+tag+ciphertext
  dek.fill(0);

  return {
    edek: Buffer.from(edek).toString("base64"),
    iv:   iv.toString("base64"),
    tag:  tag.toString("base64"),
    ct:   ct.toString("base64"),
    alg:  "AES-256-GCM",
    kekVersion: 1,
  };
}

export async function decryptField(record, context) {
  // 1. KMS unwraps the DEK — this call is audited
  const { Plaintext: dek } = await kms.send(new DecryptCommand({
    CiphertextBlob: Buffer.from(record.edek, "base64"),
    EncryptionContext: context,                  // mismatch → decrypt fails
  }));

  // 2. Decrypt with AES-GCM
  const decipher = crypto.createDecipheriv("aes-256-gcm", dek, Buffer.from(record.iv, "base64"));
  decipher.setAuthTag(Buffer.from(record.tag, "base64"));
  const plaintext = Buffer.concat([decipher.update(Buffer.from(record.ct, "base64")), decipher.final()]);

  dek.fill(0);
  return plaintext.toString("utf8");
}

// Usage — store encrypted; decrypt only when needed
app.post("/users/:id/ssn", requireAuth, requireRole("admin"), async (req, res) => {
  const encrypted = await encryptField(req.body.ssn, { userId: req.params.id, field: "ssn" });
  await db.user.update({ where: { id: req.params.id }, data: { ssnEncrypted: encrypted } });
  await audit.log({ actor: req.user.id, action: "ssn.encrypt", target: req.params.id });
  res.sendStatus(204);
});

Trade-offs: managed KMS (AWS KMS, GCP KMS, Azure Key Vault, HashiCorp Vault Transit) is the right default — hardware-backed (HSM), audited, automatic rotation. Do not roll your own crypto. Prefer libraries over primitives: Tink (Google), AWS Encryption SDK, libsodium all provide safe defaults that resist common mistakes. EncryptionContext / AAD (Additional Authenticated Data) binds the ciphertext to its context — an attacker can't copy an encrypted SSN from user A to user B. Key rotation: rotate KEK annually (KMS handles it); rotate DEK on every write; old ciphertext stays readable because records remember which KEK version. Deterministic encryption (same plaintext → same ciphertext) enables queries on encrypted fields but leaks equality; use sparingly (CipherSweet pattern). Search: blind indexes (HMAC of normalized value) let you look up encrypted records without decryption. Audit: log every decrypt with actor, reason, and record — compliance demands it (HIPAA, PCI, SOC 2).

Question 89

Your API needs to support facial recognition. How would you design and implement this?

Situation: users upload selfies; the API verifies identity by matching against a reference photo (KYC), or unlocks devices, or tags photos. Facial data is biometric PII — the most sensitive category under GDPR, BIPA, and most regulations.

Approach: convert faces to embeddings (dense vectors, ~512 dims) rather than storing raw images; compare with cosine similarity; layer liveness detection to defeat photo attacks; require explicit consent with clear purpose limitation.

// POST /identity/verify — KYC flow: compare selfie to ID document photo
app.post("/identity/verify", requireAuth, upload.fields([
  { name: "selfie", maxCount: 1 }, { name: "idDoc", maxCount: 1 },
]), async (req, res) => {
  // 1. Consent check — refuse if not recorded
  const consent = await db.consent.findFirst({
    where: { userId: req.user.id, purpose: "biometric_verification", revokedAt: null },
  });
  if (!consent) return res.status(403).json({ error: "biometric_consent_required" });

  const selfieBuf = req.files.selfie[0].buffer;
  const idDocBuf  = req.files.idDoc[0].buffer;

  // 2. Liveness — ensure the selfie is a live person, not a printed photo
  //    AWS Rekognition DetectFaces with Quality attrs, or specialized services:
  //    FaceTec, iProov, Onfido, Jumio, Veriff, Incode
  const liveness = await livenessService.check(selfieBuf);
  if (liveness.score < 0.9) {
    return res.status(422).json({ error: "liveness_failed", reason: liveness.reason });
  }

  // 3. Extract embeddings from both images
  const [selfieEmb, idDocEmb] = await Promise.all([
    embeddingService.extract(selfieBuf),
    embeddingService.extract(idDocBuf),
  ]);

  // 4. Compare (cosine similarity > 0.85 ≈ same person; tune per security budget)
  const similarity = cosineSim(selfieEmb, idDocEmb);
  const match      = similarity > 0.85;

  // 5. Never persist the raw images; embeddings only, encrypted
  const verificationId = crypto.randomUUID();
  await db.verification.create({
    data: {
      id: verificationId,
      userId: req.user.id,
      similarity,
      match,
      // optional: store encrypted embedding for later re-verification
      embeddingEnc: match ? await encryptField(Buffer.from(selfieEmb.buffer).toString("base64"), { userId: req.user.id, field: "face_embedding" }) : null,
      expiresAt: addMonths(new Date(), 6),     // auto-delete per retention policy
    },
  });

  await audit.log({ actor: req.user.id, action: "identity.verify", target: verificationId, result: match });

  res.json({ verificationId, match, confidence: similarity });
});

// POST /me/face-data — DELETE right — users can revoke and delete biometrics
app.delete("/me/face-data", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  await db.verification.deleteMany({ where: { userId: req.user.id } });
  await db.consent.updateMany({
    where: { userId: req.user.id, purpose: "biometric_verification" },
    data:  { revokedAt: new Date() },
  });
  res.sendStatus(204);
});

Trade-offs: Specialized vendors (Onfido, Jumio, Veriff, Incode, Persona, FaceTec) handle KYC end-to-end including document OCR, liveness, AML screening, and regional compliance — worth every cent unless identity is your core product. Self-hosted models (InsightFace, ArcFace, DeepFace) are feasible but demand serious ML ops. Legal considerations are paramount: BIPA (Illinois) awards $1000-5000 per violation, has triggered multi-hundred-million-dollar settlements (Meta, Google, Clearview). GDPR treats biometrics as special-category data requiring explicit consent. Illinois, Texas, Washington and several EU countries have stricter rules than federal US. Never store raw face images longer than needed for the transaction. Store embeddings (irreversible with modern models) encrypted at rest. Offer alternative verification (doc review, ID + address) for users who refuse biometrics. Age-gating minors is explicit prohibition territory — have legal review. Liveness detection defeats photo/video replay; without it, any child with a printout can fool the system.

Question 90

How would you handle real-time data synchronization across multiple devices and platforms?

Situation: one user's laptop, phone, and tablet all show the same note/list/cart. Edits made on any device appear on the others within seconds, including while offline — changes merge on reconnect without losing work.

Approach: authoritative server state + per-device local replica + sync protocol based on a cursor or vector clock. For collaborative text/structured data, CRDTs eliminate conflicts. For simple key-value, last-write-wins with timestamps is sufficient.

// Sync endpoints — the three primitives: pull, push, ack
//
// GET  /sync/pull?since=<cursor>       → changes since cursor + new cursor
// POST /sync/push                      → { mutations: [...] } — client applies locally, then pushes
// WebSocket /sync/live                 → server pushes new changes as they happen

// PULL — incremental sync
app.get("/sync/pull", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const since = req.query.since || "0";
  const changes = await db.changeLog.findMany({
    where: { userId: req.user.id, seq: { gt: BigInt(since) } },
    orderBy: { seq: "asc" },
    take: 500,                                  // page large histories
  });

  const cursor = changes.at(-1)?.seq?.toString() ?? since;
  res.json({
    changes: changes.map(serializeChange),
    cursor,
    hasMore: changes.length === 500,
  });
});

// PUSH — client sends mutations; server applies with conflict resolution
app.post("/sync/push", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const results = [];
  for (const m of req.body.mutations) {
    // Idempotency — each mutation has a client-generated ID; replayed pushes are no-ops
    const exists = await db.mutation.findUnique({ where: { id: m.id } });
    if (exists) { results.push({ id: m.id, status: "duplicate", serverId: exists.serverId }); continue; }

    // Apply with version check — optimistic concurrency
    try {
      const updated = await db.$transaction(async (tx) => {
        const current = await tx.note.findUnique({ where: { id: m.noteId } });
        if (current && current.version !== m.expectedVersion) {
          // Conflict — server wins for simple scalars; CRDT merge for rich text
          throw new ConflictError({ serverVersion: current.version, serverState: current });
        }
        return tx.note.update({
          where: { id: m.noteId },
          data: { ...m.patch, version: { increment: 1 } },
        });
      });
      await db.mutation.create({ data: { id: m.id, userId: req.user.id, serverId: updated.id } });
      await broadcast(req.user.id, { type: "note.updated", note: updated });
      results.push({ id: m.id, status: "applied", serverVersion: updated.version });
    } catch (err) {
      if (err instanceof ConflictError) results.push({ id: m.id, status: "conflict", server: err.serverState });
      else throw err;
    }
  }
  res.json({ results });
});

Trade-offs: Scalar data with LWW (last-write-wins keyed by timestamp or version) is simplest; conflicts silently overwrite — acceptable for settings, not for documents. CRDTs (Yjs, Automerge, Loro) merge concurrent edits mathematically — the right tool for text, lists, and rich data; more complex; larger payloads (they encode merge history). Operational Transform (Google Docs) is battle-tested but requires a central coordinator. Transport: WebSocket for live; HTTP polling for reliable delivery on flaky networks; background fetch for mobile. Platforms: Firebase Realtime / Firestore, Supabase Realtime, Convex, Ably, Liveblocks, PartyKit, PowerSync, Replicache, ElectricSQL — all solve subsets of this problem so you don't build from scratch. Offline queue: client persists pending mutations to IndexedDB / SQLite; retries with backoff on reconnect; reconciles via idempotency IDs. Security: every mutation is authorized server-side — client-trust is the #1 bug in sync systems.

Question 91

Describe how you would implement a custom logging framework for your API.

Situation: structured logs from every service need to flow to a central store where they're searchable, correlated across services, and retained according to policy. Off-the-shelf loggers don't match internal conventions (trace propagation, PII redaction, custom fields).

Approach: wrap a mature base logger (pino, winston, bunyan) with conventions: required fields, context propagation via AsyncLocalStorage, PII redaction, sampling of high-volume debug logs, and a ship path to the backend.

// logger.ts — opinionated wrapper around pino
import pino from "pino";
import { AsyncLocalStorage } from "node:async_hooks";
import { trace } from "@opentelemetry/api";

const als = new AsyncLocalStorage();

const baseLogger = pino({
  level: process.env.LOG_LEVEL || "info",
  formatters: {
    level: (label) => ({ level: label }),
    bindings: (b) => ({
      service: process.env.SERVICE_NAME,
      env:     process.env.ENV,
      version: process.env.GIT_SHA,
      region:  process.env.AWS_REGION,
      host:    b.hostname,
    }),
  },
  redact: {
    paths: [
      "*.password", "*.token", "*.ssn", "*.cardNumber",
      "req.headers.authorization", "req.headers.cookie",
      "*.email",                        // or use hashing instead of full redaction
    ],
    censor: "[REDACTED]",
  },
  timestamp: () => `,"time":"${new Date().toISOString()}"`,
});

// Auto-enrich every log with trace, req, user context
export function log(level, msg, extra = {}) {
  const ctx = als.getStore() ?? {};
  const span = trace.getActiveSpan();
  const spanCtx = span?.spanContext();

  baseLogger[level]({
