    ...ctx,                                     // reqId, userId, orgId from middleware
    traceId: spanCtx?.traceId,
    spanId:  spanCtx?.spanId,
    ...extra,
  }, msg);
}

// Middleware — wrap every request in context
export function logContext(req, res, next) {
  const reqId = req.get("X-Request-Id") ?? crypto.randomUUID();
  res.set("X-Request-Id", reqId);
  als.run({ reqId, userId: req.user?.id, orgId: req.user?.orgId, path: req.path }, () => {
    const start = Date.now();
    res.on("finish", () => {
      log("info", "request", {
        method: req.method, path: req.path, status: res.statusCode, durMs: Date.now() - start,
      });
    });
    next();
  });
}

// Sampling helper — keep 100% of errors/warnings, sample debug
export function logSampled(level, msg, extra, sampleRate = 0.01) {
  if (level === "error" || level === "warn" || Math.random() < sampleRate) {
    log(level, msg, extra);
  }
}

// Shipping — stdout → Vector / Fluent Bit → Loki / Elastic / Datadog
// Container runtime collects stdout; no SDK to the log backend in app code.

Trade-offs: ship via stdout, not direct SDK. Apps write JSON to stdout; a sidecar (Vector, Fluent Bit, promtail) enriches, batches, and ships. Decouples app from logging backend — swap Datadog for Grafana Loki without a code change. AsyncLocalStorage solves the "why is this log not tagged with reqId" problem permanently — context flows through awaits and callbacks without threading arguments. OpenTelemetry integration bakes in traceId/spanId so logs correlate with traces in one UI (Grafana, Datadog, Honeycomb, Tempo). Structured only: no console.log("user " + id + " did thing"). Log { event: "user.action", userId, action } so you can query. Sampling matters at scale — 10 billion debug logs/day is a million dollar bill. Sample debug, not errors. PII redaction is a source of audit findings; encode allow-list of safe fields rather than deny-list of risky ones. Retention tiers: hot (7-30 days, searchable), warm (90 days, query with delay), cold (S3/Glacier, 7 years for compliance, restore via job).

Question 92

Your API needs to support augmented reality (AR) applications. How would you design this?

Situation: mobile and headset AR apps (phone camera, Vision Pro, Quest, Hololens) need to fetch 3D assets, anchor them to real-world locations, enable multi-user shared experiences, and track analytics — all with low latency to avoid motion sickness.

Approach: three API surfaces — asset delivery (static, CDN-heavy), spatial anchors (semi-dynamic, per-location), and real-time session (dynamic, per-user-group). Offload heavy lifting to the device's GPU; the API delivers metadata and coordinates.

// Asset delivery — served from CDN; API returns signed URLs
// GET /ar/assets/:id
app.get("/ar/assets/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const asset = await db.arAsset.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  if (!asset) return res.sendStatus(404);
  if (!await canAccess(req.user, asset)) return res.sendStatus(403);

  // Platform-specific — iOS wants USDZ, Android wants glTF, Vision Pro USDZ
  const platform = req.get("X-Client-Platform") ?? "gltf";
  const file     = asset.formats[platform] ?? asset.formats.gltf;

  res.json({
    id:        asset.id,
    url:       await signedUrl(file.s3Key, 3600),     // CDN-backed
    size:      file.bytes,
    sha256:    file.sha256,                           // client verifies integrity
    lod:       file.lodLevels,                        // level-of-detail variants for phones vs headsets
    bounds:    asset.boundingBox,                     // client sizes placement UI
    triangles: file.triangleCount,
  });
});

// Spatial anchors — persistent world-locked positions (GPS + visual features)
// POST /ar/anchors
app.post("/ar/anchors", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const AnchorSchema = z.object({
    name: z.string(),
    geolocation: z.object({ lat: z.number(), lng: z.number(), altitude: z.number().optional() }),
    visualFeatures: z.string(),              // platform-specific ARWorldMap / ARCore cloud anchor blob
    assetId: z.string().uuid(),
    visibility: z.enum(["private", "shared", "public"]),
  });
  const data = AnchorSchema.parse(req.body);

  const anchor = await db.arAnchor.create({
    data: { ...data, userId: req.user.id, createdAt: new Date() },
  });
  res.status(201).json(anchor);
});

// GET /ar/anchors/nearby?lat=&lng=&radius=
app.get("/ar/anchors/nearby", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { lat, lng, radius = 100 } = req.query;
  // PostGIS or geohash-based spatial query
  const anchors = await db.$queryRaw`
    SELECT id, name, geolocation, asset_id, visibility
    FROM ar_anchor
    WHERE ST_DWithin(
      geolocation::geography,
      ST_MakePoint(${lng}::float, ${lat}::float)::geography,
      ${radius}::int
    )
    AND (visibility = 'public' OR user_id = ${req.user.id})
    LIMIT 50
  `;
  res.json({ anchors });
});

// Multi-user shared session — WebRTC DataChannel or WebSocket for sub-100ms sync
// WS /ar/sessions/:id — presence, transform updates, voice/hand tracking
// Use a managed platform (Agora, Vonage, LiveKit, PlayCanvas) rather than rolling your own.

Trade-offs: Asset formats are platform-fragmented — USDZ for Apple, glTF for Android/Web. Ship both; pick by X-Client-Platform header. CDN is non-negotiable — AR assets are 5-50 MB and users abandon if loading takes >3s. Serve via Cloudflare R2, CloudFront, or Bunny.net with edge caching. Compressed textures (KTX2, BasisU) cut file size 50-80% and are GPU-native. Spatial anchors: ARKit ARWorldMap, ARCore Cloud Anchors, and Niantic Lightship provide geo-accurate persistent anchors — use them; don't rebuild. Multi-user real-time: transform updates at 60 Hz need WebRTC DataChannel or aggressive WebSocket (binary, flatbuffers) — HTTP polling is unusable. Privacy concerns escalate with AR: cameras scan private spaces; server should never receive raw frames unless absolutely needed; visual features (not images) get uploaded for anchors.

Question 93

How would you implement a feature that allows users to run custom scripts and automation?

Situation: power users (automation platforms like Zapier/Make, enterprise admins, data analysts) want to write custom logic that runs on API events — "when a deal closes, post to Slack and update the CRM" — without waiting for built-in features.

Approach: sandbox user code in an isolated runtime (V8 isolates, WebAssembly, Firecracker microVMs). Trigger via webhooks / event subscriptions. Enforce CPU, memory, and time limits. Audit every execution.

// POST /scripts — user registers a script
const ScriptSchema = z.object({
  name: z.string().min(1),
  trigger: z.object({
    type:  z.enum(["webhook", "event", "schedule"]),
    event: z.string().optional(),            // e.g. "deal.closed"
    cron:  z.string().optional(),
  }),
  language: z.enum(["javascript"]),          // start with one language
  source: z.string().max(100_000),           // 100 KB limit
});

app.post("/scripts", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const data = ScriptSchema.parse(req.body);

  // Parse + lint before saving — reject obviously malicious patterns
  const ast = parseJs(data.source);
  assertNoDynamicEval(ast);                  // no eval, no Function constructor
  assertAllowedGlobals(ast, ["fetch", "console", "crypto"]);

  const script = await db.script.create({
    data: { ...data, userId: req.user.id, version: 1, enabled: true },
  });
  res.status(201).json(script);
});

// Execution — use isolated-vm (V8 isolates) for strong sandboxing
import ivm from "isolated-vm";
const EXEC_TIMEOUT_MS = 5000;
const MEMORY_LIMIT_MB = 128;

async function runScript(scriptId, triggerData) {
  const script = await db.script.findUnique({ where: { id: scriptId } });
  if (!script?.enabled) return;

  const isolate = new ivm.Isolate({ memoryLimit: MEMORY_LIMIT_MB });
  const context = await isolate.createContext();
  const jail    = context.global;

  // Expose a sandboxed fetch — rate-limited, URL-allowlisted
  await jail.set("fetch", new ivm.Reference(async (url, opts) => {
    if (!isUrlAllowed(url, script.userId)) throw new Error("URL not allowed");
    const resp = await fetch(url, { ...opts, signal: AbortSignal.timeout(3000) });
    return { status: resp.status, body: await resp.text() };
  }));

  await jail.set("input", new ivm.ExternalCopy(triggerData).copyInto());

  const started = Date.now();
  try {
    const compiled = await isolate.compileScript(script.source);
    const result   = await compiled.run(context, { timeout: EXEC_TIMEOUT_MS });
    await db.scriptRun.create({
      data: {
        scriptId, status: "success", durMs: Date.now() - started,
        memoryKb: isolate.getHeapStatisticsSync().used_heap_size / 1024,
      },
    });
    return result;
  } catch (err) {
    await db.scriptRun.create({
      data: { scriptId, status: "error", durMs: Date.now() - started, error: err.message },
    });
    if (consecutiveFailures(scriptId) > 10) {
      await db.script.update({ where: { id: scriptId }, data: { enabled: false } });
      await notifyUser(script.userId, "script_disabled_repeated_failures");
    }
  } finally {
    isolate.dispose();
  }
}

Trade-offs: Isolation choice is the most important decision. V8 isolates (isolated-vm, Cloudflare Workers, Deno Deploy) give microsecond startup and strong sandboxing — best for high-volume short scripts. WebAssembly (Wasmtime, Wasmer, Wasm Workers, Fermyon Spin) supports many languages; capability-based security. Firecracker microVMs (AWS Lambda's kernel) give full Linux; higher overhead; best when scripts need full language runtime. Docker containers are the weakest isolation — use only if you control all inputs. Don't use vm or vm2 Node modules — both have unfixed escape vulnerabilities. Resource limits must be enforced at the isolate level (not app level), covering CPU, memory, wall-clock, file descriptors, and outbound network. Managed platforms: Cloudflare Workers (for user code), Modal, Val.Town, and Deno Deploy offer user-script-execution as a service — for most teams, build on these rather than running isolation infra. Observability: per-script metrics, execution logs users can see, alerts on high failure rate. Outbound rate limits per script prevent a malicious script from DDoS-ing a third party through your infra.

Question 94

Describe how you would handle data integrity and consistency in a distributed API architecture.

Situation: the API spans multiple services, databases, regions. An order involves payment, inventory, shipping, and notification services — all with their own stores. Traditional ACID transactions don't span them. Yet users and auditors demand "when an order exists, payment, inventory, and fulfillment are in agreement."

Approach: adopt eventual consistency as the default; use outbox pattern for reliable event publication; compensate with sagas for multi-service workflows; reconcile with periodic audit jobs.

// Outbox pattern — transactional event publishing
// When a domain write happens, write an event row in the SAME transaction.
// A separate relay publishes unpublished events; retries on failure.

await db.$transaction(async (tx) => {
  const order = await tx.order.create({ data: { userId, total, status: "pending" } });

  // same transaction as the business write — atomic with the domain change
  await tx.outboxEvent.create({
    data: {
      id:          crypto.randomUUID(),
      aggregateId: order.id,
      eventType:   "order.created",
      payload:     JSON.stringify({ orderId: order.id, userId, total }),
      createdAt:   new Date(),
    },
  });
  return order;
});

// Relay worker — reads unpublished events and publishes to Kafka / SNS / RabbitMQ
async function publishOutboxEvents() {
  const batch = await db.outboxEvent.findMany({
    where: { publishedAt: null }, orderBy: { createdAt: "asc" }, take: 100,
  });

  for (const evt of batch) {
    try {
      await kafka.send({ topic: evt.eventType, messages: [{ key: evt.aggregateId, value: evt.payload }] });
      await db.outboxEvent.update({ where: { id: evt.id }, data: { publishedAt: new Date() } });
    } catch (err) {
      log("warn", "outbox_publish_failed", { id: evt.id, error: err.message });
      // stays unpublished; will retry next tick
    }
  }
}

// Saga — orchestrate multi-service workflow with compensations
async function placeOrder({ userId, cartItems }) {
  const saga = new Saga("place-order");

  saga.step("reserve-inventory", async () => {
    return inventoryService.reserve({ items: cartItems });
  }, async (reservationId) => {
    await inventoryService.release({ reservationId });              // compensation
  });

  saga.step("charge-payment", async (ctx) => {
    return paymentService.charge({ userId, amount: ctx.total, idempotencyKey: saga.id });
  }, async (chargeId) => {
    await paymentService.refund({ chargeId });
  });

  saga.step("create-shipment", async (ctx) => {
    return shippingService.create({ address: ctx.address, items: cartItems });
  }, async (shipmentId) => {
    await shippingService.cancel({ shipmentId });
  });

  try {
    return await saga.run();
  } catch (err) {
    await saga.compensate();                                        // rollback all completed steps
    throw err;
  }
}

// Reconciliation — nightly audit job
async function auditOrderConsistency() {
  const orders = await db.order.findMany({ where: { status: "paid", createdAt: { gte: yesterday() } } });
  for (const order of orders) {
    const [charge, shipment] = await Promise.all([
      paymentService.getCharge(order.paymentId),
      shippingService.getShipment(order.shipmentId),
    ]);
    if (charge?.status !== "captured" || shipment?.status === "not_found") {
      await alertOps("order_inconsistency", { orderId: order.id, charge, shipment });
    }
  }
}

Trade-offs: Two-phase commit (2PC) across services is available (XA transactions) but rarely worth it — it couples availability, blocks resources, and performs badly. Outbox + eventual consistency is the 2026 default: atomic domain write, relayed later. Debezium tails database change logs (CDC) as an alternative to polling the outbox table — near-zero latency, more ops. Sagas handle unhappy paths (compensating actions). Workflow engines (Temporal, Inngest, Trigger.dev, AWS Step Functions) bake in durability, retries, and observability — rolling your own saga is a common mistake. Idempotency keys on every cross-service call guard against retry-induced duplicates. Audit / reconciliation jobs are the safety net; when eventual becomes "never consistent," reconciliation surfaces it before customers do. Strong consistency where it matters: the payment authorization step, the inventory decrement step — keep those in single-service ACID transactions. Spread async only where business can tolerate a 2-5 second delay.

Question 95

Your API needs to support natural language processing (NLP). How would you implement this?

Situation: users want to search by meaning ("find mentions of customer frustration"), summarize documents, extract structured data from free text, detect intent in support tickets, or translate content. Classical keyword matching can't do this; modern LLMs can.

Approach: combine embedding-based retrieval for semantic search with LLM completion for generation / extraction / classification. Keep prompts structured; validate outputs; cache aggressively.

// POST /search/semantic — vector search across user's content
app.post("/search/semantic", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { query, topK = 10, filters = {} } = req.body;

  // 1. Embed the query (cached — identical queries = identical vectors)
  const queryEmbedding = await embedWithCache(query);

  // 2. Vector search — cosine similarity with metadata filter
  const results = await vectorDb.search({
    vector: queryEmbedding,
    topK,
    filter: { userId: req.user.id, ...filters },     // tenant isolation critical
  });

  // 3. Optionally re-rank with a cross-encoder for higher precision
  const reranked = results.length > 5
    ? await rerank(query, results)
    : results;

  res.json({ results: reranked });
});

// Embedding with cache — deterministic, perfect cache hit rate on repeated queries
async function embedWithCache(text) {
  const key    = `emb:${hash(text)}`;
  const cached = await redis.get(key);
  if (cached) return JSON.parse(cached);

  const { data } = await openai.embeddings.create({
    model: "text-embedding-3-small",
    input: text,
    dimensions: 1536,
  });
  const emb = data[0].embedding;
  await redis.set(key, JSON.stringify(emb), "EX", 30 * 24 * 3600);
  return emb;
}

// POST /nlp/extract — structured extraction with JSON-mode LLM
const InvoiceSchema = z.object({
  vendor: z.string(),
  total: z.number(),
  currency: z.string().length(3),
  dueDate: z.string().optional(),
  lineItems: z.array(z.object({
    description: z.string(), quantity: z.number(), unitPrice: z.number(),
  })),
});

app.post("/nlp/extract-invoice", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { text } = req.body;

  const completion = await anthropic.messages.create({
    model: "claude-opus-4-7",
    max_tokens: 2000,
    system: "Extract invoice data. Return ONLY valid JSON matching the schema. No prose.",
    messages: [{ role: "user", content: `Schema:\n${JSON.stringify(InvoiceSchema.shape)}\n\nDocument:\n${text}` }],
  });

  // Validate — LLMs occasionally return invalid JSON or wrong types
  const raw = completion.content[0].text;
  try {
    const parsed = InvoiceSchema.parse(JSON.parse(raw));
    res.json(parsed);
  } catch (err) {
    log("warn", "llm_extract_validation_failed", { error: err.message, raw });
    res.status(422).json({ error: "extraction_failed", detail: err.message });
  }
});

Trade-offs: Model choice is vast — managed (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, Cohere, Mistral, AWS Bedrock) vs open (Llama, Qwen, DeepSeek via vLLM, Together, Fireworks, Groq). Start managed; switch when volume economics or privacy require. Vector databases: pgvector (PostgreSQL extension), Pinecone, Weaviate, Qdrant, Milvus, Chroma — pgvector is fine up to ~10M vectors with IVFFlat or HNSW; specialized DBs win above that. Hybrid search (BM25 + vector) consistently outperforms either alone; retrieve N candidates with BM25, rerank with vectors + cross-encoder. Prompt engineering: use structured output modes (JSON mode, tool calling, Anthropic's constrained generation) rather than parsing free text. Cost controls: cache embeddings forever (they're deterministic), cache completions when possible, truncate context windows to essentials, use smaller models for classification. Evaluation: build a labeled test set early; tools like Langfuse, Braintrust, Ragas track quality over prompt/model changes. Privacy: redact PII before sending to third parties; use Azure OpenAI / Bedrock for data residency requirements; on-prem open models when data can't leave.

Question 96

How would you design an API to support interactive and real-time dashboards?

Situation: dashboards (executive KPI view, support queue monitor, trading screen) display dozens of metrics that update live. Users expect sub-second updates without refreshing, across many concurrent viewers of the same data.

Approach: three-layer architecture. Event producers write to a stream; aggregation layer rolls up into metric windows; API push layer delivers updates via WebSocket or SSE. Don't query the OLTP database on every tick.

// Producers — services emit events to Redis Streams / Kafka
// Every API call, every DB write, every external event feeds the stream
await redis.xadd("events:orders", "*",
  "type", "order.placed", "orgId", order.orgId, "total", String(order.total),
);

// Aggregation worker — windowed counters in Redis
// Per org, per metric, per time bucket (minute / hour)
async function processEvent(ev) {
  const minute = Math.floor(Date.now() / 60_000);
  const pipeline = redis.multi();

  pipeline.hincrby(`dash:${ev.orgId}:${minute}`, "orders.count", 1);
  pipeline.hincrbyfloat(`dash:${ev.orgId}:${minute}`, "orders.revenue", ev.total);
  pipeline.expire(`dash:${ev.orgId}:${minute}`, 86_400);        // 24h TTL

  await pipeline.exec();

  // fan-out to connected subscribers for this org
  await redis.publish(`dash-updates:${ev.orgId}`, JSON.stringify({
    minute, orderId: ev.orderId, total: ev.total,
  }));
}

// WS /dashboard/live — client connects, subscribes to their org's channel
const wss = new WebSocketServer({ noServer: true });

wss.on("connection", async (ws, req) => {
  const { orgId, userId } = await authenticateWs(req);
  if (!orgId) return ws.close(4401, "unauthorized");

  // 1. Initial snapshot — last 60 minutes of aggregates
  const snapshot = await getSnapshot(orgId, 60);
  ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: "snapshot", data: snapshot }));

  // 2. Subscribe to this org's Redis pub/sub channel for new updates
  const subscriber = redis.duplicate();
  await subscriber.subscribe(`dash-updates:${orgId}`, (msg) => {
    ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: "delta", data: JSON.parse(msg) }));
  });

  // 3. Heartbeat to detect dead connections
  const hb = setInterval(() => ws.ping(), 30_000);

  ws.on("close", () => { subscriber.quit(); clearInterval(hb); });
});

// REST fallback — point-in-time queries for non-live widgets
app.get("/dashboard/metrics", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const { from, to, metrics } = req.query;
  // Serve from Redis for recent; Clickhouse / materialized views for historical
  const data = from > hourAgo()
    ? await queryRedis(req.user.orgId, from, to, metrics)
    : await queryWarehouse(req.user.orgId, from, to, metrics);
  res.set("Cache-Control", "max-age=30, stale-while-revalidate=60").json(data);
});

Trade-offs: Transport choice: SSE is simpler (one-way, HTTP-native, proxy-friendly, auto-reconnect); WebSocket is bidirectional and better for interactive dashboards (filter, drill-down, configure). Aggregation engines: Redis for sub-minute real-time, ClickHouse / TimescaleDB / DuckDB / Druid / Pinot for historical; Materialize and Tinybird offer streaming SQL with real-time materialized views — arguably the cleanest 2026 architecture for this use case. Don't query OLTP for dashboards — it kills production. Replicate to a warehouse or dedicated store. Downsampling: keep 1-minute resolution for 24h, 1-hour for 30 days, 1-day for years. Connection budget: one WebSocket instance handles ~10-50k connections; scale horizontally with Redis pub/sub fan-out; use sticky routing or managed platforms (Ably, Pusher, Supabase Realtime, PartyKit). Backpressure: slow clients can't drink from a firehose — the server should throttle or drop stale messages rather than buffer infinitely.

Question 97

Describe how you would implement a custom access control system for your API.

Situation: the application has complex authorization needs that RBAC can't express: "users can view their team's documents, but only editors can modify; admins see everything in their org; certain documents are restricted to named individuals." Policy lives in business logic scattered across 50 endpoints.

Approach: centralize authorization in a policy service. Every request routes through the same can(user, action, resource) check. Policies live as code (reviewed, versioned) rather than scattered if statements.

// Policy primitives — model as relationships (ReBAC) or attributes (ABAC)
// Here we use a ReBAC model inspired by Google Zanzibar / OpenFGA.

// Relationships in a dedicated table
// (user_id, relation, resource_id)
// e.g. (alice, "editor", doc:42), (team:eng, "member", alice), (doc:42, "parent", folder:research)

// Authorization check — recursive relationship resolution
async function can(userId, action, resourceType, resourceId) {
  // Action → required relations (policy definition)
  const RULES = {
    "document:read":   ["viewer", "editor", "owner", "admin"],
    "document:write":  ["editor", "owner", "admin"],
    "document:delete": ["owner", "admin"],
    "document:share":  ["owner", "admin"],
  };

  const required = RULES[`${resourceType}:${action}`];
  if (!required) return false;

  // Check direct relationship
  const direct = await db.relationship.findFirst({
    where: { userId, resourceId, relation: { in: required } },
  });
  if (direct) return true;

  // Check transitive (team → user, parent folder → user)
  const teams = await db.relationship.findMany({
    where: { userId, relation: "member", resourceType: "team" },
  });
  for (const t of teams) {
    const teamAccess = await db.relationship.findFirst({
      where: { userId: t.resourceId, resourceId, relation: { in: required } },
    });
    if (teamAccess) return true;
  }

  // Check inherited (document inherits from folder)
  const resource = await db.resource.findUnique({ where: { id: resourceId } });
  if (resource?.parentId) return can(userId, action, resource.parentType, resource.parentId);

  return false;
}

// Middleware — applied per route; policy identifies action+resource from route
function authz(action, getResourceId) {
  return async (req, res, next) => {
    const resourceId = typeof getResourceId === "function" ? getResourceId(req) : req.params[getResourceId];
    const allowed    = await can(req.user.id, action, "document", resourceId);
    if (!allowed) return res.sendStatus(403);
    await audit.log({ actor: req.user.id, action, resource: resourceId, allowed });
    next();
  };
}

// Routes wire the policy declaratively
app.get   ("/documents/:id",       requireAuth, authz("read",   "id"), getDoc);
app.patch ("/documents/:id",       requireAuth, authz("write",  "id"), updateDoc);
app.delete("/documents/:id",       requireAuth, authz("delete", "id"), deleteDoc);
app.post  ("/documents/:id/share", requireAuth, authz("share",  "id"), shareDoc);

Trade-offs: Model selection is consequential. RBAC (role → permissions) is simple but rigid — fine for internal tools with fixed hierarchies. ABAC (attributes of user + resource + environment evaluated by policy) is flexible but complex to debug. ReBAC (relationships, Zanzibar-style) handles "team members can access team resources" natively — excellent for collaboration apps. PBAC with OPA / Cedar centralizes policy in a declarative language. Managed services: OpenFGA, Auth0 FGA, SpiceDB, Warrant implement Zanzibar; Oso Cloud offers policy-as-code; Permit.io and Cerbos are other options. Building from scratch is tempting but delivers bugs for years — the Zanzibar paper explicitly covers the subtleties (namespaces, tuples, consistency tokens) that take time to get right. Defense in depth: gateway checks auth + coarse scopes; service checks business authz; database enforces row-level security (PostgreSQL RLS) as a final safety net. Audit every decision — both granted and denied — for compliance and debugging. Performance: cache check results per-request with tight TTL; bulk-check endpoints ("which of these 100 documents can I read?") amortize overhead.

Question 98

Your API needs to support data export in multiple formats (CSV, JSON, XML). How would you design this?

Situation: users need to export their data for backups, analysis, or migration — in CSV for Excel users, JSON for developers, XML for enterprise integrations. Exports can be large (millions of rows), so requests must be async; outputs must stream to avoid memory blow-ups.

Approach: single POST /exports endpoint with format as a parameter; job queue for async processing; streaming writers for each format; signed URL to a temporary S3 object. Share format logic where possible.

// POST /exports — accepts format, filters, entity; returns job ID
const ExportSchema = z.object({
  entity: z.enum(["orders", "customers", "products"]),
  format: z.enum(["csv", "json", "xml", "jsonl", "xlsx"]),
  filters: z.object({
    from: z.string().datetime().optional(),
    to:   z.string().datetime().optional(),
  }).optional(),
  fields: z.array(z.string()).optional(),           // column selection
});

app.post("/exports", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const data = ExportSchema.parse(req.body);
  const job = await db.exportJob.create({
    data: { ...data, userId: req.user.id, status: "queued" },
  });
  await queue.add("export", { jobId: job.id });
  res.status(202).location(`/exports/${job.id}`).json({ jobId: job.id });
});

// Worker — stream from DB, write to S3 without buffering in memory
import { Upload } from "@aws-sdk/lib-storage";
import archiver from "archiver";
import { stringify as csvStringify } from "csv-stringify";

async function runExport({ jobId }) {
  const job = await db.exportJob.findUnique({ where: { id: jobId } });
  await db.exportJob.update({ where: { id: jobId }, data: { status: "processing" } });

  const s3Key = `exports/${jobId}/${job.entity}.${extensionFor(job.format)}.gz`;
  const gzip  = zlib.createGzip();
  const upload = new Upload({
    client: s3,
    params: { Bucket: process.env.EXPORT_BUCKET, Key: s3Key, Body: gzip, ContentType: contentTypeFor(job.format) },
  });

  // DB cursor — stream rows without loading all into memory
  const cursor = db[job.entity].cursor({
    where: buildFilter(job.filters, job.userId),
    select: job.fields ? pick(ALLOWED_FIELDS[job.entity], job.fields) : ALLOWED_FIELDS[job.entity],
  });

  const writer = createWriter(job.format, gzip);    // format-specific writer
  for await (const row of cursor) {
    writer.write(row);
  }
  writer.end();

  await upload.done();
  await db.exportJob.update({
    where: { id: jobId },
    data: { status: "completed", s3Key, completedAt: new Date() },
  });
  await notifyUserComplete(job.userId, jobId);
}

// Format-specific writers sharing the streaming interface
function createWriter(format, out) {
  if (format === "csv") {
    const stream = csvStringify({ header: true, quoted_string: true });
    stream.pipe(out);
    return stream;
  }
  if (format === "jsonl") {
    return { write: (r) => out.write(JSON.stringify(r) + "\n"), end: () => out.end() };
  }
  if (format === "json") {
    let first = true;
    out.write("[");
    return {
      write: (r) => { if (!first) out.write(","); out.write(JSON.stringify(r)); first = false; },
      end:   () => out.end("]"),
    };
  }
  if (format === "xml") {
    out.write('<?xml version="1.0"?><export>');
    return {
      write: (r) => out.write(toXmlElement("record", r)),
      end:   () => out.end("</export>"),
    };
  }
  throw new Error(`unsupported format ${format}`);
}

// GET /exports/:id — poll status; completed returns signed download URL
app.get("/exports/:id", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const job = await db.exportJob.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id, userId: req.user.id } });
  if (!job) return res.sendStatus(404);

  if (job.status !== "completed") return res.json({ status: job.status });

  const downloadUrl = await getSignedUrl(s3, new GetObjectCommand({
    Bucket: process.env.EXPORT_BUCKET, Key: job.s3Key,
  }), { expiresIn: 3600 });
  res.json({ status: "completed", downloadUrl, expiresIn: 3600 });
});

Trade-offs: Streaming is non-negotiable beyond 10k rows — loading into memory crashes the worker and blocks on large tenants. Database cursors + stream writers keep memory constant. Compression (gzip, zstd) halves bandwidth and storage for text formats. Format-specific libraries: csv-stringify for CSV with proper escaping; SheetJS / exceljs for XLSX (binary format; can't stream as easily); xmlbuilder2 for XML; native JSON.stringify for JSON Lines. Avoid "export" endpoints that run synchronously — 60-second proxy timeouts and memory limits bite. Signed URLs with 1-hour expiry balance convenience and security; longer windows leak data longer; shorter windows frustrate slow downloaders. Entity + format matrix is combinatorial; write the format layer once and compose with the entity layer via a shared Writer interface. Rate limit export requests — one export can touch millions of DB rows; unbounded concurrency from one user takes down everyone. Audit every export with user, timestamp, entity, row count — exports are the #1 DLP risk.

Question 99

How would you handle API scaling to accommodate a sudden spike in traffic?

Situation: a marketing campaign, a viral tweet, a Black Friday sale, or an outage cascade triggers a 10-100x traffic spike within minutes. The system must absorb the surge without cascading failure and degrade gracefully if capacity is exceeded.

Approach: multi-layer defense. Edge absorbs the bulk via caching and rate limits; autoscaling adds capacity; load shedding protects core users; queue-based leveling converts spikes into sustained work; fallbacks serve degraded responses rather than 5xx.

// 1. CDN / edge cache — the single biggest lever for GET traffic
//    Cloudflare, Fastly, CloudFront cache at 300+ PoPs.
//    With Cache-Control + s-maxage, a popular endpoint hits origin once per CDN node per TTL.

app.get("/products/:id", async (req, res) => {
  const product = await db.product.findUnique({ where: { id: req.params.id } });
  res
    .set("Cache-Control", "public, max-age=60, s-maxage=300, stale-while-revalidate=600")
    .set("Surrogate-Key", `product:${req.params.id}`)                // for tag-based purge
    .json(product);
});

// 2. Autoscaling — Kubernetes HPA or serverless
// HPA example — scale when CPU > 60% or custom metric (RPS per pod) exceeds threshold
// yaml:
//   apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
//   kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
//   spec:
//     minReplicas: 4
//     maxReplicas: 100
//     metrics:
//       - type: Resource
//         resource: { name: cpu, target: { type: Utilization, averageUtilization: 60 } }

// 3. Load shedding — reject excess before overwhelming the database
// Use a token bucket per endpoint; 429 excess traffic at the edge

// 4. Queue-based leveling — convert write spikes into steady-state work
// Instead of hitting the DB 10k/s, accept into a queue, process at sustainable rate
app.post("/signups", async (req, res) => {
  // minimal validation here; heavy work in the worker
  const signupData = SignupSchema.parse(req.body);
  await queue.add("signup", signupData);                             // queue depth acts as buffer
  res.status(202).json({ status: "accepted" });
});

// 5. Circuit breaker — prevent cascade failure when downstream is slow
import CircuitBreaker from "opossum";
const recommender = new CircuitBreaker(fetchRecommendations, {
  timeout: 500, errorThresholdPercentage: 50, resetTimeout: 10_000,
});
recommender.fallback(() => []);                                       // return empty recs when upstream is failing

app.get("/home", requireAuth, async (req, res) => {
  const [feed, recs] = await Promise.all([
    getFeed(req.user.id),
    recommender.fire(req.user.id),                                    // can't drag down the whole endpoint
  ]);
  res.json({ feed, recs });
});

// 6. Request prioritization — shed low-priority first
app.use((req, res, next) => {
  if (overloaded() && !req.user?.premium && req.path.startsWith("/recommendations")) {
    return res.status(503).set("Retry-After", "30").json({ error: "capacity_shed" });
  }
  next();
});

Trade-offs: CDN caching is the biggest lever: 95% cache hit rate means origin sees 5% of requests. Design for cacheability — stable URLs, Vary minimal, Cache-Control on every response. Stale-while-revalidate serves stale content while a background fetch refreshes — users see fast responses; origin is never starved. Autoscaling has lag — new pods take 30-60s to be ready. For traffic that spikes faster than that, you need over-provisioning (run at 30% utilization so you have headroom), predictive scaling (scale up before the sale starts), or serverless (Lambda, Cloudflare Workers scale in milliseconds). Database is usually the bottleneck before compute; read replicas, connection pooling (PgBouncer, RDS Proxy), and query-level caching are the next levers. Queue-based leveling turns write spikes into queue depth — the API always responds 202 quickly; workers catch up. Monitor queue depth as a SLI. Load shedding hurts but is better than total failure — prefer it over crashing. Preserve premium / paid / critical-path traffic. Chaos testing (Gremlin, Chaos Mesh, Litmus) validates the plan: simulate spike, kill a region, saturate DB — your runbook must hold. Post-incident: review what worked, what didn't, feed back into capacity planning.

Question 100

Describe how you would implement a custom middleware to handle API request validation and transformation.

Situation: the API has many endpoints that share cross-cutting concerns — validation, sanitization, authorization, transformation of request/response formats, localization, logging. Scattering this logic per-handler creates drift; centralizing it in middleware keeps handlers focused on business logic.

Approach: write composable middleware functions in the framework's conventions (Express (req, res, next), Fastify hooks, Koa async stack). Each middleware has one responsibility; chain them per-route.

// validate — generic Zod-backed middleware; one function for all endpoints
export function validate(schemas) {
  return (req, res, next) => {
    try {
      if (schemas.body)   req.body   = schemas.body.parse(req.body);
      if (schemas.query)  req.query  = schemas.query.parse(req.query);
      if (schemas.params) req.params = schemas.params.parse(req.params);
      next();
    } catch (err) {
      // RFC 7807 problem+json
      res.status(422).type("application/problem+json").json({
        type: "https://api.example.com/problems/validation",
        title: "Validation failed",
        status: 422,
        errors: err.errors.map((e) => ({ path: e.path.join("."), code: e.code, message: e.message })),
      });
    }
  };
}

// transform — normalize request bodies (trim strings, coerce dates, strip unknown fields)
export function normalize(req, res, next) {
  req.body = normalizeValues(req.body);
  next();
}

function normalizeValues(obj) {
  if (typeof obj === "string") return obj.trim();
  if (Array.isArray(obj)) return obj.map(normalizeValues);
  if (obj && typeof obj === "object") {
    return Object.fromEntries(
      Object.entries(obj).map(([k, v]) => [k, normalizeValues(v)]),
    );
  }
  return obj;
}

// sanitize — strip HTML from user-supplied strings to prevent stored XSS
import DOMPurify from "isomorphic-dompurify";
export function sanitizeHtml(fields) {
  return (req, res, next) => {
    for (const f of fields) {
      if (typeof req.body?.[f] === "string") {
        req.body[f] = DOMPurify.sanitize(req.body[f]);
      }
    }
    next();
  };
}

// requestId — propagate / generate
export function requestId(req, res, next) {
  const id = req.get("X-Request-Id") ?? crypto.randomUUID();
  req.id = id;
  res.set("X-Request-Id", id);
  next();
}

// responseShape — wrap all successful responses in a consistent envelope
export function envelope(req, res, next) {
  const original = res.json.bind(res);
  res.json = (data) => original({
    data,
    meta: {
      requestId: req.id,
      apiVersion: req.apiVersion,
      timestamp: new Date().toISOString(),
    },
  });
  next();
}

// Compose per-route — each middleware has a clear responsibility
const UserCreateSchema = {
  body: z.object({
    email: z.string().email(),
    name:  z.string().min(1).max(100),
    bio:   z.string().max(1000).optional(),
  }),
};

app.post(
  "/users",
  requestId,
  authenticate,
  authorize("user:create"),
  normalize,
  validate(UserCreateSchema),
  sanitizeHtml(["bio"]),
  envelope,
  async (req, res) => {
    // handler stays tiny — all cross-cutting concerns handled by middleware
    const user = await db.user.create({ data: req.body });
    res.status(201).json(user);
  },
);

Trade-offs: Composability > inheritance — middleware is functional; apply where needed, not in a rigid base class. Order matters critically: requestId before everything (so logs include it); authenticate before authorize; normalize before validate (so trimmed inputs pass); envelope late (wraps the final response). Get this wrong and you have subtle bugs — unauthenticated requests hitting validation, or logs missing request IDs. Avoid fat middleware that does unrelated things; prefer many small, testable units. Unit test each in isolation with mocked req, res, next — CI catches ordering regressions. Async middleware: always try/catch and call next(err); unhandled promise rejections in Express kill the response. Framework-specific patterns matter: Fastify's typed hooks and plugin system are more performant and type-safe than Express middleware; NestJS interceptors and pipes are classier for large apps; tRPC uses procedure middleware with full type inference. Library ecosystem: helmet (security headers), cors, express-rate-limit, pino-http, compression, cookie-parser — prefer battle-tested middleware over rolling your own for security concerns. OpenTelemetry auto-instrumentation injects tracing middleware automatically across frameworks; one line of setup, no per-route work.
